By contrast, the Houthi-controlled economy proved relatively more stable (albeit under an authoritarian command that suppressed economic freedoms). This set the stage for profound political changes in 2022 (notably, Hadi’s removal) as well as shifts in how each side approached the economic front of the conflict.

2022: Leadership Transition and Revenue Warfare

In April 2022, Yemen’s political landscape shifted when President Abdrabbuh Mansur Hadi – under Saudi auspices – transferred power to an 8-member Presidential Leadership Council (PLC) that included diverse faction leaders (among them an STC representative and others). This development had significant economic implications. The Saudis and Emiratis simultaneously announced a US$3 billion financial support package for Yemen’s economy . Of that, $2 billion was to come as a deposit split between Saudi Arabia and the UAE into the Aden central bank, and $1 billion for development projects and fuel support . This infusion, aimed at stabilizing the currency and government finances, buoyed optimism in government-held areas. Indeed, the Yemeni rial (Aden) strengthened sharply in the first half of 2022, rising from around YR 800/US$ in January to about YR 550–600/US$ by April 2022 in anticipation of the Gulf aid and the new unity government. For a brief period, the exchange rate gap between north and south nearly closed – a remarkable reversal of the extreme divergence a year prior.

However, the promise of the Gulf aid was not fully and immediately realized. Saudi Arabia and the UAE were slow to actually disburse the funds, reportedly conditioning them on economic reforms and absorption capacity. By late 2022, only a fraction of the pledged support had materialized in CBY-Aden accounts (the bulk was delayed amid bureaucratic and political hesitations). Still, the early 2022 currency rally had some positive effects: prices of staple foods in government areas fell for the first time in years (for example, the cost of the minimum food basket in Aden dropped by roughly one-third between late 2021 and mid-2022 as the rial appreciated)  . There was palpable, if cautious, hope that perhaps the currency war might ease if the PLC could negotiate some economic truce with the Houthis.

Yet, the Houthi strategy turned increasingly towards economic coercion in 2022. After the UN-mediated nationwide truce began in April 2022 (lasting 6 months through October), frontline fighting largely paused, but economic warfare intensified. The Houthis made salary payment a key demand: they insisted that the IRG/PLC must pay the salaries of public employees in Houthi areas (using oil export revenue) as a condition to extend the truce. Negotiations on this issue stalled. In the meantime, the Houthis leveraged their strongest economic weapon: attacks on Yemen’s oil export infrastructure. In July, September, and especially October 2022, they launched drone and missile strikes (or threats of strikes) on oil terminals at Ash Shihr (Hadhramaut) and Rudum (Shabwah) when tankers were due to load  . These attacks succeeded in halting all oil exports by the IRG from October 2022 onward. As a result, Yemen’s government lost its primary source of hard currency earnings (around US$30–50 million per month) . The PLC was suddenly deprived of 75% of its budget revenue, deepening its fiscal crisis . By late 2022, the government could not pay many of its remaining civil servant salaries (already, it had been paying mostly only military and some civilian salaries, and often intermittently). Electricity outages worsened in Aden because the government couldn’t afford fuel for power plants when a Saudi fuel grant ran out . Aden and other cities saw renewed street protests in November–December 2022 as public workers went unpaid and prices crept back up.

The Houthis’ calculation was clear: strangling the government’s finances would erode the PLC’s ability to govern and possibly force it (and the Saudis) to concede to Houthi demands on revenue sharing. Indeed, in Houthi areas, there was a relative improvement – at least for the Houthi authorities themselves – because they no longer faced competition from southern oil exports. The Houthis had earlier imposed a blockade on domestically produced cooking gas from Marib (government territory), forcing people in Houthi areas to buy higher-priced gas imported via Hodeidah on which the Houthi authorities earned customs  . By mid-2022, the economic war centered on control of fuel and gas markets, customs revenues, and the banking system data.

One major standoff of 2022 was the issue of unifying the central bank or at least its data. The PLC government, under international pressure, required banks to relocate their headquarters from Sana’a to Aden and share data with the Aden CBY. In September 2022, CBY-Aden issued Resolution No. 69 ordering all banks to move their financial HQ to Aden by the end of the year or face sanctions  . In parallel, they launched a “unified exchange network” project (sometimes referred to as the “Unmoney” network) to centralize domestic transfers through CBY-Aden systems – effectively an attempt to undermine the Houthi-controlled clearing of YER transfers. The Houthis reacted fiercely: they banned banks that complied from operating in the north, closed branches of Tadhamon Bank and Al Kuraimi Microfinance Bank in Sana’a as punishment for cooperation with Aden, and threatened other banks’ staff  . By late 2022, this banking relocation drive had reached an impasse. A few small banks moved to Aden; most did not (given their assets and staff were in Sana’a). The Aden CBY did revoke the licenses of two banks that refused orders, but the practical effect was limited since those banks simply continued under Houthi protection.

Throughout 2022, inflation continued to hurt Yemenis. Even though the early-year currency appreciation had briefly lowered prices in the south, global commodity price spikes (partly due to the Ukraine war) hit Yemen hard. The price of wheat, much of which came from Russia/Ukraine, rose significantly. By end of 2022, food prices were roughly 60% higher than pre-war in the north and over 100% higher in the south (south more so due to the rial’s net depreciation over the year)  . A nationwide fuel crisis was averted when the UN truce allowed more fuel ships into Hodeidah port – ironically improving fuel availability in Houthi areas in 2022 compared to earlier. But after the truce expired in October, Houthi attacks on oil facilities meant fuel for government areas had to be imported at higher cost (no domestic extraction), causing diesel prices in Aden to jump by year’s end.

On a positive note, 2022 did see incremental progress in unifying certain financial mechanisms: for example, commercial flights from Sana’a resumed (to Amman, Jordan) during the truce, which eased travel for Yemenis and potentially remittance channels. Also, the central banks in Aden and Sana’a communicated indirectly via international mediators about technical issues (like keeping the exchange rates somewhat aligned for humanitarian operations). The World Bank and UN set up a floating exchange rate mechanism for aid: as of mid-2022, ~80% of international humanitarian funds were disbursed in the north through banks at a rate slightly below market, to avoid arbitrage  .

By the end of 2022, Yemen’s economic situation was at a crossroads: The IRG’s PLC government was cash-strapped and dependent on yet-to-be-fulfilled Gulf pledges, its central bank reforms partially implemented (e.g., the new foreign exchange auction system had improved transparency, and in fact by late 2022 the Aden CBY claimed to stabilize the rial around YR 1,100/US$ by selling $65 million via auctions over the year). The Houthi authorities were flush with retained revenues (they collected customs on all fuel and goods via Hodeidah during the truce without sending any to the government, and they no longer paid most salaries), enabling them to pay their security and military apparatus regularly – which solidified their control. However, the Houthis faced their own economic pressures: by not paying public servants, they generated public resentment; and their tight grip created a high cost of living and lack of investment in their areas (the economy there was stagnating, just without the volatility of the south).

In short, 2022 entrenched the north-south economic divide even as a political attempt was made to bridge it (through the PLC). The year ended with uncertainty: would there be a new truce (and perhaps a deal on salaries and revenues), or would the economic warfare worsen? The answer came in early 2023 with neither a formal truce nor a return to full war, but continued economic armistice by default, as described next.

2023: Persistent Stalemate and Attempts at Economic Reconnection

In 2023, Yemen did not see a major eruption of fighting; instead, the conflict increasingly played out in the economic domain amid a tenuous stalemate. There was no official UN truce after October 2022, but an undeclared cessation of major hostilities largely held throughout 2023. This provided space for cautious economic engagement and also for continued economic maneuvering by both sides.

A top agenda item was the payment of civil servant salaries in Houthi-controlled areas – a key Houthi demand. Throughout 2023, Omani mediators shuttling between Sana’a and Riyadh discussed a proposal for Saudi Arabia (and the Yemeni government) to fund salaries nationwide (especially for teachers, health workers, etc.) using oil and gas revenues  . The PLC government resisted handing revenues to the Houthis outright, fearing it would legitimize the Houthi administration and free up resources for their war efforts. Negotiations continued, and as a goodwill gesture, some salaries (e.g. retired civil servants) started being paid across lines with donor support. But a comprehensive deal was not reached by late 2023, partly because the Houthis insisted on including military and security personnel in salary payments (many of whom were their fighters, effectively asking the other side to pay their army) and on payment according to the pre-war payroll which they had swollen with thousands of new recruits in 2014  .

Economically, macroeconomic conditions in 2023 remained dire but somewhat stable on the surface. The Yemeni rial in Aden fluctuated between ~YR 1,100 and YR 1,400 per US$, averaging around 1,250 by late 2023. In Houthi areas, the rial remained pegged ~560–600 per US$ officially, though an emerging black market put it closer to 600–620. This meant the parallel exchange rate gap persisted around 2:1. Traders and smugglers continued to arbitrage: for instance, remittances kept shifting toward Houthi zones where one dollar fetched only ~600 (so senders prefer to send to Marib or Aden where they get ~1200, then money is carried north in goods or via circuitous routes). To counter this, in mid-2023, the PLC government tried a new tactic: it restricted certain imports (like cooking gas cylinders, which the Houthis profited from) and mandated that importers obtain hard currency through the Aden CBY’s auction system for key goods. Additionally, the Aden authorities announced plans for a new national customs dollar exchange rate, hoping to unify customs tariffs between north and south (previously the Houthis had a lower customs exchange rate to make imports via Hodeidah cheaper than via Aden). These measures were aimed at closing arbitrage loopholes and forcing more trade through government channels  .

Another noteworthy development was the expansion of the Aden CBY’s policy tools. In 2023 the CBY-Aden rolled out a system of electronic rials in a limited way for its own operations and tried to promote cashless payment networks in the south. The aim was to catch up with the north’s implementation and also to reduce reliance on physical cash (since too much cash was chasing too few dollars in Aden, fueling inflation). The Aden CBY also began publishing more detailed financial statements and strengthened bank supervision (with IMF technical assistance) – for example, it took action against a couple of exchange companies for non-compliance with anti-money laundering rules  . Confidence in southern banks remained very low, though; most Yemenis still would rather hold dollars under the mattress or Yemeni cash than deposits in a local bank.

The Houthis in 2023 doubled down on their control of the financial sector in their areas. They launched a new Houthi-controlled money transfer company (“One Yemen” or Quality Connect) to centralize domestic remittances, effectively bypassing the traditional exchange network that was historically centered in Sana’a but somewhat neutral  . By doing so, they attempted to cut out exchange businesses that might cooperate with Aden. They also intimidated exchange houses into adopting an in-house “exchange rate” for the USD pegged around 600 to maintain the illusion of stability, while actual market needs were handled quietly through parallel channels.

Despite the fragmentation, some economic relief arrived via external factors: Global fuel prices moderated in 2023, lowering Yemen’s import bill for petroleum products. The UN and donors increased aid in-kind (food distributions), offsetting the reduced purchasing power. Furthermore, Saudi Arabia began allowing more Yemeni labor migration again, which could boost remittances.

Late 2023 saw tentative optimism as Saudi-Houthi direct talks progressed, raising the prospect of a broader peace deal that would include economic arrangements. Part of these talks (as per leaks) involved Saudi financial guarantees to pay Yemeni salaries for a period and to help unify the central banks. Also, China’s diplomatic rapprochement with Iran early in 2023 (and Iran-Saudi normalization) had indirectly positive economic effects: Iran reportedly stopped or reduced sending free fuel to the Houthis (as they had from 2020–2021) once the dynamics shifted  , possibly nudging the Houthis to seek a negotiated solution to secure revenue.

By the end of 2023, Yemen’s economy remained deeply fractured and feeble: GDP was estimated at only about 50% of its 2014 level  , poverty persisted at roughly 75%  , and inflation, though slightly lower than the peaks, still eroded livelihoods (annual inflation ~25% in 2023). The “war economy” – smuggling, profiteering, aid diversion – was entrenched. But the reduction in violence and increased diplomacy offered a glimmer that in the near future, Yemen could pivot toward reconstruction if a political settlement is reached. The decisions taken in 2022–2023 (like the currency auctions, partial data transparency, and donor re-engagement) at least provided a framework that could be built upon in a post-conflict scenario.

2024–2025: Toward Fragile Stabilization

(Note: As of November 2025, Yemen’s conflict has not fully ended, but a de facto calm continues and economic conditions have stabilized somewhat compared to the worst years.)

In 2024, Yemen witnessed a cautiously improving economic trend under what might be termed “relative monetary stability on a fragile foundation”  . Several factors contributed: the absence of major new fighting allowed commerce to pick up modestly; Saudi Arabia, pursuing a détente with the Houthis, injected more funds to prevent collapse; and both Yemeni central banks tacitly avoided drastic moves that would upset the status quo. As a result, the southern rial, while still weak, settled into a steadier range (~YR 1,250–1,350 per US$)  , and by mid-2025 even saw a bout of appreciation after new Saudi deposits, strengthening by over 40% from its low point in 2023  . This led to a significant, if partial, reduction in prices in government-held areas: the WFP reported basic food basket costs in the south dropped ~34% month-on-month in mid-2025 following currency gains  . In the Houthi-controlled north, the continued use of old rials and de facto peg meant official prices hardly changed – inflation was muted in the official ledger, though a hidden inflation occurred via black-market trading of currency at higher rates and product shortages.

The Presidential Leadership Council (PLC), after weathering internal tensions in 2023, focused in 2024 on shoring up the economy in areas it controlled. It took steps such as forming a National Import Regulation Committee to streamline import financing and reduce rent-seeking  . The PLC also mandated the relocation of key state-owned banks (like the Yemen Bank for Reconstruction and Development) to Aden by issuing Resolution No. 17 of 2024, trying again to assert control over the banking sector  . Compliance remained an issue, but by 2025, more bank operations had at least partially shifted to Aden (particularly those of banks needing correspondent banking with the outside world, since only CBY-Aden was internationally recognized). To combat the ubiquitous hawala networks out of its reach, the Aden authorities in late 2024 launched a “Unified Network for Transfers” (sometimes dubbed “Shamil” network) connecting licensed exchange companies, aiming to undermine unlicensed hawala channels and bring transactions onto a monitored platform  . This was only partly successful; many Yemenis still used traditional channels, but gradually NGOs and businesses moved to the unified network for ease of currency conversion at the best rates available in Aden.

The Houthi authorities in 2024–2025 introduced new financial regulations of their own. They rolled out a stricter Islamic banking compliance regime – including drafting a law to ban interest-based lending entirely in areas under their control (even though interest-based transactions were already rare due to the war and Islamic practices)  . They also started a pilot of a domestic fuel subsidy program (providing some rationed fuel at below-market rates for public transport) funded by the high taxes they place on commercial fuel. However, they kept fuel prices for consumers in their areas artificially lower than in the south, which encouraged smuggling of fuel from north to south for profit. A significant share of the Houthi war economy now revolved around such arbitrage – buying goods (fuel, mobile cards, etc.) in the north at controlled prices and covertly selling them in the south for a markup  . This dynamic essentially meant as long as dual economies exist, war profiteers find opportunities.

On the international front (2024–2025), donors and financial institutions have shifted from pure relief to talk of recovery and reconstruction financing. The World Bank scaled up its Yemen portfolio via IDA grants, including projects to rehabilitate power, roads, and to strengthen the financial sector (e.g. a $20 million Financial Market Infrastructure project to modernize payment systems)  . The International Monetary Fund remained engaged in providing technical assistance and policy advice – the IMF’s 2023 Article IV report for Yemen (even though no comprehensive data from Sana’a was available) emphasized the need to unify the central banks and implement fiscal reforms to rein in deficit monetization. Gulf countries, notably Saudi Arabia, in 2023 and 2024 provided several tranches of support directly to pay salaries of certain Yemeni government sectors (for example, funding the Yemen Petroleum Company to supply fuel to power plants, effectively a subsidy for electricity generation in Aden).

A notable trend by late 2025 is the growth of some “bridge institutions” that work across the divide. For instance, the Social Fund for Development (SFD) – Yemen’s largest social protection institution – has managed to operate in both north and south throughout the conflict. It coordinated with local councils and paid workers for community projects using donor money, thereby injecting cash into local economies. Similarly, microfinance banks like Al-Amal and Al-Kuraimi, although headquartered in one side or the other, served customers in all governorates as much as they could, basically acting as neutral financial intermediaries for millions of Yemenis  . By 2025, Al-Kuraimi Islamic Microfinance Bank – the largest, with over 3 million depositors – was truly nationwide and had to navigate regulation from both central banks (it maintains two sets of accounting: one for its operations in Houthi jurisdiction and one for IRG jurisdiction). Such actors might be key to future reintegration.

Looking at indicators at end of 2025: The Yemeni economy remains roughly halved compared to 2014 (nominal GDP around $30–35 billion vs ~$60 billion pre-war)  . Oil production is only a few tens of thousands of barrels per day (mostly for domestic use, no exports). Foreign reserves are minimal – excluding the still-unused SDR holdings, CBY-Aden has maybe $200 million in liquid reserves . Annual inflation is estimated ~20% in 2025 (south), lower in north but with caveats of suppressed prices . Humanitarian aid flows, while lower than their 2018 peak, still account for over 10% of GDP. Remittances also remain crucial at an estimated $3–4 billion per year (with Saudi Arabia’s recent easing of expat employment restrictions benefiting Yemeni workers). Poverty levels likely plateaued around 80% of the population in need of assistance  .

In summary, as of November 2025, Yemen’s financial and economic landscape shows signs of fragile stabilization after a decade of extreme volatility and fragmentation. The dual monetary systems persist, but the gap has not widened further and is somewhat managed; inflation and the rial are not in free-fall, thanks to injections of external support and internal adaptations. However, this stability is built on shaky ground – it relies on continued ceasefire-like conditions, outside financial aid, and the absence of new shocks. Any breakdown in the political détente could quickly unravel the gains. Moreover, without a political resolution to truly unify economic institutions, Yemen’s economy cannot fully recover – at best, it can limp along with parallel structures. The next section of this report will now provide a thematic deep-dive into the key components of this complex financial system: the central banks, commercial banks, informal networks, major economic actors, and how the war reshaped all of their roles.

Central Bank of Yemen: Fragmentation and Currency Warfare

The Central Bank of Yemen (CBY) has been the fulcrum of Yemen’s financial crisis. Once a unified institution that managed monetary policy and safeguarded the economy, it split into rival branches in 2016 – one internationally recognized in Aden, and one under Houthi control in Sana’a. This CBY fragmentation lies at the heart of Yemen’s currency chaos and banking system disruption. Below, we profile each branch and their policies since the split, and examine the impact of this division.

CBY (Sana’a branch, Houthi-controlled): Headquartered in the capital Sana’a and operating under the Houthi de facto authorities (who call themselves the “National Salvation Government”), this branch retains much of the pre-war CBY staff and infrastructure. However, it is not internationally recognized as Yemen’s central bank and thus has no direct access to Yemen’s foreign assets or international banking  . After the 2016 split, all Yemen’s official foreign reserves (then around $1 billion) were effectively under the control of the Aden CBY. The Sana’a CBY also cannot deal openly with organizations like the IMF or receive dollar deposits from Saudi Arabia, etc. In practice, the Sana’a branch’s role has been to manage liquidity and clearing within Houthi-controlled territories and to enforce the Houthi financial policies. It:
	•	Holds and prints local currency (old banknotes): The Sana’a CBY ended up with the bulk of Yemen’s stock of pre-2017 rial banknotes in its vaults. It has continued to re-circulate these and even printed counterfeit-lookalike new notes of the old design in 2017–2019 to increase supply (though they officially deny printing currency, there is evidence of some secret note production). They explicitly banned the Aden-printed notes in 2019  , so they deal only in these “old rials.” This ironically constrained their ability to expand the money supply (which helped keep the northern rial stable, at the cost of cash shortages).
	•	Sets a pegged exchange rate and interest rate: The Sana’a CBY still declares an official rate (roughly YR 600/US$ for most of 2020–2025) , and insists that banks and exchange shops in their areas transact at or near that rate. Of course, since they don’t provide hard currency, a black market persists. But the official peg has served as a psychological anchor. They do not really use interest rates as a policy tool (and in 2018 officially abolished interest on savings, aligning with Islamic finance principles), so monetary policy is mainly through quantitative control (cash limits) and administrative measures.
	•	Regulates banks in the north: The Houthi-controlled CBY supervises the commercial banks headquartered in Sana’a (which as of 2023 is most Yemeni banks). It has its own Governor (currently Hashim Ismail) and board. It issues circulars on reserve requirements, bank fees, anti-money laundering, etc., often conflicting with Aden’s instructions  . For example, it instructed banks not to share data with Aden and not to participate in Aden’s FX auctions. Banks that complied with Aden faced harassment: in 2020 the Houthi CBY detained senior staff of Tadhamon Bank and Al-Kuraimi Bank on suspicions of working with Aden  . The Sana’a CBY has also issued its own debt instruments – it’s known to have rolled over pre-war government bonds and credited interest into frozen accounts (essentially an accounting trick since that interest can’t be withdrawn)  .
	•	Implements Houthi fiscal decisions: Since the Sana’a CBY still processes what remains of state finances in Houthi areas, it’s responsible for moving money for salary payments (to the extent they happen) and financing the Houthi government’s operations. However, because revenues in Houthi areas largely bypass the formal system and go directly to their war efforts, the Sana’a CBY’s role in 2020s fiscal matters has been limited to paying partial salaries to some sectors (often via those voucher schemes or through the Yemen Petroleum Company’s accounts) and facilitating internal loans from banks. One controversial action was that the Sana’a CBY in 2018-19 seized government salary funds that were in transit in the banking system and redirected them to pay Houthi security forces, etc., effectively raiding deposits earmarked for civil servants (this was documented by the UN Panel)  .

In short, the Sana’a CBY has acted as the central bank of a parallel state – it keeps the Houthi economy running in local currency, maintains a stable (if artificial) exchange rate, and holds the banking sector in line through a mix of persuasion and coercion. Notably, Houthi authorities have been highly critical of the Aden CBY and often accuse it of currency speculation and mismanagement (as in the Saudi deposit affair). From the Houthi perspective, their CBY is “protecting” Yemen from what they frame as a collapse induced by Hadi/PLC’s corruption  .

CBY (Aden branch, IRG/PLC-controlled): Based in the temporary capital Aden, this is the internationally recognized Central Bank of Yemen – the one that the IMF, World Bank, etc., deal with as the legitimate monetary authority  . Since September 2016, it has been the custodian of such foreign assets and international accounts as Yemen has. However, it initially started with almost no staff, data, or domestic banking relationships in 2016. Over time, it built capacity (with hundreds of employees eventually relocating to Aden and new hires, often less experienced). The Aden CBY’s trajectory has been fraught with governance issues and political interference (multiple changes of governor: there have been five governors between 2016 and 2023). Key aspects of the Aden CBY:
	•	Currency issuance: The Aden CBY, working with the internationally recognized government, printed substantial new Yemeni rial banknotes starting 2017 (contracting firms like Goznak in Russia). These new notes (different size and designs) were shipped into Aden in batches. By 2020, they had printed an estimated YR 1–2 trillion in various denominations . This injection was used to pay salaries and finance the deficit, but of course it greatly expanded money supply, leading the southern rial’s value to plummet. After the Houthi ban in late 2019, these new notes became essentially only usable in government areas (with some black-market trading across lines). The Aden CBY also introduced 200-rial and 1,000-rial new coins/metallic notes in 2022 to circumvent Houthi restrictions (since the Houthi ban only mentioned paper notes) – a quirky workaround that saw limited use.
	•	Foreign exchange and reserves: The Aden CBY holds Yemen’s SDRs at the IMF and had custody of the $2 billion Saudi deposit of 2018 (which it largely spent by end of 2020 on import subsidies)  . It manages any new external financial assistance (like the $300 million from Saudi Arabia in 2023 to bolster reserves, or the $1 billion from the Arab Monetary Fund arrangement). However, its usable foreign reserves have often been near zero . For example, by late 2021 it literally had only a few million dollars left freely available until new aid came. This has constrained its ability to intervene in the currency market consistently. It does run FX auctions now – selling $15 million per week on average in 2023   – which help smooth the exchange rate somewhat. The IMF SDR allocation in 2021 remains largely untouched at the IMF, because converting it requires either finding willing counterparties or reaching an accord with the Houthis (who assert claim to a share of it).
	•	Monetary policy: The Aden CBY has used classical tools to a limited extent. It raised the benchmark interest rate on Yemeni rial deposits to as high as 27% in late 2021 to try to attract rials out of circulation (though with high inflation that was not a real positive rate) . It also occasionally issues CBY certificates of deposit or bonds to local banks to mop up excess liquidity – but since banks are cash-strapped, uptake is low. In effect, the Aden CBY’s main policy instrument has been the exchange rate (letting it slide or trying to stabilize it via auctions) and direct market interventions (selling dollars) when possible.
	•	Banking system oversight: The Aden CBY has legal authority over all Yemeni banks, but in practice since 2016 it only fully controls those banks that relocated to Aden or are newly established there. It has tried to assert oversight by, for instance, demanding that banks move their SWIFT operations to Aden and report their balance sheets to Aden. Some banks have complied partially – e.g. the International Bank of Yemen moved some back-office operations to Amman (Jordan) and then channels info to Aden. The Aden CBY has also penalized banks seen as non-compliant: in 2021 it froze the assets of Yemen Kuwait Bank and Yemen Commercial Bank in government areas and signaled intent to liquidate them when they refused data sharing  . The outcome was mostly symbolic (those banks continue in Houthi areas regardless). The Aden CBY also works to keep Yemeni banks connected internationally – in 2017–2018 many foreign banks cut correspondence with Yemeni banks due to risk, but Aden CBY, with Western and Saudi help, arranged some temporary overseas accounts (e.g. with the Saudi National Bank) to facilitate trade payments. It also set up a U.S. dollar clearing mechanism in Aden for NGOs so they can bring in dollars and convert at a unified rate for projects.
	•	Leadership and credibility: A challenge for the Aden CBY has been frequent leadership turnover and allegations of corruption or mismanagement. For instance, the UN Panel’s 2021 allegation that the CBY and government embezzled $423 million of the Saudi deposit via collusion with traders – albeit later retracted – severely hurt the CBY’s credibility  . It prompted donors to bypass the CBY for aid (UN agencies wouldn’t deposit aid dollars into CBY-Aden, instead they operated currency swaps through controlled auctions to avoid boosting the black market rate) . The appointment of Governor Ahmed Ghaleb in 2022 came with an agenda of reform (he’s an experienced technocrat), and since then the CBY-Aden has published more financial information and undergone an audit by an international firm . Still, its independence is compromised by politics – decisions often require consultation with the Presidential Council and foreign backers. The Southern Transitional Council’s influence also looms: in 2020, STC forces physically surrounded the Aden CBY to demand certain policy changes  ; and in 2023 the STC has placed loyalists in some CBY local management positions.

To summarize, the duel between the two CBYs has created a “fractured monetary policy” – one side tight and static, the other side loose and reactive. A Sana’a Center report encapsulated it: “neither the Aden nor Sana’a branch can function independently; one lacks funds and recognition, the other lacks staff and nationwide reach”  . The outcomes have been disastrous: multiple exchange rates, loss of monetary control, and a collapse of public trust in the currency. Indeed, Yemenis refer to this period as the “currency war” – a war that has “undermined confidence in the rial and driven people to hoard dollars and Saudi riyals”  .

Reunifying the central bank is widely seen as a prerequisite for economic recovery. Talks on this were held (in Amman, 2020) but collapsed over disputes like who would be governor and how to handle past debts  . For now, coordination is minimal: occasionally, via back-channels, they have synchronized certain moves (e.g. both CBYs reportedly agreed in late 2022 to caution exchange businesses not to profiteer too much on arbitrage as it was hurting everyone, according to a World Bank briefing). But those are band-aids.

The impact on the population of the central bank split is clear: by cutting the banking system in two and undermining the rial’s stability, it has “fueled inflation and deepened economic fragmentation, directly contributing to increased poverty and humanitarian needs”  . One direct metric: as noted earlier, about 8 million Yemenis lost regular salary income after 2016 because the central bank could no longer pay them  , which immediately doubled poverty levels.

In conclusion, the story of Yemen’s central bank(s) is one of a vital institution torn apart by war. The Sana’a branch became a tool of the Houthi war economy (albeit also a stabilizer for the local market), while the Aden branch struggled to fill the shoes of a national central bank but without the nation under its control. Any future peace deal will almost certainly have to address reintegrating the CBY – perhaps via a neutral technical team – because as long as two central banks issue conflicting policies, Yemen will effectively have two economies. As one economic analyst put it, “Without a functioning and unified Central Bank, Yemen’s economy will remain fragmented and any recovery will be stalled”  .

Commercial and Islamic Banks: A Sector Squeezed by War

Yemen’s commercial banking sector, once modestly sized and conservative, has been among the biggest casualties of the war. Banks have faced liquidity crunches, loss of public confidence, and a tug-of-war between two regulatory regimes (Aden and Sana’a). This section profiles the banking sector’s evolution from 2010 to 2025, including the major banks and the challenges they encountered, effectively mapping out a banking system in near-collapse and its partial rebirth through microfinance banks.

Pre-2015 Baseline: Before the war, Yemen had a small banking sector comprising 17 commercial banks (10 private domestic banks, 4 private foreign banks, and 3 state-owned banks) and 2 Islamic banks  . Total assets were around $6 billion (very small relative to GDP). Key banks included:
	•	State-owned banks: Central Bank of Yemen (not a commercial bank, but held government accounts), National Bank of Yemen (NBY) (the largest state commercial bank, headquartered in Aden, with branch network nationwide), and Cooperative & Agricultural Credit Bank (CAC Bank) – a semi-state bank that by 2015 had a significant retail business. These together held a large share of government deposits and served state-owned enterprise needs.
	•	Private banks: Notable ones: Tadhamon International Islamic Bank (TIIB) – Yemen’s largest private bank, Islamic, associated with the Al-Ahmar business family, Yemen Kuwait Bank (YKB) – one of the oldest (est. 1975) conventional banks, International Bank of Yemen (IBY), Yemen Commercial Bank, Arab Bank – Yemen (Jordanian-owned foreign branch), Bank of Yemen and Kuwait (also known as Shamil Bank after merging with an Islamic window), Saba Islamic Bank, Arab Banking Corporation (ABC Yemen) (Bahraini-owned), etc. These banks provided limited services: letters of credit for trade, some corporate lending (often to well-connected families), and basic retail accounts. Even pre-war, only ~6% of Yemenis had a bank account , reflecting low financial inclusion and trust.
	•	Microfinance institutions: There was also Al-Amal Microfinance Bank (est. 2009 as the first microfinance bank in MENA) and a network of microcredit programs under the Social Fund for Development, but those weren’t direct competitors to banks.

Banks in Yemen traditionally held large amounts of government debt (treasury bills) and deposits, meaning they were heavily exposed to public finances. By end of 2014, the government owed local banks YR 1.2 trillion (≈US$5.6 billion) in debt  . Liquidity was adequate then, supported by the Central Bank which offered facilities and had a stable exchange rate. However, banks were not very profitable or efficient, and some, like CAC Bank, had a lot of non-performing loans even pre-war.

Impact of War (2015–2016): When war erupted and the economy imploded, banks immediately suffered a run on deposits and cash. People, fearing instability, rushed to withdraw savings. The Central Bank (still unified in 2015–16) mitigated this to some degree by providing cash and enforcing withdrawal limits, but as conflict dragged, trust evaporated. Many branches in conflict zones closed or lost connection to HQ. By late 2016, when the CBY split, banks were put in an impossible position – two central banks gave them conflicting orders.

A crucial blow was President Hadi’s decision in September 2016 to freeze the Central Bank’s accounts. In practice, this meant that banks in Sana’a could no longer easily settle transactions or get new banknotes. Moreover, the Aden CBY instructed banks to transfer their operations to Aden; the Sana’a CBY (under Houthis) warned them not to. Most banks tried a middle approach: keep head office in Sana’a (since that’s where most clients and staff were), but quietly establish a presence in Aden to deal with the Aden CBY.

The result by 2017 was fragmentation of bank operations. For example:
	•	National Bank of Yemen (NBY) – being state-owned in the south, it fully relocated to Aden (its HQ was already there pre-war) and became the primary bank for the Aden government transactions. Its branches in Houthi areas (like Sana’a) became cut off or under Houthi-appointed managers who presumably only handled local business. NBY’s role expanded in Aden as the “government’s bank” (it received large deposits when other banks couldn’t).
	•	CAC Bank – this unique case: it was state-owned but headquartered in Sana’a pre-war. Post-split, it essentially split into two entities: CAC-Sana’a (which Houthis treat as a state bank under their control) and CAC-Aden (which was re-established in 2018 with a new license under Aden authority)  . So effectively two CAC Banks now exist, each claiming to be the legitimate one.
	•	Other private banks (YKB, Tadhamon, Saba, IBY, etc.) – all remained headquartered in Sana’a through at least 2020. They complied with many Houthi directives (like providing detailed account info, restricting withdrawals per Houthi rules, etc.) because their physical operations were mostly there. At the same time, they tried to maintain correspondent accounts abroad via Aden’s auspices to continue trade finance. For instance, Yemen Kuwait Bank and IBY were targeted by OFAC sanctions in early 2021 because they were seen as tied into Houthi financial flows  . That scared them significantly as it cut off their foreign transfers temporarily. They then sought to get off that list by showing compliance (which they did later when the FTO designation was reversed).

One can measure the banking collapse by a few indicators:
	•	Deposits: It’s estimated that from 2014 to 2017, about 50% of bank deposits were withdrawn or disappeared  . People took out Yemeni rial cash and/or whatever dollars they could (though dollars were scarce). Those who left deposits found them effectively frozen – banks often refused withdrawals above small caps because they lacked cash. In fact, by 2019 most banks imposed withdrawal limits of as low as $100 per account per month in FX and equivalent in rials.
	•	Non-performing loans (NPLs): Businesses couldn’t repay loans amid war, so NPLs skyrocketed. Banks largely stopped new lending after 2015. A CBY Sana’a report in 2020 (by the Development Champions Forum) claimed NPLs exceeded 50% of loan portfolios  .
	•	Capital erosion: With the rial’s value plunging 75%+ and assets in rials, banks’ capital in real terms evaporated. Several banks likely fell below minimum capital adequacy. The Aden CBY at one point (2021) threatened to revoke licenses of banks not meeting capital requirements (set in Yemeni rials)  , but those requirements became meaningless due to currency distortion.
	•	Disconnection from global banking: After 2015, one by one, foreign correspondent banks cut ties with Yemeni banks. HSBC, Citibank, etc., had earlier been correspondents; they closed accounts citing risk. By 2017, Yemeni banks struggled to confirm letters of credit. The Lebanese banking crisis in 2019 also hit Yemeni banks that held deposits in Beirut for foreign exchange needs  . For example, it’s reported that Yemeni banks had tens of millions stuck in Lebanese banks after 2019. Additionally, Yemen was blacklisted by FATF as high-risk in 2015  , making any international transactions very difficult.

Given these shocks, most Yemenis lost whatever limited trust they had in banks. People stopped using checks or electronic payments (beyond a narrow business elite). The economy became over 90% cash-based by 2019, according to the World Bank. This is illustrated by the massive physical cash movements – traders hire trucks or camels to move cash between governorates instead of bank transfers.

Banks essentially went into survival mode. They drastically cut operations, closed branches in dangerous areas, and shifted to fee-based services (like money transfers and currency exchange) to make any income. A telling statistic: by 2021, the combined loan portfolio of all Yemeni banks was about 1/3 of what it was in 2014, meaning credit to private sector collapsed  .

Yet banks did not outright collapse en masse – partly because of how they restructured. They basically became warehouses for deposits they couldn’t use, and their biggest liability – government deposits and debt – was put into deep freeze. The Houthi-run CBY in Sana’a took a critical step in 2018: it told banks that all interest payments on existing government bonds would be recognized but put into a “blocked account” that can’t be withdrawn  . In other words, banks would accrue interest on paper for treasury bills, but they wouldn’t actually get paid. Similarly, in Aden, the government stopped paying interest on its debts after 2016. Thus, banks had huge assets in government debt that were illiquid – effectively, a large chunk of their balance sheet was frozen.

This “freeze” of assets and liabilities created a zombie banking system. Banks neither truly functioned nor were they resolved; they just existed. For ordinary customers, the reality was you could deposit money, but withdrawing it was another story. People joked that a bank account is a one-way street.

Major Individual Banks in War Economy:
	•	Tadhamon International Islamic Bank (TIIB): Before war, it was largest by assets. Owned by Hayel Saeed Anam Group and Al-Ahmar family interests. During war, it remained in Sana’a, presumably functioning under Houthi supervision. Notably, in 2020 the Houthi Security forces detained TIIB’s deputy GM for cooperating with Aden’s currency auctions  . TIIB also reportedly handled a lot of NGO funds in Sana’a (as one of few banks with broad reach and trade finance capability). By 2022, TIIB was one of the banks targeted by Aden CBY’s relocation order because it hadn’t moved; it largely ignored that order.
	•	Yemen Kuwait Bank (YKB): Oldest private bank. HQ Sana’a. YKB got entangled in sanctions in 2021: OFAC listed it as facilitating Houthi financial transactions  . YKB denied wrongdoing, claiming it was just providing normal services. After US policy reversal on Houthis, YKB was not sanctioned further, but it underscores how banks were seen as aligned with authorities controlling their zone. YKB was punished by Aden CBY too: in late 2021, Aden CBY “suspended” YKB from clearing facilities in government areas  . YKB’s fate exemplifies the squeeze: by 2022, it could only operate fully in Houthi areas and lost most business elsewhere.
	•	CAC Bank: As mentioned, it split formally. CAC (Aden) was relaunched and used by the Aden government (e.g., in 2022 Aden CBY asked state agencies to transfer accounts from private banks to state banks like CAC and NBY to keep money close  ). CAC (Sana’a), meanwhile, was used by Houthis to handle things like public telecom revenues etc. Interestingly, in 2022, the Houthis militarized CAC-Sana’a: they turned its extensive branch network into quasi money exchange outlets for the e-Rial scheme.
	•	International Bank of Yemen (IBY): Private, HQ Sana’a. In 2020, Aden CBY threatened to prosecute IBY’s top managers for not relocating data  . In 2021, IBY along with YKB and YBRD (Reconstruction Bank) were referred by Aden to public prosecution on charges of violating currency regulations  . Of course, Aden’s ability to enforce that was limited, as the execs were in Sana’a. It mainly served to burn bridges. IBY continued in north without much presence in south. ACAPS (a humanitarian analysis org) noted that IBY, YKB, YBRD, Kuraimi, and Tadhamon together handled ~80% of all humanitarian organization money transfers by 2018  . That means these banks, despite issues, became crucial conduits for aid (since aid groups had to pay staff/vendors in both regions, they often deposited dollars outside and asked these banks to deliver rials inside Yemen).
	•	Hayel Saeed Anam (HSA) Group’s role in banking: HSA, Yemen’s largest conglomerate, holds significant shares in at least two banks: Tadhamon and Yemen Kuwait Bank (and possibly others indirectly). This relationship drew scrutiny especially around the Saudi deposit corruption allegations: the UN Panel accused CBY-Aden of giving HSA’s Tadhamon Bank preferential treatment  . While those specific allegations were withdrawn, it’s clear HSA’s banks were heavily involved in the LC program (since HSA Group companies received many LCs and likely routed them via Tadhamon or YKB). The war thus further entwined big business and banking.

Banking under dual authority (2020–2025): Essentially, by 2020 the core functions of banks had shifted:
	•	Money transfer and remittances: Banks partnered with hawala companies to facilitate transfers since banks themselves lost networks. For example, QualityConnect, a Houthi-aligned fintech, is partly owned by YKB along with exchange houses   – showing how banks had to join exchangers to remain relevant in remittances.
	•	Trade finance: Still, some banks maintained ability to do letters of credit (especially for UN and large importers). They did this through Aden CBY’s coordination. The Aden CBY in 2022 started a system of opening LCs for importers via a few designated banks (like NBY, CAC-Aden), marginalizing others. Meanwhile, the Houthi side created their own import financing schemes (e.g., requiring importers via Hodeidah to deposit equivalent funds in Sana’a banks, etc.). This meant importers often had to engage with both sets of banks depending on route. Some importers reportedly had “mirror accounts” – one in Aden, one in Sana’a – and had to juggle both. For example, fuel importers might pay in Aden for paperwork but then pay taxes to Houthi via CAC-Sana’a; complex.
	•	Currency speculation: Many banks (especially those in Sana’a) were accused of profiting from arbitrage – e.g., using their access to old and new rials across both markets. In mid-2021, Aden CBY actually inspected several banks for speculation and closed 50 exchange shops for fueling the rial’s fall  . It’s believed some banks did use front companies to buy currency high/low and gain. However, banks also had to do this to meet customer needs in absence of official channels.

By 2022–2023, the banking sector’s condition was starting to receive attention for recovery plans. The Development Champions Forum (a Yemeni expert group) recommended in 2022 a “comprehensive banking sector rescue roadmap” including: debt restructuring (because banks held lots of unpayable gov’t debt), recapitalization, unification of regulation, and restoring correspondent banking lines  .

A hopeful sign: A few banks that had been effectively defunct restarted in Aden. Bank of Aden, a new small public bank, opened in 2019. Also, Wadi Hadramout Bank, a private bank licensed by Aden in 2018, began operations focusing on eastern governorates  . These indicate the PLC was trying to foster banking in its areas separately from Sana’a. But these are tiny compared to established banks.

In Houthi areas, rather than create new banks, authorities leaned on exchange companies morphing into microfinance banks (discussed next section). They also considered merging or forcing weak banks to close – e.g., in 2020 the Houthi CBY briefly forbid Saba Islamic Bank from certain activities until it increased capital.

The net effect: Yemen’s formal banking sector has been largely supplanted by informal financial networks. A 2021 study found informal exchangers handled ~80% of all financial transactions in Yemen  . Banks that survive do so by becoming quasi-money changers themselves or by aligning with authorities.

For the Yemeni public, this means higher costs and risks: bank credit for businesses is practically unavailable (so businesses can’t easily invest or expand), people’s savings either are stuck in a bank you can’t withdraw from or held as cash that loses value with inflation, and doing any cross-regional or international payment is cumbersome and expensive due to multiple intermediaries.

To quote the Sana’a Center: “The net effect [of the war] is a ‘de-banked’ economy: banks still exist on paper… but the real circulation of money has migrated elsewhere.”  . Banks now primarily play niche roles: facilitating some import/export paperwork and safeguarding some funds for big clients who have no choice. Everything else – salary payments, transfers, currency exchange, credit provision – has shifted to unregulated or parallel mechanisms.

In conclusion, Yemen’s commercial banking system has been one of the biggest economic victims of the conflict. Once the pipes of financial intermediation, they became clogged or broken, forcing money to move through informal channels. Rehabilitating this sector will be a herculean task requiring peace, trust-building, and likely external financial support (for recapitalization and clearing the massive bad assets off their books). Without functioning banks, it’s hard to imagine a robust private sector recovery, and Yemen risks remaining a cash and carry economy.

Next, we turn to one area where some financial innovation and resilience did occur amid this collapse: the rise of microfinance and informal networks as substitutes for the traditional banks.

Microfinance and Informal Financial Networks

As Yemen’s formal banking sector retreated during the war, microfinance institutions (MFIs) and money exchange/hawala networks expanded to fill the void. A striking outcome of the conflict has been the transformation of many exchange companies into licensed microfinance banks, effectively creating a new tier of quasi-banks that operate with lighter regulation and closer ties to local communities. Simultaneously, the traditional hawala system – an informal remittance and money transfer network – became the backbone of domestic and international financial flows. This section explores the growth of microfinance, the role of the Yemen Microfinance Network, and the functioning of major money exchange/hawala networks like Al-Kuraimi and Swaid & Sons.

Pre-war Microfinance: Yemen’s microfinance sector began in the late 1990s with donor support to alleviate poverty  . By 2010, a number of small MFIs operated as NGO programs providing micro-loans (often under the Social Fund for Development’s umbrella). In 2009, Yemen established Al-Amal Microfinance Bank – the first deposit-taking microfinance bank in the Arab world  . Al-Amal was co-founded by the Yemeni government and the World Bank; by 2014 it had a few tens of thousands of clients. Also around 2010, exchange companies like Al-Kuraimi started offering quasi-banking services (Al-Kuraimi had thousands of money transfer agents across Yemen even then). The Yemen Microfinance Network (YMN) was formed in 2009 as an association to support MFIs with training, technical assistance, and advocacy  . By 2014, microfinance had about 121,000 active borrowers (nearly double the number in 2010) and a loan portfolio of YR 12.2 billion (~$57 million)  . It was making inroads in rural areas and among women entrepreneurs.

Impact of war on microfinance: Initially, the war hit MFIs hard: by early 2016, active microfinance borrowers dropped ~23% to ~93,000 as clients fled conflict zones or defaulted  . Some MFIs, especially NGO-run programs, lost donor funding or saw their offices destroyed. However, something paradoxical happened from 2017 onward: microfinance banks, especially Al-Kuraimi, saw a surge in depositors. With big banks paralyzed and people distrustful of them, many Yemenis turned to microfinance banks as safer or more accessible places to put money  .
	•	Al-Kuraimi Islamic Microfinance Bank: Originally an exchange company (Al-Kuraimi Exchange, established in the 1990s), it received a microfinance bank license in 2010. During the war, it has arguably become the largest financial institution in Yemen by client base. By 2023, Al-Kuraimi had 3.3 million depositors – an astonishing growth capturing 91% of all microfinance depositors and 96% of deposits in the microfinance sector  . Essentially, Al-Kuraimi became “the people’s bank” during the conflict  . Why? Several reasons:
	•	It had a ubiquitous agent network (hundreds of outlets across all governorates, including rural areas, often through local retailers acting as agents). So even when banks closed, Kuraimi agents were open, facilitating transfers and savings.
	•	It embraced technology: Al-Kuraimi introduced a mobile money app early on and e-wallet services, making it easier to transact amid checkpoints and fuel shortages  .
	•	Public trust: Kuraimi marketed itself as more accessible and less bureaucratic than banks, and not politically aligned. Even when the rial depreciated, Kuraimi offered partial hedging via letting customers hold balances in either rials or sometimes in commodities like mobile credits.
By 2021–22, Al-Kuraimi held over 90% of all microfinance bank deposit accounts in Yemen  . That equates to millions of Yemenis trusting Al-Kuraimi more than conventional banks or even keeping cash at home. Al-Kuraimi was so crucial that both central banks didn’t shut it down even when tensions arose. In March 2020, Houthi authorities briefly closed Al-Kuraimi’s Sana’a branches to pressure it into compliance with their data-sharing demands  . This caused an uproar because pensions and humanitarian cash transfers were distributed through Kuraimi. The branches reopened after Kuraimi agreed to conditions like moving its HQ to Aden on paper (as per Aden CBY requirement) while still cooperating with Sana’a CBY rules in practice – a delicate balancing act.
	•	Other microfinance banks: The war spurred proliferation of new microfinance banks (MFBs). Many money exchange firms decided to formalize as MFBs since CBY-Aden made it easier to get an MFB license than a full commercial bank license  . For instance:
	•	Al-<Name>Microfinance Bank: multiple ex-exchangers launched MFBs. Examples: Al-Oulufi Microfinance Bank, Al-Kuraimi (already covered), Azal Microfinance Bank, etc. According to YMN, by 2023 there were at least 11 licensed microfinance banks, up from 2 in 2010  .
	•	Regional MFBs: Al-Busairi Microfinance Bank (est. 2022 in Mukalla, from an exchange house; focuses eastern Yemen)  ; Bin Dowa Microfinance Bank (2022 in Hadhramaut)  ; Al-Qasimi Islamic Microfinance Bank (2023, originated from Al-Qasimi Exchange in Sana’a, interestingly got licensed by both Aden & Sana’a authorities)  .
These examples highlight how exchangers in both north and south took advantage of regulatory arbitrage: by becoming MFBs, they could take deposits and expand services without needing to meet the higher capital of a commercial bank. The Aden CBY encouraged this to some extent as a way to improve financial inclusion  .

This boom in MFBs led to concerns of over-saturation and weak oversight. By 2024, experts noted “rapid proliferation; quality and risk management vary widely; mission drift possible as MFBs compete in urban markets”  . In response, the Aden CBY in 2023 mulled a moratorium on new MFB licenses until supervision capacity improved  . The Development Champions Forum recommended exactly that: pause new MFB licensing, strengthen regulation, and possibly merge some for stability  .

The Yemen Microfinance Network (YMN) has been a behind-the-scenes player trying to support these institutions. YMN, with support from Social Fund for Development (SFD) and donors, provided training to MFI staff, collected sector data, and advocated for policy support. For instance, YMN data revealed that by 2023, the number of microfinance “active savers” (depositors) grew 28% annually despite war reaching 3.3 million  , largely due to Al-Kuraimi as noted. YMN also documented that informal MFIs (NGO-run) struggled more as donor funds dried; some pivoted to become lending-only NGOs as they couldn’t take deposits like MFBs can  .

Hawala and Money Exchange Networks: Parallel to microfinance banks, Yemen’s traditional money exchangers (hawaladars) became absolutely central to the wartime economy. They are used for:
	•	Remittances from abroad: with banking channels severed, Yemeni diaspora (especially in Saudi, Gulf, US, UK) rely on hawala companies to send money home. These come in through networks like Al-Omgy, Al-Akwa, Al-Jamal etc., often via Dubai or Amman intermediaries. The World Bank estimated remittances remained the largest forex source even as official flows were partly unrecorded  .
	•	Domestic transfers: People moving money between north and south nearly all use exchangers due to the currency and banking complications. Traders also use them to settle invoices.

One prominent network is Swaid & Sons Exchange. Based in Sana’a with links abroad, Swaid grew massively during the war. It reportedly “became one of Yemen’s largest exchange houses, operating across the country and internationally”  . The US Treasury alleged Swaid (led by one Saeed al-Jamal network) was channeling Iranian funds to the Houthis through a complex scheme involving oil smuggling  . In mid-2021, the US sanctioned Swaid & Sons, calling it part of an IRGC-Quds Force finance network  . In reaction, the Yemeni government’s Aden branch froze Swaid’s accounts and licenses in June 2021  . However, Swaid’s nationwide network (branded as “Mal” network) continued underground   – the Houthis shielded it, and only its Marib branch (in gov’t territory) was shut  . The “Mal” transfer system, linking many exchangers, still processed probably millions in aid and remittances, albeit now unofficially (since official license was revoked by Aden).

Another key player was Al-Omgy Exchange. The Al-Omgy brothers historically had ties to various groups. In 2016, the US Treasury sanctioned Al-Omgy Exchange for alleged AQAP money laundering  . They were one of the largest exchangers in Aden pre-war, and that sanction forced them basically out of formal business (which likely boosted Kuraimi in the south).

Hawala networks vs banks: Over the war, hawaladars and exchangers formed consortia to self-regulate somewhat and lobby the powers. In Sana’a, there’s a Money Exchangers Association that in 2021 publicly criticized the US Houthi sanctions, warning it could destroy Yemen’s financial market  . They also negotiated with Houthi CBY on how to implement bans etc. In Aden, big exchangers influenced CBY’s decisions on auctions (some allegedly colluded to drive up rates at auctions in 2021).

The interplay is such that many exchangers effectively became MFBs, and many MFBs operate like exchangers. For example, Al-Kuraimi still does tons of pure remittance business beyond micro-loans. It’s often listed among top exchangers as well as top microfinance banks.

Yemen’s reliance on informal networks had pros and cons:
	•	Pros: These networks kept money moving when formal channels broke. They are fast and accessible – even remote villagers can often find an exchange agent within a day’s travel. They also have been flexible in working around blockades (smuggling cash, phone credit transfers, barter, etc., as needed to deliver value to intended recipients).
	•	Cons: Transparency and AML/CFT compliance suffered. There’s minimal oversight, raising risks of money laundering (for terrorism, narcotics, etc.)  . Also, fees soared in the worst periods – the north-south transfer fee hitting 12% in 2020 as noted  . Additionally, heavy reliance on hawala undermines central bank monetary control and revenue (since taxes on informal transfers are often evaded).
	•	Regulatory response: Aden CBY in 2019–2020 licensed many exchangers formally (over 600 licensed money exchange businesses at one point). Then it realized things got out of hand and in late 2020 moved to revoke or not renew licenses of hundreds of small exchangers to curb speculation. Houthi CBY also tried to consolidate exchangers – in 2019 they shut down many small shops and insisted transfers go through a handful of big networks to monitor. Both authorities also instituted a sort of “clearing system” for hawala: e.g. in 2022 they nearly agreed on connecting a shared digital platform to settle north-south transfers by offsetting flows (that’s reportedly how the UN and ACAPS came to the *“80% of aid flows through banks in Houthi areas” observation – possibly meaning flows end up cleared through banks or a system in Sana’a)  .

The Yemen Microfinance Network (YMN) and SFD, for their part, continued supporting MFIs with whatever resources. SFD’s Microenterprise Unit (SMEPS) pivoted to helping small businesses survive war, and in doing so, sometimes used MFIs as channels (loans to fishers, farmers, etc.). YMN and SFD also advocated for MFIs to get relief: e.g., urging that donors provide credit lines to MFIs so they can on-lend to struggling microbusinesses (some of that happened, like an Islamic microfinance fund by Islamic Relief Yemen to support MFIs).

By 2025, microfinance banks and hawala networks have effectively become Yemen’s primary financial system for the public  . This was summarized succinctly: “The formal monetary center lost control, while informal and semi-formal networks – exchangers, remittance houses, microfinance banks – quietly became the spine of the economy”  . Indeed, these networks handle everything from a rural family getting cash from a son working in Saudi, to an NGO paying its contractors, to traders paying suppliers abroad via middlemen.

The sustainability of this “shadow banking” system is questionable. Risks include: lack of deposit insurance (if a big exchanger/MFI fails, depositors lose out), possible collapses if war dynamics shift (imagine if a peace unifies currency at a certain rate – some hawala dealers might go bankrupt covering positions), and their limited capacity to finance larger investments (micro-loans seldom exceed a few thousand dollars, insufficient for bigger reconstruction needs).

Policy recommendations emerging have been:
	•	Bring these networks into the formal fold gradually. For instance, encourage the biggest exchangers to become microfinance banks (already done) then eventually banks when safe.
	•	Create a credit registry and payment systems that include MFIs, so that micro-borrowers can build credit history and MFIs can avoid overlending  .
	•	Improve regulation: specialized microfinance banking law updates to address the boom; capacity building for CBY to supervise dozens of MFIs effectively  .
	•	Possibly merging some MFBs to avoid unhealthy competition, as too many players could lead to risk-taking and defaults .

In conclusion, Yemen’s microfinance and hawala sector stepped up to keep money moving during the war. They innovated and expanded where formal institutions failed. While they mitigated humanitarian suffering by allowing remittances and aid to reach households (an estimated 20% of Yemeni households rely on hawala remittances as main income  ), they are no substitute for a stable, integrative financial system. They have, however, laid a foundation for financial inclusion: whenever peace comes, Yemen can leverage the fact that millions got accustomed to using these microfinance services, meaning there’s potential to quickly scale up banking penetration with the right reforms.

Next, we shift from institutions to actors – specifically, the domestic and foreign stakeholders whose financial roles have shaped Yemen’s war economy: from regional states like Saudi, UAE, and Iran, to international organizations and local business conglomerates like HSA.

International and Regional Actors in Yemen’s War Economy

Yemen’s financial and economic landscape from 2010–2025 has been profoundly influenced by external actors. Regional states – notably Saudi Arabia, the UAE, Iran, Oman, Kuwait, Qatar, and others – as well as global powers like the US, UK, and EU, have all played roles through aid, military intervention, sanctions, and economic engagement. International financial institutions (IMF, World Bank) and humanitarian agencies (UN, NGOs) have also shaped outcomes by injecting funds or imposing conditions. This section profiles each major state actor and international stakeholder, detailing their financial involvement in Yemen’s conflict and economy.

Saudi Arabia

Saudi Arabia has been the single most influential external economic actor in Yemen during this period. Its roles include:
	•	Direct war financing and aid: Saudi Arabia led the military coalition supporting the Hadi government from 2015, spending billions on military operations. It also shouldered much of the humanitarian and budgetary aid for the IRG. In 2018, Saudi deposited US$2 billion into Yemen’s Central Bank (Aden), effectively rescuing the Yemeni rial at that time  . By late 2021, most of that deposit was used (financing critical imports of food and fuel)  . In mid-2022, upon formation of the Presidential Leadership Council, Saudi (with UAE) pledged another $2 billion deposit plus $1 billion in development support . However, disbursement was slow: about $1 billion of that reached Aden in 2023 via the Arab Monetary Fund as a special drawing arrangement, boosting reserves and stabilizing the currency  .
	•	Budgetary support: Saudi grants have propped up Yemen’s government finances. From 2012–2014, Saudi gave at least $1 billion in cash grants to Hadi’s government (and fuel donations)  . Post-2015, Riyadh continued intermittent support: paying salaries of certain Yemeni military units, providing heavily subsidized fuel for power plants (e.g. a $60 million monthly fuel grant in 2018–2019 kept lights on in Aden)  . In late 2023, Saudi committed to fund civil servant salary payments as part of peace talks – for example, exploring mechanisms to pay Yemeni teachers and health workers via international channels (this is under negotiation). Saudi also bankrolls specific programs like the Yemen Economic Stabilization program and had set up the Saudi Development and Reconstruction Program for Yemen (SDRPY) which implemented small projects (schools, hospitals, power generators) and injected cash-for-work funds.
	•	Humanitarian aid: Saudi Arabia has been either the #1 or #2 largest donor to the UN Yemen Humanitarian Appeals each year since 2015. For instance, in 2018 it contributed about $500 million to UN (and similar to what the US gave) . It often coordinates with the UAE on pledges. Saudi aid goes through UN, KSrelief (King Salman Humanitarian Aid and Relief Center), and directly to communities. Notably, Saudi & UAE covered a $200 million/month gap for fuel & salaries in 2019 for a few months to prevent collapse  . However, that support has fluctuated; by 2020, Saudi focused more on in-kind aid.
	•	Currency and monetary policy influence: The Saudi riyal (SAR) has become a secondary currency in parts of Yemen (particularly eastern provinces and as a store of value in the south) because of rial volatility  . Saudi economic policies (like deporting Yemeni workers during Saudization in 2013 and again in 2021) directly affect Yemen’s remittance flows and thus macro stability . In 2021, when Riyadh restricted Houthi-controlled passport holders in the Kingdom, thousands of Yemenis lost jobs, cutting remittances – this was partly reversed under truce atmospherics in 2022.
	•	Political patronage: Saudi has long financially supported various Yemeni tribal leaders, political figures and even Salafi networks. During the war, the Presidential Leadership Council members received Saudi stipends presumably. This stream is opaque but significant – it fuels local power structures and war economy aspects (e.g. buying loyalties).

Overall, Saudi Arabia’s financial involvement has been a lifeline for the IRG economy and a tool of influence. It uses money as carrot and stick: e.g., withholding the second tranche of the 2022 deposit until reforms are seen, and offering salaries if the Houthis agree to terms  . However, Saudi’s largesse also came with downsides: the massive Saudi deposit spending in 2018–2020, while stabilizing currency, was mired in the corruption controversy (UN Panel’s allegation of $423 m misused via favorable exchange rates to traders)  , which hurt confidence. And heavy aid reliance may have induced complacency in economic management (some Yemeni officials expecting “the Saudis will pay for it”).

United Arab Emirates (UAE)

The UAE has also played a major financial role, though somewhat more narrowly focused compared to Saudi:
	•	Military spending and local clients: The UAE was the second-largest in the coalition; it spent substantial sums arming and training local Yemeni factions, especially the Southern Transitional Council (STC) and affiliated southern militias (Security Belt, Shabwani Elite, Giants Brigades). From 2016–2019, the UAE essentially funded the parallel security apparatus in the south and west coast, including reportedly paying the salaries of 90,000 Yemeni fighters  . This injection of money created a war economy in those areas – e.g. many young men joined UAE-backed units for the steady pay (which was often higher than government army pay, and paid on time). The UAE also directly financed infrastructure in southern areas under STC control – rebuilding Aden’s airport and ports, for instance.
	•	Direct aid and development: The UAE has been a top humanitarian donor as well, often jointly pledging with Saudi. It contributed similarly large amounts in 2018 (around $500 million) and continued significant aid via the Emirates Red Crescent, focusing on south and west coast. One notable program: the UAE supplied free fuel to run Aden’s power plants for years; however, in 2021 it halted that, contributing to Aden’s electricity crisis  . The UAE, through Abu Dhabi Fund for Development, also deposited US$300 million in Yemen’s CBY in 2018 (out of the $2b combined deposit). In 2022, it pledged $1b as part of the new package, but as of late 2023, it had not fully disbursed its share (reports said the UAE was waiting to see progress on reforms and STC inclusion) .
	•	Control of strategic assets: The UAE’s financial role is also tied to its geopolitical interests. It effectively controls seaports and airports in certain regions: e.g., Socotra Island, where since 2018–2020 the UAE (and STC) presence meant Socotra’s economy is basically under UAE patronage. The UAE developed infrastructure there (telecom networks, ports) and in doing so monopolized some local businesses (the report notes a UAE-linked distributor raising fuel prices ~20x on Socotra, causing protests about a “monopoly”  ). On the Red Sea coast, the UAE via its ally Tariq Saleh finances the National Resistance forces and their civil administration around Mocha – it’s even funded an expansion of Mocha port for shipping. These create local economies tethered to UAE support.
	•	Private sector investment: The UAE has signaled interest in Yemen’s future resources – e.g., reportedly negotiating with the PLC to invest in Yemeni oil/gas fields and ports in a post-war scenario. Meanwhile, Emirati companies have quietly become involved in Yemen’s telecommunication sector by proxy (some suspect they have stakes in a new southern telecom company).

In sum, the UAE’s financial engagement has been significant in the south/west (less so in the Houthi-controlled north, which it isolated). It enabled the STC’s rise by bankrolling it  , and it stabilized some local services (for a period) through aid. However, its drawdown in late 2019 left a gap – Aden’s services suffered when UAE left. The UAE’s approach to money is tied to influence: it pays STC fighters and civil servants in areas it influences directly rather than channel via the Yemeni government.

Iran

Iran’s financial and economic role in Yemen has been covert but consequential, primarily through its support to the Houthi movement (Ansar Allah):
	•	Financial support to Houthis: Iran has provided the Houthis with funding, though not through formal budgets or visible aid. Instead, it’s alleged that Iran (via the IRGC Quds Force) orchestrated a regional smuggling network to generate revenue for the Houthis – mainly via oil. For example, the US and UN reported that Iran supplied fuel to a network led by Iranian national Al-Jamal, which sold it and funneled proceeds (some $30+ million) to the Houthis  . This indirect financing has helped the Houthis buy arms and pay fighters. Iran also reportedly delivered cash occasionally (some sources say Hezbollah advisers facilitated turning Iranian rial shipments into Yemeni rials via exchangers). Precise amounts are hard to pin down, but Iran’s support is much smaller in scale than Saudi/UAE aid to the other side, yet crucial to Houthi sustainment.
	•	Subsidized fuel and goods: Beyond cash, Iran has given in-kind economic aid. The Houthis have at times received “subsidized fuel” shipments   – presumably sold at below-market price or on credit – to help their areas’ needs. There were also claims that Iran sent free gasoline and diesel in 2019–2020 (some tankers seized like the Jan 2013 “Jihan-1” had fuel and weapons)  . Iran’s state broadcaster also arranged shipments of medicine and medical supplies to Sana’a (often via Oman as mediator).
	•	Technical assistance on economy: Iran’s formal recognition of the Houthi government came in 2020 by sending an “ambassador” to Sana’a (Hassan Irloo, an IRGC member). With him, reportedly, came Iranian technical advisors to assist on governance including economic management (e.g., advising on how to circumvent sanctions and manage a war economy – skills Iran honed under its own sanctions). For instance, the concept of an e-currency or voucher system the Houthis tried has echoes of Iranian “digital rials” used to dodge banking restrictions.
	•	Diplomatic shielding for Houthi finance: Iran used its influence in international forums to challenge measures that would financially strangle the Houthis. E.g., when the US labeled Houthis terrorists in Jan 2021, Iran loudly criticized it because it would block humanitarian and financial channels  . Iran’s stance likely contributed to Biden’s quick reversal of that designation, thereby keeping some financial flows (remittances, aid) going into Houthi areas.

It’s important to note that Iran’s financial aid, while critical, is not enormous – one estimate by a think tank in 2021 was Iran gives the Houthis tens of millions per year, not beyond that. The Houthis still rely far more on self-financing (taxes, etc.). But Iran’s support was vital in early war years when the Houthi central bank was cut off; it plugged some gaps and kept their war machine oiled (literally and figuratively).

Oman

Oman has played a unique role – not a financier of war, but a facilitator/mediator that has provided financial leniency and quiet aid:
	•	Commercial lifeline for Houthis: Oman maintained trade with Houthi-controlled north even as others cut off. The Oman-Yemen border became a route for goods (and possibly cash/arms) entering Houthi areas. Reports indicate smuggling of Iranian fuel and weapons through Oman’s Mahra border  . Oman likely didn’t officially sanction arms smuggling but its neutral stance meant the border was porous. This supply line had financial impact: it allowed Houthis to get fuel which they taxed and sold – a revenue source. For instance, many of the consumer goods in Sanaa markets during blockade came via Oman truck routes, generating customs income for Houthi authorities.
	•	Safe haven for funds and negotiations: Oman is where many Houthi leaders traveled for diplomacy; presumably, it’s also where they parked some money. If Houthis receive funds in foreign currency (from Iran or donors), Muscat’s banks might hold some for them (given Oman’s friendly neutrality). Oman historically handled some of Yemen’s banking – e.g., after 2016 CBY split, the Aden central bank relocated some international operations to Muscat briefly until they settled in Amman. Oman likely facilitated small-scale financial transfers (like hosting accounts to pay for Houthi delegation travel etc.).
	•	Humanitarian and development aid: Oman quietly contributed to Yemen’s humanitarian response, albeit much less than big donors. It provided for example free medical treatment in Oman for thousands of Yemenis (which reduces Yemen’s health burden). Also, in the severe 2019 fuel crisis, Oman helped broker a deal to allow fuel ships into Hudaydah, easing prices. Oman’s financial role is more in kindness and access than cash: it “offered no-interest loans to Yemeni importers of essential goods via Omani banks” at times (this claim comes from a Sana’a Center piece noting Oman’s potential as financial intermediary).
