In summary, Oman’s value was as a neutral channel – keeping a door open for commerce and communication when others were shut, and that had important economic stabilization effects especially in the north.

Kuwait & Qatar

Both Kuwait and Qatar had smaller but notable roles:
	•	Kuwait – A member of GCC, initially part of coalition but with minimal military involvement. Kuwait hosted peace talks (in 2016)  , offering funds for the dialogue logistics. It’s also been a generous donor to UN humanitarian appeals. Kuwaiti aid often went to health and education projects (Kuwait rebuilt some schools, via Kuwait Relief Society). Post-2017 GCC rift, Kuwait kept ties with all Yemeni sides, possibly giving modest direct aid to north as well. Kuwait didn’t provide budget support like KSA/UAE, but its humanitarian aid 2015–2021 totals in hundreds of millions. It also is home to Yemeni expatriates sending remittances (though smaller community than in Gulf).
	•	Qatar – Was part of coalition 2015 but pulled out in 2017 due to the Saudi-Qatar rift. After that, Qatar became closer to the Oman/Iran position on Yemen, advocating engagement with Houthis. Qatar’s financial role: it provided significant humanitarian aid – by 2021, Qatari charities (Qatar Charity, RAF) were active in Yemen, reportedly spending >$100 million on relief projects. The Qatari government also, some suspect, gave financial support to Islah party-affiliated groups in Taiz and Marib (Islah was in exile in Doha for a period). Qatar’s involvement is politically sensitive – in 2020 the Houthis even claimed Qatar was now supporting them (likely propaganda), but at minimum, Qatar’s exit from coalition removed one source of war funding and rechanneled Qatari money into diplomacy and aid. After the 2021 GCC reconciliation, Qatar pledged funding at UN Yemen events again ($50 m in 2022).

United States, United Kingdom, and EU

The United States, United Kingdom, and European Union (collectively) have primarily shaped Yemen’s financial situation through aid, sanctions, and diplomatic support:
	•	Humanitarian & Development Aid: The US and UK have each been top donors to Yemen’s humanitarian appeals, together contributing billions since 2015. For example, the US provided $3.6 billion in humanitarian aid from 2015 to 2022, including food assistance via USAID/FFP, health and WASH programs  . The UK contributed hundreds of millions (Oxfam and others implementing). The EU (and ECHO) collectively has given about €1 billion in humanitarian aid over the war. This aid has been crucial in injecting foreign exchange and supporting the balance of payments (many aid dollars converted to rials). However, these actors channeled funds via UN and NGOs, explicitly avoiding the Central Bank due to corruption fears . So, while they saved lives, they did not bolster the government’s capacity or reserves much (except indirectly by reducing import needs for food).
	•	Sanctions & Conditionalities: The US and EU have imposed targeted sanctions that impacted financial flows. The US OFAC sanctions on Houthi leaders and entities (like the 2021 designations of the Houthis as Specially Designated Global Terrorists, which was then modified to sanctions on certain individuals) led international banks to de-risk Yemen transactions  . European banks especially became wary of any Yemen-related business. The UK and EU have similarly sanctioned Houthi individuals and affiliated companies – meaning any of their assets abroad are frozen. While these measures aim to pressure the Houthis, they also contributed to Yemen’s isolation from the banking system (foreign banks saw Yemen as too high-risk broadly). On the positive side, the US and EU have issued humanitarian exemptions to ensure aid flows can continue despite sanctions   – a complicated balancing act that required constant adjustments (e.g., General License changes after the brief FTO listing fiasco in 2021).
	•	Diplomatic support for economic stabilization: The US and UK both supported the IMF’s SDR allocation to Yemen in 2021 and lobbied Saudi and others to help Yemen’s economy. They also funded technical assistance: e.g., the UK funded a Development Champions economic forum that produced policy papers and convened Yemeni economists to advise on currency and banking issues  . The EU similarly funded IOM and UNDP development programs (like solar microgrid projects to stimulate local economy). Post-2022, these Western actors have emphasized that any peace deal should include an economic component (e.g. unified CBY, paying salaries) – indicating willingness to provide financial support to underpin a peace. For example, in 2023 the US said it would support a multilateral funding mechanism to pay Yemeni civil servants if a truce extended (this might entail a World Bank trust fund perhaps).
	•	Military expenditure: The US and UK also spent on Yemen in military terms – the US supported Saudi/UAE with logistics (mid-air refueling until 2018, intelligence) which, while not direct spending in Yemen, reflect resources used. The UK continued arms sales to Saudi used in Yemen war (billions of pounds worth), fueling controversy. These military outlays indirectly affected Yemen’s economy by prolonging conflict (and thus humanitarian need).

An notable event: Under President Trump, the US launched “Operation Rough Rider” in March 2025 – a hypothetical described in sources  , entailing large-scale airstrikes on Houthi targets. If that happened (as scenario suggests with 123 civilians killed and FTO re-designation of Houthis  ), it would have huge economic repercussions (tightening blockade, etc.). But since this was an analytical scenario and not actual as of 2025, it underscores the ever-present risk of external military decisions on Yemen’s fragile economy.

Overall, Western actors provided critical humanitarian relief financing but were reluctant to finance the Yemeni government directly, beyond small capacity-building projects. They leveraged sanctions as financial pressure on Houthis, for better or worse. As peace prospects improve, it’s likely the US, UK, EU, World Bank, and IMF will step in more robustly with reconstruction funds and budget support – but tied to governance reforms (e.g., anti-corruption, central bank unification).

International Financial Institutions & UN Agencies

Finally, IFIs and UN agencies themselves were key economic actors:
	•	The IMF: After Yemen’s war began, the IMF had no active lending program (an IMF-supported reform program collapsed in 2014, and they haven’t done another due to the war). But the IMF did deliver the 2021 SDR allocation of ~$665 million to Yemen , which sits as potential foreign asset. The IMF also gave technical aid (they maintain a Sana’a monitoring team albeit limited). The IMF’s presence offers a possible future macro-stabilization program if peace comes – likely requiring major donor funding.
	•	The World Bank: The World Bank shifted to grant-only assistance through the IDA. It innovated by channeling IDA grants via UN agencies to deliver “development” projects even amid war (e.g., an Emergency Cash Transfer project that funded the Social Welfare Fund payments to 1.5 million poor families through UNICEF, and labor-intensive works via UNDP). The Bank by 2022 had ~$2.5 billion in active projects for Yemen (in healthcare, solar, small business support, etc.), effectively substituting for government services. Financially, these projects have pumped money into local economies (in Yemeni rial), mitigating collapse. For instance, the cash transfer program injected $1.1 billion equivalent from 2017-2021  . The World Bank also helped coordinate donor economic support (it hosts the Economic Monitoring Initiative platform since 2021 to track Yemen’s economic trends  ).
	•	UN Agencies: WFP, UNICEF, UNDP, WHO, etc., collectively manage billions of dollars that flow through Yemen’s economy. WFP’s food aid has arguably been Yemen’s largest “import” for years (e.g., in 2019 WFP delivered over 1 million tons of food). This in-kind aid prevented famine but also competed with local farmers and traders, affecting markets. UNICEF and others paying suppliers and staff meant tens of thousands of Yemenis’ incomes actually come from aid agencies (almost a parallel civil service). UNOPS, for example, hires local contractors for solar projects, injecting cash. This humanitarian economy has become a significant part of Yemen’s GNI (~10–15% of GDP each year), with skilled Yemenis gravitating to jobs with NGOs/UN (which pay in USD or at preferential rates). This has stabilized some households but could vanish if aid declines, hence creating risk of an “aid cliff.” The UN also plays mediator on economic issues – the Special Envoy’s office convenes technical talks on things like the “joint mechanism to pay salaries” or manage oil tanker Safer’s salvage (the latter funded by donor $120 m to avert environmental disaster, indirectly saving fishing livelihoods).

In conclusion, international and regional actors have been financial patrons, manipulators, and lifelines for Yemen’s economy. Their money flows – whether aid, deposits, sanctions, or covert funds – have often determined whether Yemenis eat or starve, whether the rial holds or freefalls, and who among Yemen’s factions can sustain their war efforts. The external economic engagement has been double-edged: it alleviated suffering (aid), prevented total collapse (deposits), but also at times deepened Yemen’s dependency and fragmentation (sanctions, funding rival local forces). A future settlement will likely hinge on these actors converting their short-term relief into long-term recovery support – e.g., Saudi and UAE shifting from war spending to reconstruction grants, Iran ceasing illicit flows in exchange for peace, and Western donors coupling relief with development aid to rebuild Yemen’s self-reliance.

Next, we examine the role of domestic business conglomerates and power brokers in Yemen’s war economy, to complete the picture of relevant economic actors.

Domestic Conglomerates and War Economy Elites

Yemen’s private sector has been both victim and participant in the war economy. Large family-owned conglomerates – notably the Hayel Saeed Anam (HSA) Group, but also others like Al-Rowaishan, Thabet, Fahim, Al-Ahmar businesses – have had to navigate conflict realities, sometimes profiting from monopolies or import schemes and at other times struggling to keep operations afloat. This section profiles major domestic economic players, focusing on HSA Group as a case study, and explores their subsidiaries, employment impact, financial networks, and roles in sustaining or mitigating the crisis.

Hayel Saeed Anam (HSA) Group

The HSA Group is Yemen’s largest and most influential business conglomerate – a true empire that spans manufacturing, trade, banking, and more. Founded in 1938 by Hayel Saeed Anam, it’s family-owned (the Saeed Anam family) and prior to the war it reportedly accounted for over 35-40% of Yemen’s non-oil GDP through its various companies  . Key aspects of HSA:
	•	Subsidiaries and sectors: HSA has dozens of companies under its umbrella, prominent ones including:
	•	Yemen Company for Flour Mills & Silos (in Hudaydah and Aden) – major wheat importer/miller.
	•	National Cement Company (in Lahj) – one of Yemen’s few cement plants (vital for construction).
	•	HSA’s manufacturing: e.g., Hayel Saeed Anam Industrial Co. making consumer goods (biscuits, dairy, beverages). They produce staple food brands that Yemenis rely on, like “Shamlan” water, “Sana’a biscuits,” etc.
	•	Imports and Trading: through Rawabi Trading and others, HSA imports sugar, rice, flour, oil in massive quantities, being the largest food importer.
	•	Banking: HSA has stakes in Tadhamon Bank (Islamic) and perhaps indirect stakes in other financial firms. Tadhamon is sometimes even called “the HSA Bank” since it primarily serves HSA and allied business interests.
	•	Retail and services: they have a supermarket chain, a logistics arm, packaging companies, etc.
	•	Overseas ventures: HSA diversified abroad (Malaysia, Egypt, UK) in the 2000s, but Yemen remains core.
	•	Employment: HSA Group pre-war employed around 15,000–20,000 Yemenis (some sources say up to 35,000 if including all affiliates) . This made it arguably Yemen’s largest private employer. Through the war, it continued to pay many employees (though in areas like Hudaydah the 2018 battle disrupted their factories). In terms of supply chain, HSA’s operations support hundreds of thousands of livelihoods indirectly (farmers supplying it, distributors selling its goods, etc.). For instance, by keeping flour mills running, HSA provides work for transporters, bakers and so on.
	•	Role in wartime economy: HSA’s sheer scale made it a central player in war economics. It handled much of the UN food import program (WFP tendered large food orders often won by HSA’s trading arm). It kept its factories going to ensure some domestic production of essentials. However, HSA also came under scrutiny. In 2018–2020, the group benefited heavily from the Saudi $2b deposit LC scheme: the UN Panel reported HSA’s trading arm got 48% of the LCs at preferential rate  . The panel implied HSA might have exploited this to turn a profit (getting cheap dollars and selling food at prevailing prices) . Although the Panel later retracted corruption allegations, it’s acknowledged HSA gained a windfall from that program (at one point making tens of millions in arbitrage). HSA responded that they used the funds appropriately and pointed out they provide food at stable prices . The Sana’a Center recommended HSA should audit its use of the LC mechanism and publish pricing data to ensure transparency  .
	•	Navigating both sides: HSA Group has had to carefully balance relations with both the Houthi authorities and the IRG/coalition. It has companies in Sana’a and others in Aden, and it can’t afford antagonizing either. For example, HSA companies reportedly pay taxes to both governments – essentially double taxation – to keep operating  . In Houthi areas, HSA kept its Hudaydah flour mill running until 2018 war when it got damaged (the ceasefire saved it, and operations resumed after repairs, which was critical for local bread supply). HSA’s leadership engaged with Houthi officials to secure import permits, etc., and likewise lobbied the IRG in Aden for access to the LC facility. This dual engagement shows how conglomerates can act as quasi-diplomats bridging the divide in pursuit of business continuity.
	•	Community role: HSA is known for philanthropy in Yemen – they run Al-Saeed Foundation funding education and health projects, and during the war they stepped up charity (food donations, COVID-19 support to health facilities)  . This softened their image and indeed many Yemenis credit HSA’s factories and imports for preventing absolute scarcity.

Other Conglomerates and Elite Networks:
	•	Al-Rowaishan Group: Another large family business, active in food (they own e.g. Aden Flour Mills separate from HSA) and petroleum distribution. Mentioned in sources where Aden Silos & Mills (Rowaishan-owned) was initially mistaken as part of HSA in the UN report  . That company got $32.5m via the LC mechanism on its own. Al-Rowaishan also have retail and real estate. They had closeness to Saleh’s regime historically. In war, their Aden operations thrived (silos processing grain for WFP, etc.), while their Sana’a side likely struggled or collaborated with Houthi demands. They aren’t as international as HSA, but regionally significant in Aden.
	•	Thabet Brothers Group: Based in Hodeidah, historically they manufactured food (soft drinks, biscuits) and managed the port trucking. War devastated their facilities (2018 fighting on coast damaged a lot). They pivoted – possibly cooperating with Houthi economy e.g. managing port storage for them. Many Thabet factories closed due war but some production resumed under Houthi protection in Hodeidah after ceasefire. They are a pillar of the local economy there (and once partnered with international brands like Coca-Cola).
	•	Yemen Economic Corporation (YECO): Not a private conglomerate, but a military-run business network from Saleh era. Under Houthi control, YECO took over some import channels (like handling food ration cards for Houthi fighters, running some confiscated businesses). It’s essentially a war economy vehicle now – e.g., it opened “thrift stores” selling goods at subsidized rates to families of Houthi martyrs, etc., thereby undercutting private shops sometimes. Financially, Houthi-run YECO has become a large conglomerate of its own (with warehouses, farms, even a bank plan). It’s relevant because it replaced some roles that HSA or others would normally fill – Houthis lean on YECO for procurement to avoid reliance on possibly hostile private companies.
	•	Islah-affiliated businesses: The Islah party (Muslim Brotherhood-aligned) had businesspeople (e.g., Hamid al-Ahmar) who owned businesses like Sabafon (telecom), Al-Trust Bank, etc. The war scattered them: e.g., Sabafon relocated from Sana’a to Aden in 2020 (Houthi authorities replaced it in north by a seized clone “Yemen Telecom”). Hamid al-Ahmar’s bank (Al-Trust) was dissolved by Houthis in 2018. These networks lost ground in Houthi areas but still influence in Marib/hadi government zones. They might be recipients of Qatari or Turkish support quietly, and could re-emerge post-war.
	•	Tariq Saleh & National Resistance’s business wing: On the Red Sea coast, Tariq Saleh (nephew of former President Saleh) leads forces that have carved out an enclave around Mocha. They started “Mocha Hunters (coffee)” as an export brand, revived some port trade. Effectively, an emerging local economic zone backed by UAE funds. They manage revenue from small ports (fuel imports through Mocha out of Houthi purview) – which finances their military and some local services. Not a conglomerate per se, but a warlord economy example.

War Economy Dynamics: Private sector conglomerates often had to engage in the wartime “elite pact” – paying informal levies to armed groups, joining import cartels, etc., to survive. For instance, the UN panel noted by 2018, Decree 75’s restrictions “inadvertently granted large traders privileged access, encouraging monopoly”  . This hints big conglomerates (like HSA, Rowaishan) formed a kind of oligopoly controlling essential imports with tacit political approval, in exchange ensuring supply stability.

Another aspect: capital flight and asset protection – many elites moved capital abroad. The HSA family, for example, already had assets in Dubai, London, etc., and likely moved more during war (the panel mention of HSA needing an audit was partly to quell rumors they moved subsidized dollars out as profit instead of benefiting Yemeni consumers) . Others, like Al-Ahmar family, have substantial holdings outside that they tapped when local business was disrupted.

Economic and Political Impact: These conglomerates employ thousands and produce basic goods, so their fortunes directly affect employment and inflation. When HSA’s flour output dips, bread gets scarce or expensive – a serious issue. Politically, these business elites often mediate between factions – e.g., in 2020 the HSA and other businessmen’s pressure was rumored to push both sides to agree to let fuel ships in to avoid famine (since they’d also lose if famine erupted). In any peace plan, getting these powerful business families on board is vital as they will help rebuild (or could spoil if excluded).

In summary, Yemen’s conglomerates like HSA Group acted as both stabilizers and profiteers in the war economy. They ensured some continuity of supply (preventing total economic free-fall by leveraging their international supply chains), but also adapted to conflict by navigating monopolistic opportunities and deals with de facto authorities. Going forward, they will be engines for recovery – HSA’s manufacturing, for instance, can expand to reduce Yemen’s heavy import dependence – provided a stable environment and inclusive economic policies are in place. The challenge is to harness their capacity for national good while preventing the wartime cronyism and rent-seeking from persisting into the post-war period.

(Sources: HSA profile from Sana’a Center & UN Panel  , Decree 75 impacts  , business war economy behavior from various economic briefs.)

⸻

Having examined Yemen’s financial actors and structures – from central banks to hawala networks to international donors and domestic conglomerates – we now integrate these insights to analyze Yemen’s macroeconomic trends, socio-economic impacts, and prospective paths forward. The final sections will present macroeconomic indicators and their causes, followed by scenarios for the future and policy recommendations to guide Yemen’s financial and economic stabilization and revival.

(The next section covers Macroeconomic Indicators in depth, followed by Risks, Scenarios, and Recommendations.)

Macroeconomic Indicators and Trends

Yemen’s macroeconomy has been devastated by war, fragmenting into two divergent monetary zones with overall drastic declines in output and welfare. The table below presents a snapshot of key indicators, comparing the pre-conflict baseline to the latest available estimates:

Indicator	Pre-War (2014)	Latest (2023–2025)	Notes and Trends
GDP (nominal)	~$43 billion 	~$20–30 billion (2023 est.)  	Economy halved (50% of 2014 level) due to conflict; cumulative output loss ~$126 billion 2015–20  . Non-oil sectors severely contracted.
GDP per capita	~$1,300–1,400	~$500 (2023 est.)	~60–70% drop in per-capita income since 2014 , reflecting both GDP collapse and population growth amid conflict.
Real GDP change	+2.1% (2014) 	2015–2020: –30% cumulated; 2021–2023: modest growth (truce period)	GDP plunged ~30% in 2015 alone. Some stabilization 2021–23 with 2–3% growth in truce years (off a low base), but output remains far below pre-war.
Inflation (annual)	~8–11% (2010–2014)	~30% (2023) ; Peaked ~45% (2021) 	Hyperinflation avoided, but prices soared: overall consumer prices ≈200–300% higher than 2014. In 2022, inflation ~27% and mid-2025 peaked >35%  due to currency swings and global commodity prices. Food prices in 2023 average 60–100% above pre-war .
Exchange Rate (YR per US$)	Official: ~215 YR Market: ~250 YR (2014)	Govt (Aden) Rial: ~1,100–1,350 YR (2023–25)  Houthi (Sana’a) Rial: ~550–600 YR 	Single currency split into two. Southern rial depreciated ~500% (2014–2023) ; hit ~YR 1,700 in late 2021 before recovering with aid  . Northern rial stable by fiat ~600 YR (older notes) . A >2:1 exchange rate gap persists, causing arbitrage and transaction costs  .
Foreign Reserves	~$5 billion (Dec 2014) 	~$0.3 billion (usable, 2023) 	CBY-Aden’s reserves were largely depleted by 2020 . Saudi deposits and IMF SDRs boosted reported reserves to ~$1.8 billion, but <20% is liquid  . Sana’a’s CBY holds no FX reserves (lost access in 2016).
Public Debt	~49% of GDP (2014)	>85–90% of GDP (2022 est.)	Domestic debt soared as government borrowed from CBY and banks (much of it in arrears). External debt (mostly long-term concessional) stayed around $7–8 billion, but debt/GDP spiked with GDP shrinkage. Most debt service is suspended.
Fiscal Balance	–8% of GDP (deficit, 2014)	–15–20% of GDP (avg. 2016–2022)	Oil revenues collapsed and tax collection split. IRG’s deficit financed by money-printing ; Houthi areas run deficits covered by forced borrowing (unpaid salaries, etc.). No unified budget since 2014.
Poverty Rate	~45–50%  	74–80% (2022)  	Poverty roughly doubled. An estimated 80% of Yemenis now live below poverty line , up from ~50% pre-war. Some 13 million people face acute food insecurity despite aid  .
Unemployment	~13% (open unemployment, 2014)  (actual underemployment ~30%)	~32% (est. 2022)   (youth >50%)	Precise data unavailable; surveys indicate massive job losses in public sector (1.2 million salaries stopped)  and private collapse. Most employment now informal or humanitarian. Youth unemployment very high, many into fighting or migration.
Remittances	~$3.3 billion (2014) (21% of GDP) 	~$3.0 billion (2021)   (largest FX source)	Remittances peaked at ~$3.8 b in 2018, then dipped ~20% in 2020 due COVID . Still a vital lifeline (exceeding aid and exports), propping up household incomes and foreign exchange supply. Heavily from GCC (Saudi).
Export Earnings	~$7.3 billion (2014) (incl. $5 b oil)  	~$0.5–1.5 billion (2022)  	Oil exports halted Oct 2022 by Houthi attacks . Non-oil exports (fish, coffee) remain minimal. Yemen now runs a huge trade deficit financed by aid and remittances.
Humanitarian Need	~15 million people (2014 needed aid) 	21.6 million need assistance   (2023); 5 million on brink of famine	Conflict drove one of world’s worst crises: two-thirds of the population require aid . Malnutrition is widespread and development indicators (HDI rank fell from 160 to 183) collapsed  . Aid (≈$4 b/year at peak) became an economic pillar, equivalent to 20–30% of GDP in some years.

Sources: Pre-war data from World Bank & Yemeni authorities  . Latest figures from IMF, World Bank, UN agencies as compiled in Sana’a Center briefs   . All estimates subject to uncertainty given fragmented data collection.

Macroeconomic trends: The war economy is marked by massive output loss, surging prices, fiscal collapse, and humanitarian dependence. Yemen’s GDP shrank by roughly half, a reflection of shattered infrastructure (oil production fell from ~130k barrels/day in 2014 to <40k by 2023), capital flight, and disruption of commerce. Inflation skyrocketed especially in 2016–2018 and 2021 when currency crises hit – eroding purchasing power and plunging most Yemenis into poverty  . The dual exchange rate system introduced steep regional price divergences: for example, as of 2023 a food basket cost in Aden was about double the cost in Sana’a (in rial terms)   before recent southern currency gains narrowed the gap. Government finances splintered – the internationally recognized government could barely collect revenue (losing Houthi-controlled customs that used to be ~30% of revenue), while the Houthi authorities built a makeshift fiscal system reliant on local taxation (customs, telecom fees, zakat) and external smuggling support  . Both administrations resorted to printing money or commandeering resources to finance wartime expenses  , fueling further currency weakness.

On the external front, exports have virtually collapsed – Yemen’s once-modest oil exports (which funded 60–70% of the state budget pre-war) have been largely off-line since 2015, with a brief partial restart 2018–2021 before being cut again by late 2022  . Imports, however, remained high (Yemen still imports ~90% of food and fuel) – running ~$5–6 billion annually. The trade deficit thus widened, but has been financed by grants and remittances. Remittances became the backbone of Yemen’s balance of payments – equivalent to 20–25% of GDP in the late 2010s  . When Saudi Arabia expelled some Yemeni workers and COVID hit in 2020, remittances dipped ~$500 million, contributing to a severe foreign exchange crunch . Humanitarian aid, peaking at $4.5 billion in 2019, also injected foreign exchange (aid agencies convert dollars to rials to pay programs), effectively substituting for lost export earnings. As a result, despite economic collapse, Yemen maintained a semblance of external stability thanks to aid and remittance inflows – evidenced by the fact that large-scale famine was averted and the exchange rate, while depreciated, did not spiral into complete hyperinflation (the southern rial even appreciated in 2022–2023 after new support)  .

Socially, the macroeconomic implosion translated into extreme hardship for households. With real incomes down perhaps 70% on average  and prices of essentials 3–5 times higher than pre-war, coping mechanisms were exhausted. Millions have been internally displaced, and unemployment (especially among youth) spiked – many Yemenis joined armed groups or illicit trades simply to earn an income. The public sector – once 1.25 million employees – largely went unpaid in the north (only ~200k still receive partial salaries there) and was only intermittently paid in the south  . This led to collapse in basic services: education and health workers left jobs or relied on incentives from aid projects. The human development index (HDI) rank of Yemen fell from 160 (2014) to 183 (2021)  , one of the lowest in the world. Poverty is near-universal, and inequality has likely widened (those with access to hard currency – traders, war economy elites – thrived, while salary-dependent and rural households suffered most).

In summary, Yemen’s macroeconomic picture 2010–2025 is one of calamity and divergence. The pre-war challenges (high poverty, oil-dependence, weak institutions) were massively compounded by war: GDP halved, inflation soared, the currency and state finances fragmented, and the vast majority of the population was thrown into humanitarian reliance. Yet, a complete economic free-fall was prevented by external assistance and the adaptability of informal economic networks. These trends set the stage for Yemen’s future: either continued fragility and aid dependence, or – with peace and reforms – a chance to gradually recover. The following section will outline possible future scenarios for Yemen’s economy and present policy recommendations to steer it toward stability and inclusive growth.

Future Scenarios and Policy Recommendations

Yemen’s financial trajectory from 2025 onward will depend on political outcomes and policy choices. Below we consider several plausible scenarios – from stalemate to breakthrough – and then offer recommendations to address risks and lay the groundwork for recovery. The goal is to inform decision-makers, donors, and Yemeni stakeholders of the potential paths and pragmatic steps to support a stable and unified financial system.

Potential Scenarios (2025–2030)

Scenario 1: Prolonged Stalemate (“Fragile Status Quo”) – In this scenario, no formal peace deal is reached, but active conflict remains low (similar to 2023–24). Two de facto economic zones persist. The southern rial hovers in the YR 1,000–1,500/US$ range with continued Saudi-Emirati financial drip feeds, while the Houthi-controlled rial stays around YR 600 (with periodic unofficial adjustments)  . Humanitarian aid levels gradually decline as global attention wanes, forcing import dependence back onto precarious local revenues. Economic activity improves slightly in some stable areas (e.g. Aden port throughput rises, Marib’s gas output feeds local industry) but is offset by continued isolation in the north. Living conditions remain dire for most: poverty might even inch up toward 85% as growth barely keeps pace with population. Risk: Without a political settlement, foreign support may dwindle, and a climate or commodity shock (like a drought or global food price spike) could tip this fragile equilibrium into renewed crisis. Opportunity: The prolonged lull could allow incremental economic cooperation – e.g. informal deals to share oil revenue or reopen roads for trade, preventing worst outcomes  . However, in this scenario, Yemen essentially “muddles through” with a fractured, aid-dependent economy and persistent humanitarian need.

Scenario 2: Economic Implosion (Return to Conflict) – This worst-case scenario sees the collapse of the truce and a surge in fighting – perhaps triggered by a breakdown in Saudi-Houthi talks or external shocks (e.g. regional war affecting Yemen). Renewed conflict (airstrikes on ports, fighting around Marib) would likely crash the southern rial past YR 2,000–3,000/US$ as panic sets in and hard currency reserves are diverted to war expenses. Hyperinflation could take hold – the cost of food could double (on top of existing inflation), pushing millions into famine  . The Houthis, under heavier blockade, might officially demonetize the Yemeni rial and fully dollarize or adopt a crypto/IRR proxy, fragmenting the currency entirely. Banks in government areas, already weak, could collapse if there’s a run or if the central bank in Aden is physically threatened (as happened during STC’s 2020 takeover of Aden’s CBY  ). In this scenario, humanitarian operations would be severely hampered by insecurity and sanctions (especially if the US/EU re-designate the Houthis as terrorists, cutting off financial channels – a risk noted if war resumes)  . GDP could contract further by 20–30%; the state effectively bankrupt. Consequence: This would be a bleak spiral – mass starvation, currency worth little more than scrap paper, and Yemen’s economy truly splintering (with local warlords or councils issuing their own currencies or barter systems in isolated regions). This scenario must be averted through preventive diplomacy and economic support to maintain the current calm.

Scenario 3: Gradual Recovery under a Peace Deal – In an optimistic scenario, a comprehensive peace agreement or political settlement is reached by 2025–2026. This enables the re-unification of key economic institutions (notably the Central Bank) and the resumption of oil exports and international budget support. Confidence returns: the Yemeni rial could appreciate significantly – possibly converging to a single rate around YR 500–700/US$ in the first year of peace as trust builds and hard currency inflows increase  . With ports fully open and insurance costs down, import prices fall, leading to a period of deflation or low inflation. Donors and IFIs step in with a Marshall Plan: e.g. Saudi/UAE convert wartime aid into a multi-billion reconstruction fund, the World Bank and GCC invest in infrastructure, and the IMF supports the balance of payments with a program. Under this scenario, real GDP could rebound strongly – growing 15–25% cumulatively in the first 2–3 post-war years (common in post-conflict rebounds) as idle capacity comes back online and trade resumes  . Public finances would slowly recover too: a unified government can collect customs nationwide (tripling customs revenue by regaining Hodeidah’s ~30–40 billion YR/year and land ports) and negotiate debt relief. Unemployment would gradually ease as reconstruction projects create jobs and Yemeni labor migration picks up (a peace dividend might be Gulf states offering special work visas, boosting remittances). Social conditions improve markedly – by 2030 poverty could retreat toward pre-war ~50% if growth and aid are sustained. Requirement: This rosy outcome hinges on robust and sustained international engagement and sound governance. The main risk is political relapse or corruption undermining the recovery. But with the war over, Yemen’s untapped resources (oil, gas, fisheries, a young workforce) and strategic trade position could fuel a modest economic renaissance  .

Scenario 4: Partial Economic Truce (“Economic Confidence-Building”) – In this intermediary scenario, even without a full political settlement, the parties agree on specific economic measures for mutual benefit. For example, they could form a joint technical committee under UN auspices to coordinate central bank policies, manage a common exchange-rate regime, or at least share data – stopping short of formal reunification but easing fragmentation  . They might implement a nationwide civil servant salary payment mechanism – e.g. using oil export revenues escrowed in a third-country bank to pay salaries in both north and south  . Additionally, agreement on re-opening main roads and Taiz trade routes would significantly lower food and fuel transport costs, reducing prices. In such a scenario, even absent full peace, the economy would get a boost: confidence in the rial would improve (perhaps Aden and Sana’a rates converge somewhat, say to 800–900), and markets in both zones could revive with better connectivity. It’s essentially a “cold peace” economically – less optimal than full peace, but better than stalemate. Growth might be modest (5% a year) but positive, and humanitarian needs would slowly recede as people regain income sources. Caveat: This scenario may be unstable if not cemented by a broader deal – economic cooperation could collapse if political or military tensions flare. Still, it offers a pathway to build trust: successful joint management of, say, a central bank coordination office or salaries fund would build confidence for a fuller settlement  .

In all scenarios, Yemen’s underlying vulnerabilities (water scarcity, climate shocks, historical underdevelopment) remain. The difference lies in whether policies and support can mitigate those or whether conflict amplifies them. Crucially, moving from a negative trajectory (Scenarios 1–2) to a positive one (3–4) will require deliberate policy actions by Yemenis and strong backing from international partners.

Policy Recommendations for Stabilization and Recovery

Regardless of which scenario unfolds, certain policy measures are paramount to stabilize Yemen’s financial system and set the stage for recovery. The following recommendations are evidence-based steps distilled from analyses by Yemeni experts, international financial institutions, and lessons from comparable post-conflict economies:

1. Re-unify or at least Coordinate Central Banking Authorities: Ending the monetary split is the linchpin of economic stabilization. As the Development Champions Forum stressed in 2018, a functioning and unified Central Bank is essential for Yemen’s recovery  . Thus, priority should be given to reconciling the CBY-Aden and CBY-Sana’a operations. In practice, this could mean forming a single technocratic central bank board with balanced representation (perhaps under neutral international supervision initially) and re-establishing one currency regime  . Even before full unity, a joint coordination mechanism (regular data exchange, synchronized interest rate and reserve requirement decisions, etc.) should be instituted  . International actors (IMF, World Bank) can facilitate this by providing technical assistance and convening dialogue. A unified or closely coordinated CBY can then restore nationwide money supply management, clear arrears between the two branches, and begin to regain the confidence of businesses and donors as a steward of the economy.

2. Restore Currency Stability through Transparent, One-Market Policies: The authorities must end the “dual exchange rate” situation by eliminating preferential or unofficial rates and merging auctions. This means discontinuing practices like favoring select importers with below-market rates (which fueled allegations of corruption in the $2b deposit program)  . Instead, adopt a single, market-clearing exchange rate – e.g. via unified FX auctions or interbank trading – so that businesses and humanitarians face a predictable rate. The UN Panel emphasized how mismanaged FX can erode trust  ; hence, all exchange transactions (including for food and fuel imports) should be published and at prevailing rates  . In parallel, limit monetary financing of the deficit (stop excessive rial printing) to rein in inflation  . With adequate donor support, the aim should be to gradually appreciate and then peg the Yemeni rial at a stable rate – restoring it as a credible store of value. Public communication and transparency – e.g. CBY publishing monthly reserves, intervention amounts, and an audited financial statement   – will help rebuild confidence among citizens who have understandably turned to dollars and Saudi riyals.

3. Provide Emergency Liquidity and Capital Support to the Banking Sector: Yemen’s banks, as detailed, are cash-starved and burdened with bad assets (frozen loans and government debt)  . A post-conflict financial plan must include a Bank Recapitalization and Recovery Program. Donors and IFIs, possibly through a multi-donor trust fund, should offer conditional capital injections or credit lines to viable banks – conditional on those banks restructuring governance and lending practices. The central bank should also consider establishing a temporary Liquidity Support Facility that can provide banks with hard currency liquidity against their foreign assets or loan portfolios (with donor guarantees) to enable them to meet withdrawal demands  . In tandem, set up a deposit insurance scheme (even if initially funded by donors) to reassure depositors that their money is safe  . Restructuring or liquidating insolvent banks is tough but necessary – e.g. merger of weak banks with stronger ones, under CBY oversight, to streamline the sector. These steps, along with restoring inter-bank payment systems (e.g. reviving SWIFT connections for Yemeni banks and lifting restrictions on correspondent accounts) will help re-integrate Yemen’s banks with the international financial system, reducing the need for risky hawala channels over the long term.

4. Anchor Expectations with a Credible Currency Board or Hard Peg: To break the cycle of depreciation and inflation, Yemen could consider moving toward a currency board arrangement or hard peg once unification occurs – essentially fixing the Yemeni rial to a major currency at a set rate and backing every rial in circulation with foreign reserves. This approach was suggested by Yemeni economists in 2018 as a way to import monetary credibility . It would require substantial upfront reserves (which donors could help provide) and strict discipline (no printing money beyond reserves). But it would immediately stabilize prices and allow longer-term planning. Alternatively, a managed peg within a narrow band could be adopted with IMF support, similar to arrangements in Jordan or Lebanon historically. The immediate measure, however, is to abolish the multiple exchange rates and unify at close to market rate, then hold that rate steady by mobilizing external funds (e.g. using the remaining SDRs as collateral to secure swaps with the IMF or friendly central banks)  . Exchange-rate stability is vital for reviving investment and preventing further humanitarian deterioration  .

5. Mobilize Oil/Gas and Aid Resources for a National Salary Payment Program: A core driver of Yemen’s crisis was the halt of public salaries to over a million people  . As a priority, the government (or a transitional joint fund) should resume regular salary payments to civil servants across Yemen – especially in health, education, electricity, and water sectors to reboot services. To finance this, Yemen will need external help: for instance, channeling a portion of any restored oil and gas export revenues into a ring-fenced “Salary Fund”, supplemented by donor budget support  . Saudi Arabia and others have signaled willingness to pay salaries as part of a deal – this must be seized and institutionalized (e.g. managed by the World Bank or UNDP initially to assure transparency). Paying salaries not only alleviates humanitarian needs (each salary supports an extended family, often) but also injects spending power into the economy, stimulating local markets. Additionally, re-activate the Social Welfare Fund (cash transfers to the poorest) nationwide   – which the World Bank already did via UNICEF on an emergency basis – and turn it into a permanent safety net, with donor financing for the next few years. These measures will reduce extreme poverty and can help tide the population over as the economy transitions from relief to recovery mode.

6. Encourage Remittances and Labor Mobility: With remittances proving Yemen’s lifeline  , policies should aim to boost them. The Yemeni government and partners should negotiate with Saudi Arabia and Gulf states to expand legal labor opportunities for Yemenis (e.g. special labor quotas or visa schemes). Each additional Yemeni worker in Saudi sending money home directly improves Yemen’s external position and household welfare. Also, remove any barriers or fees on remittance inflows: the central bank should ensure remittance transfers can be received at the market exchange rate without forced conversion at a lower official rate (a mistake that in some crises cut off remittances – to be avoided)  . Investing in modern remittance infrastructure – possibly with Gulf Union support – can reduce transaction costs so families receive more of what is sent. As a complementary measure, facilitate diaspora investment by offering diaspora bonds or investment guarantees – Yemen’s diaspora is sizable and could contribute capital if given secure channels (some diaspora funds have stayed abroad due to war risk; policies to attract them back could aid reconstruction financing).

7. Strengthen Food Security via Domestic Production and Smart Subsidies: Yemen will remain import-dependent, but it can mitigate vulnerability. First, rebuild the agricultural sector through grants, tools, and irrigation projects – employing cash-for-work to terrace fields, rehabilitate canals, and distribute drought-resistant seeds. This can create rural jobs and raise local food output (even modest gains in sorghum, vegetables, and fisheries can improve nutrition and reduce import bills)  . Second, reform the fuel and food subsidy system: during the war, fuel subsidies in government areas were cut causing price jumps  , while Houthi authorities kept some subsidies but via opaque rationing. A unified approach should target subsidies only to essential items for the most vulnerable – e.g. reintroduce a small subsidy on cooking gas and diesel for farms to spur production, funded by donor or GCC grant. Any generalized subsidy (like pre-2014 fuel subsidies) should be avoided as fiscally unsustainable  . Instead, use smart-card or voucher systems to deliver subsistence goods to needy households (building on WFP’s experience with vouchers). Over time, as stability returns, the focus can shift from humanitarian distribution to market support – e.g. a strategic grain reserve to buffer wheat supply and a regulated pricing mechanism to prevent price gouging on staples  . This will help ensure that even if global prices spike or local harvests fail, Yemenis can afford basic food, thus avoiding the famine-like conditions seen at the height of the war.

8. Attract External Financing and Investment through Confidence-Building Measures: Yemen will need massive external financing for reconstruction – far beyond the government’s own resources. To attract this, it must rebuild credibility of financial governance. Key steps include: conducting independent audits of key institutions (Central Bank, large public enterprises) and publishing the results  ; prosecuting egregious corruption cases from the war (to send a zero-impunity signal); establishing a robust ACU (Anti-Corruption Authority) with renewed mandate, as recommended by multiple studies  ; and strengthening public financial management systems with help from the IMF and World Bank (so donors are confident funds will be used correctly). On the legal front, conclude agreements to clear arrears with international creditors and seek debt relief – Yemen’s public external debt is mostly to funds like the Arab Monetary Fund and World Bank, who will likely forgive or reschedule much of it if a reform program is in place. For private sector investment, Yemen should set up special economic zones or one-stop investment windows especially in relatively secure areas like Aden, Mukalla, and Marib to attract regional investors (e.g. port development by DP World, tourism projects on Socotra, renewable energy farms by Gulf investors). Critical to this is improving security and rule of law – investors need assurance contracts will be honored and assets protected. Yemen’s reconstruction funding can also be partly sourced via the diaspora and Gulf philanthropists if confidence is restored through these governance measures  .

9. Support the Private Sector – Especially MSMEs – and Diversify the Economy: Yemen’s private sector (beyond a few conglomerates) has been decimated; reviving it is key for job creation. Policies should focus on micro, small, and medium enterprises (MSMEs) that generate the bulk of employment. This entails improving access to finance – e.g., donors can expand microfinance credit guarantees and the central bank can incentivize banks to lend to MSMEs (perhaps by subsidizing interest or guaranteeing a portion of SME loans)  . Additionally, cut red tape: simplify business registration and customs procedures (possibly digitize them) to reduce the informal economy and encourage entrepreneurs. Promising sectors for diversification – beyond oil/gas – include: fisheries (with Yemen’s 2,000 km coastline, investment in fish processing and cold storage can boost exports and coastal jobs); renewable energy (solar microgrid assembly and installation, given severe electricity deficits); telecommunications and IT (the young population could engage in IT services with proper training and connectivity – already some Yemeni freelancers work remotely for global clients). Investing in human capital is crucial: even during the conflict, Yemen produced many university graduates who lack opportunities. A coordinated effort with donor support can create internship and apprenticeship programs to integrate youth into the workforce and stem brain drain. Over time, building a more diversified economy will increase resilience – so shocks like oil blockade or aid cuts do not tank the entire system  . The government should also partner with the big conglomerates (HSA, etc.) not only to ensure they operate transparently (as per Recommendation 2) but also to leverage their distribution networks for economy-wide benefit – for instance, using HSA’s logistics to help local farmers reach markets at fair prices, rather than just entrenching monopolies  .

10. Strengthen and Regulate the Microfinance Sector and Informal Financial Networks: As detailed, microfinance banks and exchange companies became Yemen’s financial backbone during the war  . This positive development (financial inclusion jumped from <10% to an estimated 20%+ of adults through mobile wallets and microfinance accounts) should be reinforced. However, regulatory capacity must catch up: the central bank (once unified) should create a dedicated Microfinance Supervisory Unit to oversee the dozens of microfinance banks, enforcing prudent lending practices and consumer protection  . A temporary moratorium on new microfinance bank licenses (as suggested by Yemeni experts ) could be imposed until existing ones are stable – instead encourage consolidation where too many small players exist. At the same time, integrate microfinance into the broader financial sector: e.g., establish a national credit registry that includes micro-loans, so borrowers’ credit histories carry over as they “graduate” to larger loans  . For the hawala networks, formalize and license them without over-regulating out of existence. Yemen should develop a national payment switch that links banks, microfinance institutions, and mobile money providers, allowing interoperability (so a customer can transfer from a Kuraimi mobile wallet to a Tadhamon bank account, for instance). This will reduce costs and improve transparency of transactions  . International partners can support by providing know-how on fintech regulation and anti-money laundering safeguards – ensuring that as these networks formalize, they comply with global standards (which will prevent future sanctions risk and encourage correspondent banks to reconnect)  . Overall, the goal is to retain the inclusivity gains of the wartime shift to microfinance/hawala, while curbing the risks (poor oversight, potential illicit finance) and linking these networks to the formal economy’s revival.

11. Pursue Prudent Fiscal Management and Revenue Sharing Arrangements: Post-conflict Yemen will continue to face budget deficits, but sound fiscal policy is crucial for stability. Recommendations include: reinstate a unified national budget process – even if resources are scant, having one budget enhances accountability and planning. Implement expenditure controls and cash management so that scarce funds prioritize essential services and salary payments (perhaps with IFI technical assistance to the Finance Ministry)  . On the revenue side, Yemen must broaden its tax base beyond oil: rebuild customs offices and modernize tax administration (there is a Yemen Customs Authority modernization plan ready to roll out once war ends, focusing on valuation databases and cross-border coordination). Introduce or raise consumption taxes on luxury goods (e.g. reintroduce a sales tax/VAT which was 5% before – could be raised gradually to 10%) and sin taxes (on tobacco, qat) to raise domestic revenue – though enforcement will take time. Revenue-sharing will be a political necessity: for instance, agreement that southern oil/gas revenues partly fund northern salary payments, while northern customs revenues partly fund southern development projects, to ensure no region feels cheated  . Such an interim fiscal federalism can help keep the peace. International donors can incentivize this by tying aid to cooperation – e.g., offering budget support only if a transparent revenue-sharing mechanism is in place. Lastly, avoid rebuilding a bloated patronage public sector: instead of simply re-hiring militias as soldiers or expanding bureaucracy, focus on efficient service delivery and use savings for reconstruction and social support. Yemen’s pre-war wage bill was unsustainable  ; a peace economy should gradually shift spending from wages and fuel subsidies to capital investment and human development.

12. Embed Anti-Corruption and Good Governance Reforms in All Reconstruction Efforts: Corruption and weak institutions plagued Yemen pre-war and worsened during conflict (as wartime reports on diversion of funds show ). A successful economic recovery hinges on improving governance. Concrete measures: empower independent oversight bodies – e.g., re-staff the Supreme National Authority for Combating Corruption (SNACC) with respected technocrats and give it prosecutorial teeth. Implement e-government solutions to reduce petty corruption (for instance, e-procurement for government contracts and digital payroll to ghost-check rolls). Work with civil society to revive budget transparency – Yemen should resume publishing budgets, auditing reports, and allowing public input, aligning with initiatives like the IMF’s Enhanced Economic Monitoring  . The UN Panel of Experts recommendations on financial transparency (e.g., auditing the CBY’s use of foreign funds, auditing large food import deals) must be taken seriously by the new government  . Donors should make significant aid for government budget support conditional on meeting key governance benchmarks (for example, “we will fund X salaries for a year, if biometric payroll verification and an independent audit of the wage bill are completed”). Yemen’s leadership, for its part, must show symbolic moves too – such as prosecuting high-profile war profiteers or recovering stolen funds parked abroad (perhaps with World Bank Stolen Asset Recovery assistance). These steps are not just moral imperatives; they are practical prerequisites to unlock the massive external financing Yemen needs  . Without donor and investor confidence, none of the other recommendations (currency stabilization, investment, etc.) can fully succeed. As one analysis noted, “Donor confidence hinges on credible, impartial management of aid funds… demonstrating responsible use of prior support can unlock additional aid”  . Fighting corruption and bolstering rule of law will thus be foundational to Yemen’s economic renaissance.

⸻

Conclusion: Yemen’s financial and economic evolution from 2010 through 2025 has been one of wrenching disintegration, but also of adaptation and survival. The war shattered formal institutions, but informal systems stepped in; external actors’ money sometimes fed war, yet also prevented famine. As Yemen stands at a crossroads in late 2025, the choices made now – by Yemenis and the international community – will determine whether the coming decade is one of continued fragmentation and fragility, or one of gradual healing and growth. The analysis in this report underscores that technical fixes alone are insufficient without peace and good governance. Reuniting the central bank, rebuilding trust in the currency, paying teachers and doctors again – these are as much confidence-building measures as economic ones. Conversely, securing a peace dividend will require tangible economic improvements in people’s lives.

The recommendations outlined – from institutional reform to targeted humanitarian and development interventions – provide a roadmap for steering Yemen onto a path of stability and inclusive recovery. Implementing them will not be easy in a country still deeply divided and resource-scarce. Yet, Yemen’s people have shown remarkable resilience; given a conducive environment, local entrepreneurs, farmers, bankers, and workers are ready to rebuild their country. International partners must continue and intensify support in this critical transitional period, coordinating relief and development efforts and conditioning assistance on transparency and effectiveness. With sustained commitment, Yemen can move away from the war economy – where hunger and survival dominated – toward a revived economy that offers jobs, public services, and hope for its citizens. The coming years will be decisive. It is our hope that the comprehensive insights and measures provided in this report will help guide policymakers and stakeholders toward decisions that put Yemen on the trajectory of peace and prosperity that its people so desperately need and deserve.

(End of Report)

 

Report)

 Yemen Economic Compass Platform: A–Z Implementation Blueprint

1. Platform Narrative and Architecture

Platform Vision: The Yemen Economic Compass is envisioned as a flagship digital platform providing a comprehensive, bilingual window into Yemen’s economy and financial conditions. As a product of Causeway Financial & Tech Solutions (under Causeway Foundation), its mission is to guide policymakers, researchers, and the public through reliable data and analysis – effectively a “compass” for navigating Yemen’s complex economic landscape. The platform contextualizes Yemen’s ongoing crisis (civil war since 2015, institutional split, humanitarian emergency) with world-class data visualization and expert insights, helping bridge the information gap in an accessible way.

Context: Yemen’s economy has been shattered by conflict. Since 2015, real GDP has contracted by over 40% and GDP per capita by ~58% . The country is effectively split into two economic zones, each with its own central bank and currency regime . Inflation has surged (peaking above 35% in 2025 in government-held areas ), while most of the population has fallen into poverty (estimates of 74–80% by 2024 ). Infrastructure is devastated and critical indicators (food security, exchange rates, revenues) are in constant flux. In this fragile context, stakeholders need a unified, up-to-date platform to monitor economic trends, access credible data, and understand the policy environment.

Platform Narrative: Yemen Economic Compass will tell the story of Yemen’s economy through interactive data and narrative content. It combines real-time indicators (e.g. GDP, inflation, exchange rates, budget figures) with in-depth analysis (chronologies of economic events, sectoral reports, stakeholder profiles). The narrative tone is factual and policy-oriented, rooted in evidence and sourced data. Each data point or claim is transparently cited to authentic sources like World Bank, IMF, UN agencies, etc., reinforcing credibility. The platform emphasizes the duality of Yemen’s economy – e.g. showcasing the divergence between areas under the internationally-recognized government (IRG) and Houthi-controlled regions – while also highlighting overall national trends and humanitarian impacts. By presenting both big-picture summaries and the ability to drill down into details, the platform caters to both general audiences and specialists.

Architecture Overview: The platform’s architecture is modular and robust, comprising:
	•	Front-End Web Application: A responsive bilingual website (English and Arabic) that is the primary user interface. It will use modern web standards (HTML5/CSS3/JavaScript) and a mobile-first approach to ensure accessibility on low bandwidth and mobile devices (important given Yemen’s ~17.7% internet penetration and heavy mobile usage ). The front-end renders content pages and interactive dashboards, with language toggle support and graceful handling of right-to-left (RTL) layouts for Arabic.
	•

(Sources for context: World Bank reports and IMF statements confirm Yemen’s GDP collapse and the two-central-bank reality  , while IMF data shows inflation above 35% in 2025 . Humanitarian data indicate extreme poverty (~80%) . These underscore the need for the Yemen Economic Compass platform.)

2. Detailed Page Contents (English & Arabic)

The Yemen Economic Compass site will contain multiple pages/sections, each available in English and Arabic. Below, we outline the content of every major page in both languages. (All Arabic text is translated from the English original unless specified otherwise, ensuring consistent messaging.) The content balances factual data, explanatory context, and interactive elements. Short paragraphs and bullet points are used for clarity, per best practices, and key figures are cited or sourced in-page.

Home Page (الرئيسية)

English:
The Home Page provides a snapshot of Yemen’s economic situation at a glance, along with navigation to detailed sections. At the top, a banner displays the platform’s name and tagline: “Yemen Economic Compass – Guiding You Through Yemen’s Economy”. A brief introduction paragraph welcomes users:

“Welcome to the Yemen Economic Compass, an open data platform offering real-time economic indicators, analysis, and insights on Yemen’s economy. Explore interactive dashboards, expert commentary, and the latest updates to understand Yemen’s financial landscape and humanitarian context.”

Beneath the intro, key highlight metrics are presented in an eye-catching way (e.g. a grid of cards or infographics): for example, GDP (latest estimate), Inflation rate, Exchange rate (IRG vs Houthi), Poverty rate, and Population. Each metric is accompanied by a short description or trend indicator. For instance: “Inflation: 35% year-on-year (Jul 2025, IRG areas) – elevated due to currency depreciation ” or “Exchange Rates: 1 US$ = ~YR 1,700 (government areas) vs YR 550 (Houthi areas) – reflecting the dual currency system”. These figures update as new data comes in.

Next, a news ticker or highlights section can feature recent developments: e.g. “Saudi Arabia deposits $300m into Yemen’s Central Bank – October 2025”, “Fuel prices cut in Aden by 20% after subsidy reforms – Sep 2025”, or “UN: 17 million Yemenis face food insecurity .” Each headline might link to a relevant analysis or source. This keeps the homepage fresh with current events.

The home page also includes a “Featured Analysis” snippet: a short excerpt from the latest in-depth report or blog (for example, a summary of the Quarterly Economic Monitor or a special analysis piece). A “Read more” button invites users to continue to that full article.

At the bottom, clear navigation tiles or buttons lead to the main sections: Data Dashboard, Timeline, Stakeholders, and About. Each is represented with an icon (e.g. a chart icon for Data, a calendar/clock for Timeline, people icons for Stakeholders). A language switch toggle (EN/AR) is visible in the header for easy switching.

Finally, the footer provides contact info, an attribution to Causeway Foundation (“A project by Causeway Financial & Tech Solutions under Causeway Foundation”), and logos of data partners or sources (World Bank, IMF, etc., as small icons), emphasizing the collaborative and credible nature of the platform.

Arabic (العربية):
الصفحة الرئيسية تقدم نظرة سريعة على الوضع الاقتصادي في اليمن مع روابط إلى الأقسام التفصيلية. في الأعلى يظهر شعار المنصة وشعارها: «البوصلة الاقتصادية لليمن – دليلك لفهم اقتصاد اليمن». يلي ذلك فقرة ترحيبية موجزة تعرف بالموقع:

«مرحبًا بكم في منصة البوصلة الاقتصادية لليمن، المنصة المفتوحة للبيانات التي تقدم أحدث المؤشرات الاقتصادية والتحليلات حول اقتصاد اليمن. استكشفوا لوحات البيانات التفاعلية وتعليقات الخبراء وآخر المستجدات لفهم المشهد المالي والوضع الإنساني في اليمن.»

تظهر بعد ذلك أبرز الأرقام والمؤشرات الأساسية بشكل بارز (مثلاً في شبكة بطاقات معلومات أو رسوم بيانية مبسطة): مثل الناتج المحلي الإجمالي (أحدث تقدير)، معدل التضخم، سعر الصرف (في مناطق الحكومة vs مناطق أنصار الله)، نسبة الفقر، وعدد السكان. كل مؤشر مرفق بوصف مختصر أو دلالة عن الاتجاه العام. على سبيل المثال: «التضخم: 35% (على أساس سنوي حتى يوليو 2025 في مناطق الحكومة) – مستوى مرتفع نتيجة انخفاض قيمة العملة » أو «سعر الصرف: 1 دولار أمريكي ≈ 1,700 ريال (مناطق الحكومة) مقابل 550 ريال (مناطق أنصار الله) – مما يعكس وجود نظامين نقديين.». تتحدَّث هذه الأرقام تلقائيًا مع تحديث البيانات الجديدة.

ثم يظهر شريط أخبار متحرك أو قسم أبرز المستجدات يسلط الضوء على التطورات الأخيرة: مثل «السعودية تودع 300 مليون دولار في البنك المركزي اليمني – أكتوبر 2025» أو «تخفيض أسعار الوقود في عدن بنسبة 20% بعد إصلاحات الدعم – سبتمبر 2025» أو «الأمم المتحدة: 17 مليون يمني يواجهون انعدام الأمن الغذائي ». كل عنوان يرتبط بتقرير أو مصدر لمزيد من التفاصيل، مما يبقي الصفحة الرئيسية مواكبة لآخر الأحداث.

تتضمن الصفحة الرئيسية أيضًا مقتطفًا موجزًا تحت عنوان «تحليل مميز» لأحدث تقرير متعمق أو مقالة. على سبيل المثال، عرض مختصر لأهم ما جاء في التقرير الاقتصادي الفصلي أو دراسة خاصة، مع زر “اقرأ المزيد” الذي ينقل الزائر إلى المقالة الكاملة.

في أسفل الصفحة، يتم توضيح أقسام الموقع الرئيسية عبر أزرار أو أيقونات واضحة: لوحة البيانات، التسلسل الزمني, أصحاب المصلحة, من نحن. لكل منها رمز تعبيري مميز (مثل رمز مخطط بياني للبيانات، أيقونة تقويم للتسلسل الزمني، أشخاص لأصحاب المصلحة). كما يتوفر زر تبديل اللغة (ع/En) بشكل واضح في الأعلى لتيسير الانتقال بين اللغتين.

أخيرًا، يتضمن ذيل الصفحة معلومات الاتصال وإشارة إلى تبعية المنصة لمؤسسة كوزواي («هذا المشروع بإشراف كوزواي للحلول المالية والتقنية - إحدى مبادرات مؤسسة كوزواي»). كما يضم شعارات الجهات الشريكة ومصادر البيانات (البنك الدولي، صندوق النقد الدولي، وغيرها) بشكل أيقونات صغيرة، تأكيدًا على موثوقية المصادر وتكامل الجهود.

Yemen Overview Page (نظرة عامة على اقتصاد اليمن)

English:
The Overview page offers a comprehensive narrative summary of Yemen’s economy, providing context and background for the data. It is structured in a few short sections with subheadings for clarity:
	•	Current Economic Landscape: This section describes Yemen’s war-torn economy in plain language. For example: “Yemen’s economy has been devastated by years of conflict and fragmentation. Since 2015, GDP has shrunk to roughly half its pre-war level , and per capita income fell from ~$1,400 in 2014 to under $450 in 2024  – making Yemen one of the world’s poorest countries. Critical infrastructure is damaged, and two rival governments (in Aden and Sana’a) now operate separate central banks and policies, leading to a split monetary system.” We note that the country’s humanitarian crisis (e.g. 17+ million food insecure) intertwines with the economic collapse. A sentence highlights the dual economic zones: “The north (Houthi-controlled) and south (IRG-controlled) have separate currencies, causing divergent inflation and price levels .”
	•	Key Challenges: A bullet list (3–5 points) enumerates core economic challenges:
	•	Fragmented Institutions: e.g. “Two central banks and conflicting fiscal policies hinder nationwide monetary stability.”
	•	Fiscal Crisis: “Collapse of oil exports and revenue has left government finances in deficit (public revenue fell from ~22% of GDP in 2014 to <12% in 2024 ). The IRG government relies on foreign aid and money printing to pay expenses.”
	•	Inflation & Currency Depreciation: “Rapid currency depreciation in government areas (the rial fell from ~YER 250/$ in 2015 to ~YER 2500/$ at its worst ) drove up prices, while Houthi areas enforce a fixed rate causing liquidity crunches.”
	•	Humanitarian Impact: “Economic collapse exacerbates suffering – over 80% of Yemenis live in poverty , and unemployment and business disruptions have caused widespread hardship.”
	•	Recent Developments: A short paragraph on the latest positive or negative trends (updated periodically). For instance: “2023–2025: Despite a U.N.-brokered truce in 2022, Yemen’s economy continued to contract by ~1.5% in 2023 . In 2023, the IRG took bold measures: establishing an imports financing committee and cracking down on speculative exchange trading, which helped the southern rial appreciate ~40%  . Inflation in Aden slowed by late 2025 as the currency stabilized. However, in Houthi-controlled areas, strict control of currency supply kept official inflation low but suppressed liquidity. Fuel and food prices remain far higher than pre-war, especially in the south despite some relief in 2025.” This section cites data or reports (e.g. IMF Article IV or World Bank Economic Monitor) for credibility.
	•	Economic Outlook: A forward-looking note (2-3 sentences) closes the overview. For example: “The outlook for Yemen’s economy remains fragile. International agencies project a modest recovery if peace allows – growth might slowly resume (IMF forecasts ~0.5% in 2025, up to ~2-3% by 2030)  – but this hinges on restoring oil exports and unifying financial institutions. Without a political settlement, Yemen will likely continue to face fiscal crises, a divided currency, and heavy dependence on aid.”

Throughout the overview text, key facts and figures are hyperlinked to their sources (via our citation format or on-hover references), reinforcing trust. The tone is factual and non-partisan. Small infographics may be embedded – e.g. a mini chart showing GDP decline over 2014–2024, or an icon illustrating the dual-currency split (perhaps two Yemeni rial notes labeled “old” and “new”). These visual cues make the narrative more engaging.

A sidebar on this page (or a callout box) might list “Quick Facts”: population, pre-war vs current GDP, currency names, main exports (oil, coffee, etc.), providing context at a glance. This page essentially sets the stage, so that users can then dive into more detailed pages like the data dashboard or timeline with a solid understanding of the context.

Arabic:
تقدم صفحة النظرة العامة ملخصًا شاملاً باللغة العربية عن اقتصاد اليمن، لتوفير السياق والخلفية الضرورية لفهم البيانات. يتم تنظيم المحتوى في أقسام قصيرة مع عناوين فرعية واضحة، كالتالي:
	•	المشهد الاقتصادي الحالي: يصف هذا القسم بلغة واضحة حال الاقتصاد اليمني الممزق جراء الحرب. على سبيل المثال: «يعاني اقتصاد اليمن من دمار واسع بسبب سنوات من الصراع والتشظي. فمنذ 2015 انخفض الناتج المحلي الإجمالي إلى حوالي نصف مستواه قبل الحرب ، وتراجع دخل الفرد من نحو 1,400 دولار عام 2014 إلى أقل من 450 دولارًا عام 2024  – مما يجعل اليمن من أفقر دول العالم. وقد دُمّرت البنية التحتية الحيوية، وتتنازع حكومتان (في عدن وصنعاء) على إدارة الاقتصاد عبر مصرفين مركزيين وسياسات متعارضة، ما أدى إلى انقسام النظام النقدي.» ونشير إلى أن الأزمة الإنسانية في اليمن (مثل وجود أكثر من 17 مليون مواطن يواجهون انعدام الأمن الغذائي) متداخلة مع الانهيار الاقتصادي. كما نبرز مسألة انقسام الاقتصاد إلى قطاعين: «يعاني الشمال (مناطق أنصار الله) والجنوب (مناطق الحكومة المعترف بها) من عملتين منفصلتين، مما أدى إلى تباين كبير في معدلات التضخم والأسعار بينهما .»
	•	أهم التحديات: قائمة نقطية (3–5 نقاط) تلخّص أبرز التحديات الاقتصادية:
	•	المؤسسات المجزأة: مثلًا: «وجود مصرفين مركزيين وسياسات مالية متضاربة يعيق إرساء استقرار نقدي على مستوى الوطن.»
	•	الأزمة المالية العامة: «انهارت إيرادات الدولة (تراجع إجمالي الإيرادات من ~22% من الناتج في 2014 إلى أقل من 12% في 2024 )، مما أدى إلى عجز مالي كبير. تعتمد الحكومة في عدن على الدعم الخارجي وإصدار النقد لتغطية النفقات.»
	•	التضخم وانخفاض العملة: «أدى الانخفاض الحاد لقيمة الريال في مناطق الحكومة (من ~250 ريال للدولار في 2015 إلى ~2500 ريال في أسوأ حالاته ) إلى ارتفاع جنوني للأسعار، بينما أبقت سلطات صنعاء سعرًا ثابتًا رسميًا ما تسبب في أزمة سيولة.»
	•	التداعيات الإنسانية: «فاقم الانهيار الاقتصادي المعاناة – إذ يعيش أكثر من 80% من اليمنيين تحت خط الفقر ، وتسببت البطالة وتعطل الأعمال في مصاعب واسعة الانتشار.»
	•	التطورات الأخيرة: فقرة قصيرة تسلّط الضوء على أحدث الاتجاهات سلبًا أو إيجابًا (يتم تحديثها دوريًا). مثلًا: «2023–2025: رغم الهدنة التي رعتها الأمم المتحدة في 2022، واصل الاقتصاد اليمني الانكماش بنحو 1.5% في 2023 . وفي 2023، اتخذت الحكومة في عدن خطوات جريئة: إنشاء لجنة وطنية لتنظيم الواردات، وقمع المضاربات في سوق الصرف، مما ساعد الريال الجنوبي على الارتفاع بنحو 40%  . تباطأ التضخم في عدن أواخر 2025 مع استقرار العملة. ومع ذلك، في مناطق أنصار الله، أدى التحكم الصارم بالسيولة إلى إبقاء التضخم الرسمي منخفضًا لكنه تسبب في شُح النقد. ولا تزال أسعار الوقود والغذاء أعلى بكثير من مستويات ما قبل الحرب، خاصة في الجنوب رغم بعض التحسن في 2025.» يستند هذا القسم إلى بيانات أو تقارير (مثل بيان صندوق النقد أو مرصد البنك الدولي) لمزيد من الموثوقية.
	•	الآفاق المستقبلية: نظرة مستقبلية (سطران إلى ثلاثة) في ختام الصفحة. مثلاً: «لا تزال آفاق الاقتصاد اليمني هشة. تشير توقعات المؤسسات الدولية إلى إمكانية انتعاش متواضع في حال إحلال السلام – قد يعود النمو تدريجيًا (يتوقع صندوق النقد نموًا بحوالي 0.5% في 2025 وصولاً إلى ~2–3% بحلول 2030 )، لكن ذلك مشروط باستعادة صادرات النفط وتوحيد المؤسسات المالية. وفي غياب حل سياسي، سيواجه اليمن على الأرجح استمرار الأزمات المالية، وانقسام العملة، واعتمادًا كبيرًا على المساعدات.»

على امتداد نص النظرة العامة، تم ربط الإحصاءات والحقائق الرئيسية بالمصادر الموثوقة باستخدام روابط أو هوامش، لتعزيز المصداقية. الأسلوب موضوعي ومحايد. يمكن تضمين رسوم بيانية صغيرة – مثلاً مخطط يوضح انخفاض الناتج المحلي من 2014 إلى 2024، أو رسم يوضح ازدواج العملة (كصورتين لورقتي ريال: «قديم» و«جديد»). مثل هذه العناصر المرئية تجعل الشرح أكثر جاذبية.

قد تحتوي الصفحة أيضًا على شريط جانبي أو مربع معلومات بعنوان «حقائق سريعة» يضم حقائق أساسية: عدد السكان، حجم الاقتصاد قبل الحرب مقابل اليوم، اسم العملة، أهم الصادرات (كالنفط والبن)، لإعطاء لمحة سريعة. تهدف هذه الصفحة إلى تزويد القارئ بالأساس اللازم من المعلومات العامة، بحيث يمكنه التعمق في صفحات البيانات التفصيلية أو الخط الزمني وهو ملم بسياق الأرقام.

Interactive Data Dashboard (لوحة البيانات التفاعلية)

English:
The Data Dashboard is the heart of the platform’s interactive experience – a one-stop page where users can visualize and explore key economic indicators over time. Upon entering this page, the user sees a set of interactive charts and controls to customize the view. The layout is clean and responsive, stacking vertically on mobile.

At the top, a brief intro text (1-2 sentences) explains how to use the dashboard: e.g. “Explore Yemen’s economic indicators below. Select an indicator from the menu, adjust the time range, or compare multiple metrics. Hover over charts for exact values. Data sources: World Bank, IMF, Yemen Central Bank, etc.” The page then features several sections:
	•	Key Indicators Selector: A dropdown or tabs that let the user pick from categories like “Output & Growth”, “Prices & Inflation”, “Currency & Money”, “Public Finance”, “External Sector”, etc. By default, the dashboard might load a “Key Indicators Overview” which contains a few pre-selected major charts (GDP, inflation, exchange rate) for a quick glance. Users can then dive into each category.
	•	Dynamic Charts: For example, if the user selects “Prices & Inflation”, the dashboard might display a line chart of inflation rate over time (with separate lines for IRG vs Houthi areas if data allows, differentiated by color or dashed lines). If “Currency” is selected, a chart shows exchange rates – e.g. Yemeni rial vs USD in Aden and in Sana’a (official vs parallel rates), illustrating the post-2016 divergence . Each chart has a clear title, labeled axes (in English or Arabic as appropriate), and a legend. Hovering on a point shows the exact value and date (e.g. “July 2025 – IRG CPI +35% ”). The charts are interactive: users can toggle series on/off (for example, hide the Houthi inflation line, or show only one series), and if applicable, zoom into a specific period by dragging or using a time slider.
	•	Indicator Cards/Table: Below the charts or to the side, key current values may be summarized in text or a small table. For instance: “Inflation (annual): 27% (2024), 35% (Jul 2025) ; Exchange Rate: YR 1,630/$ (Nov 2025 in Aden), YR 600/$ (fixed in Sana’a); Real GDP Growth: –1.5% (2024) .” This provides at-a-glance figures alongside the visualization.
	•	Comparison and Filters: The dashboard might allow comparing two indicators. For example, a user could check a box to overlay oil production vs GDP or food price index vs exchange rate to see correlations. We can implement this by pre-defining some useful combinations (e.g. oil output and government revenue, or money supply and inflation). Additionally, if data by region or city is available (say, price indices in different cities), a filter could let the user choose a region to display its data. Initially, given data limitations, we focus on national-level or dual-zone data.
	•	Data Download and Source Info: Beneath each chart, a small caption notes the source (e.g. “Source: Central Bank of Yemen and WFP, via World Bank ”) and last update date. There is also an option (button) to download the data in CSV format for that chart, catering to researchers who want to do their own analysis. This transparency and openness align with best practices in open-data platforms.
	•	Technical notes (perhaps collapsible): This section explains any definitions (e.g. “Inflation is year-on-year change in consumer price index. In Houthi-controlled areas, official CPI is based on a fixed exchange rate, which may understate true inflation.”), and any caveats about the data (like missing data points during conflict years, or methodology changes). These notes ensure advanced users understand the context of the numbers.

Example of an interactive element: A timeline slider below the charts could allow users to select a custom date range (e.g. 2010–2025 or focus on 2015–2018). Alternatively, preset buttons like 1Y, 5Y, Max can be provided. When the user adjusts the range, the charts smoothly update (using the JavaScript chart library’s capabilities).

User Scenario: A policymaker using the dashboard can, for instance, select “Public Finance” and see a chart of Government Revenue vs Expenditure (% of GDP) from 2010–2025. The chart might show revenues plummeting after 2015 and a widening deficit until recent Saudi grants. Hovering over 2024 might show “2024: Revenue ~6% of GDP, Expenditure ~8% of GDP  ”. If they want to see how foreign reserves changed, they switch to External Sector category to see reserves over time, etc. The intuitive design means even a non-expert can glean insights (e.g. “the line for GDP drops sharply around 2015, coinciding with war onset”), while experts can get precise numbers.

Overall, the Data Dashboard page is highly interactive, bilingual, and informative, encouraging users to engage with Yemen’s economic data directly. It adheres to data visualization best practices – clear legends, color distinctions, and accessible color choices (color-blind-friendly palettes avoiding confusing red/green combinations , and distinct marker shapes or patterns for lines representing different zones). The Arabic version mirrors the same functionality, with charts labeled in Arabic and the interface elements (dropdowns, buttons) translated or flipped for RTL as needed.

Arabic:
