	•	Use short paragraphs (3-5 sentences) as per user instruction – achieved via CSS that can add margin between paragraphs, making scanning easier.
	•	Text Alignment: English text is left-aligned, Arabic text right-aligned. We ensure that numeric data in Arabic context is still understandable (we might keep numbers in Western digits if that’s how our data is, or use the Arabic-Indic digits if decided – we can lean toward Western digits for consistency across languages, many Arab publications do that especially for data).
	•	Directional UI elements: We will flip interface elements for RTL – e.g., the menu hamburger, arrow icons, timeline progression, etc., should mirror when in Arabic mode.

5.3 Layout and Navigation
	•	Responsive Layout: We’ll use a grid system (likely CSS Flexbox/Grid or a framework like Bootstrap which supports RTL easily). On desktop, a typical page might have a two-column layout (main content + sidebar for additional info or interactive widgets). On mobile, these stack vertically.
	•	Header & Menu: A top navigation bar with the site logo on the left (right for Arabic) and the menu links (Home, Data, Timeline, Stakeholders, About) horizontally. On smaller screens, collapse to a menu icon. Include a language switch button in the top-right (flag or “EN/AR” toggle). Possibly include a search icon in the header as well.
	•	Footer: Contains the Causeway Foundation info, contact email, maybe quick links, and partner logos. Use a dark background (perhaps the deep blue) with white text for contrast. Make sure to include copyright (©2025 Causeway Foundation).
	•	Homepage Layout: Typically a banner (with maybe a relevant image backdrop – maybe a subtle map of Yemen or a photo of Aden port, but with color overlay to not distract). Then a three-column section for key metrics (which collapse to rows on mobile). Followed by two-column sections for latest news & featured analysis (on desktop). Then a full-width call-to-action to explore the data dashboard.
	•	Content Pages Layout: Use plenty of subheadings (as we have in the blueprint) to break content. Possibly incorporate cards or callout boxes for important insights or quotes. For example, an “Insight” might be styled in a tinted box with italic text to stand out (as seen in timeline content).
	•	Lists and Tables: Use bullet and numbered lists wherever possible for clarity (the guidelines we follow). Style lists with clear bullet markers (perhaps a custom icon like a small arrow or a simple circle). Tables, if any, should be simple, with alternating row shading. Ensure tables can scroll horizontally on small screens (with a scroll hint).
	•	Interactive Elements (charts, etc.): They should ideally expand to full width of the container for maximum clarity. On desktop maybe 50% width if side by side comparisons, but generally charts need space. Provide controls in a clean way (for instance, place dropdowns above the chart aligned left for EN, right for AR). Use consistent styling for form elements (matching our color scheme – e.g. blue outline on focus).
	•	Hover and Active States: All interactive elements (buttons, links) should have hover states to indicate interactivity. For example, links underlined on hover, buttons slightly darken or lighten. For Arabic, ensure the hover effect works the same (CSS will naturally apply, just consider RTL placement if icons in buttons).
	•	Icons: Use a consistent icon set (like Font Awesome or Material Icons) for things like external link (to show if a link goes to an external site), download symbol, email, etc. They must support mirroring if direction-specific (Material Icons has RTL support for directional icons).

5.4 Accessibility and Readability
	•	Ensure high contrast between text and background (aim for WCAG AA at least: for instance, our chosen blue on white has a good contrast, and text on that blue should probably be white or a very light color).
	•	Provide alternative text for images (like if we embed an infographic or photo).
	•	Make sure the site can be navigated by keyboard (especially the interactive charts – we might provide a table of data as an alternative or at least ensure tabbing through chart points is possible, which is tricky; at minimum, key data points are also described in text).
	•	Use ARIA labels for interactive controls (e.g. the language toggle should announce “Switch language to Arabic” or vice versa).
	•	For color usage in graphs, accompany with direct labels or patterns so users don’t rely solely on color. E.g., labeling lines directly with text, or different stroke styles (dashed vs solid) for IRG vs Houthi lines in charts.
	•	The bilingual nature itself is an accessibility feature for Arabic speakers who are not comfortable in English – we treat Arabic content with equal quality and ensure it’s not an afterthought. That means proper word choices (we might use a professional tone; our example translations reflect formal Modern Standard Arabic suited to reports).
	•	Loading performance (which affects usability): optimize images (use compressed formats, no huge image files; likely only a few small images like logos and maybe one header image). Use lazy-loading for images below the fold. Minimize use of heavy scripts – combine and defer JS files where possible. We want pages to load reasonably even in Yemen’s slow internet contexts (17% penetration often on 3G networks).
	•	Possibly utilize a CDN (like Cloudflare) to serve static assets quickly in region (discussed in hosting section).
	•	We also consider using local caching for data: e.g. once the data JSON is loaded for charts, store in localStorage so if user navigates away and back, it doesn’t re-fetch immediately.

5.5 Interactivity Standards
	•	Follow principles of intuitive design:
	•	Every interactive element (button, dropdown) should have a label or icon that clearly indicates its function. E.g., a download icon with tooltip “Download data (CSV)” on hover.
	•	Provide instructions where needed (“hover over the chart to see values” could be a faint text below chart).
	•	Do not surprise the user with new windows unnecessarily – external links can open new tabs but internal navigation stays in same tab.
	•	Ensure consistency: If one chart’s dropdown is styled a certain way, all dropdowns follow the same style. If footnotes appear as hover tooltips in one page, do similarly elsewhere.
	•	Language toggle should persist the current page if possible (for example, if on the Timeline page in English and switch to Arabic, ideally it goes to the Arabic Timeline, not just reload home. This requires linking between translated pages in CMS, which we will do).
	•	SEO-friendly design: Use proper HTML tags for content structure (h1 for main title per page, h2 for sections, etc., which we have in blueprint). This also helps screen readers. We will also include meta tags and descriptive page titles (like “Yemen Economic Compass – Overview” etc., covered in SEO section).
	•	Testing on Browsers: We will test UI on modern browsers (Chrome, Firefox, Safari, Edge) and ensure the RTL CSS is correctly applied (there are plugins like rtlcss that can auto-generate an RTL stylesheet from LTR one if we go that route with minor adjustments).

5.6 Example Style Highlights
	•	Homepage Style: Possibly include a relevant photo backdrop (e.g. a skyline of Sana’a or a port in Aden) but heavily overlay it with blue filter so that white text tagline is readable. Use an elegant font or slight variant for the tagline to give a distinctive branding feel (maybe a serif or cursive for just the tagline if it fits brand persona). But keep most text in sans-serif.
	•	Charts Style: Use the site’s color palette for charts: e.g. primary series in blue, second series in orange, third in teal, etc., which also happen to be colorblind friendly combinations if chosen well. Keep chart backgrounds white for clarity. Axis lines gray. Provide ample margin so labels aren’t cut off.
	•	Bullet graphs or icons: We can use small custom icons in the stakeholder section, e.g., a small flag icon for country actors, a bank icon for central bank, etc., to visually cue the content type. Ensure they are subtle and not distracting.
	•	Hover Cards: If we implement hover definitions (like if you hover over an acronym PLC, we might show “Presidential Leadership Council”), use a subtle tooltip style (dark background, small white text, slight drop shadow).
	•	Language integration: For bilingual display if ever needed (some platforms show both languages side by side – we are not doing that because it’s a toggle system, but just to note if any charts needed bilingual labeling simultaneously, we’d avoid that complexity by separate instances).

By adhering to these design guidelines, the platform will not only be aesthetically pleasing and aligned with Causeway’s brand, but also highly usable for the target audience. It will look on par with international economic portals—clear information hierarchy, pleasing color contrast, and thoughtful interactions—while being mindful of Yemen’s context (RTL and possibly low bandwidth users).

(For inspiration, we consider sites like the World Bank Yemen page which uses blue/white and simple layout , and FocusEconomics which uses clear sections and charts. We also heed data visualization UX principles such as those by Nielsen Norman Group and datawrapper’s blog on colorblind-safe palettes .)

6. Technical Implementation Guide (Hosting, CMS, Framework, Performance)

This section provides a roadmap for the technical implementation, covering decisions on hosting infrastructure, the content management system (CMS) or framework to use, steps for performance optimization, and overall deployment architecture. The aim is to ensure the platform is secure, scalable, and maintainable by a small team (a site supervisor and two developers) and delivers fast, reliable access to users.

6.1 Platform Stack Overview

After evaluating needs, we recommend using WordPress as the CMS for content management combined with custom interactive components for data visualization. This leverages WordPress’s strength in bilingual content management (with plugins) and ease of use for editors, while allowing us to embed custom scripts for charts and tools. WordPress is widely supported (powering ~43% of websites ) and our team can quickly find solutions or plugins for common needs, reducing development time.
	•	CMS: WordPress (PHP, MySQL) – as it allows easy creation of pages (Home, Overview, etc.) and posts if needed (for blog-like updates or reports). We will use the latest stable version (likely 6.x). WordPress also has a large ecosystem of plugins for SEO, caching, security which we can utilize (see section 10 for specifics).
	•	Theme/Framework: We can start with a lightweight base theme or framework like Underscores or GeneratePress, then build a custom child theme for our design. Alternatively, use a modern headless approach (WordPress as backend only and a React frontend) – but given our small team and need for quick delivery, a classic WordPress theme with some custom templates is faster and sufficient. We will however incorporate modern build tools (Sass for CSS, possibly Webpack for bundling JS) to keep code organized.
	•	Custom Code: We will write custom JavaScript for the interactive dashboard and other tools, possibly bundling with the theme or as a separate plugin. We might package all chart code as a WP plugin called “Yemen Economic Compass Charts” so it can be managed separately. This plugin could provide shortcodes like [yec_chart indicator="inflation"] which outputs the canvas and script for that chart. This modular approach helps separate content (in pages) from code.
	•	Database: WordPress uses MySQL/MariaDB, which will store all textual content and site settings. For our numeric data, we have options:
	•	Store as JSON files (which could live in the theme directory or an uploads folder). E.g., an indicators.json file. This is simple and decoupled, but updating it programmatically needs file access.
	•	Store in custom DB tables (like wp_yec_data) for series. This allows use of WP’s database functions and perhaps an admin UI to edit data if needed.
	•	Given data might also be updated by scripts outside WP, a JSON file approach is okay. But a custom table with an admin editor (maybe using Advanced Custom Fields or a bespoke interface) could be more user-friendly if the site supervisor will update numbers manually sometimes.
	•	We can start with JSON for static data and gradually move to a custom table if manual editing is required frequently.

6.2 Hosting Setup

We plan to host the site on a reliable cloud server or managed hosting:
	•	Given the audience is likely international and local, we need global reach. A cloud provider like Amazon Web Services (AWS) or DigitalOcean can be used. For instance, an EC2 or Lightsail instance with a LAMP stack (Linux, Apache, MySQL, PHP) for WordPress. Alternatively, a managed WordPress host like SiteGround or WP Engine for ease (they handle updates, caching).
	•	The site is not expected to have extremely high traffic initially (targeting maybe hundreds or low thousands of users a month, including researchers, NGOs, etc.), so a single server with decent specs (2 vCPU, 4GB RAM) should suffice. We must ensure capacity for traffic spikes if, say, a report gets widely cited.
	•	Location: If our main user base is Yemen/MENA, a datacenter in Europe (e.g., Frankfurt or London) or Middle East (some providers have Bahrain/UAE regions) could reduce latency. Alternatively, use a CDN.
	•	Domain: Secure a domain name, e.g. yemeneconomiccompass.org (or .com). We will set up DNS (possibly through Cloudflare which can also provide CDN and WAF).
	•	HTTPS: Obtain an SSL certificate. Let’s Encrypt offers free SSL which can be auto-renewed. All site traffic will be forced over HTTPS to ensure security and SEO benefit.

6.3 Deployment and Development Workflow
	•	We will maintain a version control repository (e.g. GitHub or GitLab) for the code (theme files, custom plugin code). The two developers can collaborate via Git.
	•	Development will happen on a local environment or staging server. Once tested, changes are pushed to production. Possibly set up deployment via SSH or use a CI/CD pipeline (though might be overkill for a small WP site).
	•	We’ll have at least two environments: staging (for testing new features, accessible to team) and production (live site). Data updates can be tested on staging to ensure charts work with new data.
	•	Backups: Implement daily automatic backups of the database (content) and weekly of the whole site (files+db). If using a managed host, they often provide this. Otherwise, use a plugin like UpdraftPlus or script to backup to cloud storage (S3, etc.).
	•	We plan for the site to be launched by [target date], and ensure the team is trained in how to use the CMS (adding a news update, editing a page, etc.).

6.4 Bilingual Content Management
	•	Use a robust multilingual plugin such as WPML or Polylang. WPML (WordPress Multilingual) is popular and will allow us to create Arabic translations of each page and post, managing them as linked entities. It also handles string translation (like interface text, widget text).
	•	We’ll structure the site so that the Arabic version is on a subdirectory or subdomain (e.g., yemeneconomiccompass.org/ar/overview). WPML can do different structures; subdirectory is SEO-friendly. This requires some server rewrite setup but WPML handles it.
	•	The plugin will allow translators or bilingual editors to input the Arabic content side-by-side with English. We must be careful with any hardcoded text in the theme (we’ll make them translatable strings using WP’s gettext functions so WPML can translate those too).
	•	We will test the Arabic pages thoroughly to fix any RTL layout issues (WPML itself doesn’t handle styling; we ensure our CSS covers [dir="rtl"] contexts, or load a separate rtl.css if needed).

6.5 Performance Optimization

Performance is crucial given possibly slow internet of some users:
	•	Caching: Utilize both server-side caching and client-side caching.
	•	Install a WP caching plugin (e.g. WP Super Cache or W3 Total Cache) to generate static HTML of pages for anonymous users, so PHP/database isn’t hit on each request. This drastically speeds up delivery and reduces server load.
	•	Object caching (Memcached/Redis) can be considered for dynamic parts if needed (like if many logged-in editorial users, not likely an issue).
	•	CDN: Use a Content Delivery Network to serve static assets (images, CSS, JS). Cloudflare can be a free and easy solution, acting as a proxy and caching static content at edge locations globally. This will help Yemeni and international users alike.
	•	Minification & Bundling: We’ll minify CSS and JS. Possibly combine multiple JS files into one to reduce HTTP requests (but we also use HTTP/2 which handles parallel requests better, so bundling is less critical but still good to do). Ensure we exclude jQuery if not needed or load it in footer to not block rendering (WordPress usually loads it; we might avoid heavy reliance on jQuery to keep things fast).
	•	Image Optimization: Any images (like maybe a couple on home or icons) will be optimized (using tools like TinyPNG). Use appropriate formats (PNG for logos, JPEG for photographs, possibly SVG for logos/illustrations for crisp scaling). We might use inline SVG for the compass icon for resolution independence.
	•	Lazy Loading: Use modern loading="lazy" attribute for images below fold (WordPress does this by default now for post images). For charts, perhaps don’t render all charts on dashboard until the user selects them or scrolls to them (if many charts).
	•	Database Optimization: Ensure the MySQL DB is tuned (use InnoDB, proper indexing especially if we add custom tables for data). But volume is low, so not an issue.
	•	Testing: Use Google PageSpeed Insights or GTmetrix to test site speed. Aim for a good score (green) on desktop and mobile. Might need to fine-tune (e.g. deferring any third-party scripts, ensuring CSS is not bloated, enabling Gzip compression on server).

6.6 Security Hardening
	•	Server Security: Keep the OS updated (if unmanaged server, the team needs to apply updates or use a service to manage). Use UFW/iptables to only allow necessary ports (80, 443, maybe 22 locked to certain IPs). Possibly use a cloud firewall. Fail2Ban to block suspicious repeated login attempts.
	•	WordPress Security:
	•	Change the default login URL (with a plugin or custom) to prevent widespread bot hits on /wp-admin.
	•	Enforce strong passwords for all accounts (especially admin/editor accounts). Possibly use 2FA for admin logins (plugins available).
	•	Limit login attempts (many security plugins do this) to mitigate brute force.
	•	Install a security plugin like Wordfence or iThemes Security to monitor and firewall malicious traffic (Wordfence also blocks common exploit patterns).
	•	Keep WordPress core, plugins, and themes updated. (The site supervisor or developers should check monthly or enable auto-updates for minor versions).
	•	Use least-privilege principles: only give admin role to necessary person, editors for content people, etc. Remove any default accounts (like “admin”) if present.
	•	HTTPS and SSL: Already mentioned, all traffic encrypted. Also ensure we disable old protocols (only TLS1.2 and above, for instance).
	•	Back-end access: Use SSH/SFTP for server access instead of FTP. Possibly set up a VPN if remote DB access is needed or phpMyAdmin behind password if used.
	•	Data validation: Our custom code (e.g., the form for the newsletter, search, etc.) will be coded with security in mind (sanitize inputs, use WordPress’s built-in escaping for output to avoid XSS, etc.).
	•	Monitoring: Implement some uptime monitoring (Pingdom or UptimeRobot) to alert if site goes down. Also consider logging unusual activity (if possible, integrate with a service or at least keep server logs to review).
	•	The site doesn’t handle sensitive user data except maybe emails for newsletter, which we’ll protect and not share. We will have a privacy policy stating usage of data.

6.7 CMS/Framework Justification

We considered alternatives:
	•	A custom static site or static site generator (like Jekyll or Hugo) with embedded JS for charts. That would be extremely fast and secure (since no database), but editing content would be code-based (not ideal for non-technical editors) and building a bilingual static workflow is complex. We choose WordPress for user-friendliness.
	•	A headless CMS with a front-end in React could have given a snappier single-page app feel, but complexity is higher and for this project timeline and team, it might be overkill.
	•	WordPress with WPML is a proven solution for bilingual NGO sites (for example, many UN sites use WP with multi-language).
	•	Performance can be made good with caching, so WP’s dynamic nature isn’t a big drawback if optimized.

6.8 Deployment Steps Summarized
	1.	Development Phase: Set up local LAMP environment. Install WP, theme, essential plugins. Develop the custom theme according to design (HTML/CSS from scratch or using starter theme). Integrate chart library and test sample charts with dummy data.
	2.	Content Entry: Input the English content from blueprint into pages (Home, Overview, etc.). Use WPML to create Arabic versions and input the translated content (which we have prepared). Check formatting (especially lists, citations).
	3.	Data Integration: Write scripts to fetch actual data from sources and populate JSON or DB. Test charts with real data. Adjust any outliers or formatting (e.g. ensure thousand separators or rounding of values if needed for display).
	4.	Testing: On staging, do cross-browser testing, mobile test, and language toggle test. Check that citations (the 【†】 style references) are properly handled – we might actually implement them as footnotes or tooltips manually since WP won’t automatically handle that format. Possibly we’ll replace those placeholders with a tooltip script or link to a sources section (to maintain exactly that format might require a custom filter).
	5.	Optimization: Install caching, optimize assets, run speed tests. Optimize any slow queries (though likely none if mostly static after cache).
	6.	Security & SEO: Set up SEO plugin (Yoast) to add meta tags (we’ll input meta descriptions for each page in English and Arabic). Create a sitemap.xml (Yoast can do this). Add site to Google Search Console and Bing. For security, implement steps above.
	7.	Launch: Move to production domain, ensure DNS propagation with minimal downtime, enforce HTTPS. Announce or share with relevant networks (foundation PR).
	8.	Maintenance Plan: Document how to update data (e.g. running the Python script monthly, or a manual entry interface for staff). Schedule content updates (maybe plan that each quarter an analysis post will be added, etc.). Ensure the team knows how to revert site from backup if needed, etc.

By following this technical blueprint, we ensure a smooth implementation that the developers can follow step-by-step, and the site supervisor can oversee with understanding of each part’s purpose. The stack chosen is also familiar to many developers, which mitigates risk and ensures long-term maintainability.

(We cite WordPress’s dominance  as rationale for using it, and rely on standard AWS/Cloudflare documentation for best practices on hosting and security. The practices here also align with OWASP recommendations for web security and Google’s guidelines for performance and SEO.)

7. Admin and Editorial Workflows

A clear workflow for content updates and data management is crucial for the platform’s sustainability. This section outlines how the site supervisor and editors will create, approve, and publish content (in both languages), as well as how data updates and maintenance tasks will be handled. We define roles and step-by-step procedures to ensure smooth operation.

7.1 User Roles and Permissions

We will set up the following WordPress user roles, aligning responsibilities:
	•	Administrator: Likely the site supervisor (and possibly lead developer initially). Has full control: managing plugins, site settings, user accounts, as well as content. They’ll oversee technical updates and high-level content approvals.
	•	Editor (English): Responsible for English content creation and updates. Can publish pages/posts, upload media, etc., but not change site-wide settings. For example, adding a new analysis post or editing the overview text when needed.
	•	Editor (Arabic)/Translator: Responsible for Arabic content. They will create or edit the Arabic version of each page or post. This role ensures translations maintain accuracy and cultural appropriateness.
	•	Analyst/Contributor: (Optional) If we have a subject-matter expert who writes draft reports or data analysis but should not publish directly. They can create content drafts (in English, for instance) which an Editor then reviews and publishes. Similarly, an Arabic contributor could draft translations.
	•	Subscriber: For any general users if they sign up for newsletters (though newsletter likely handled outside WP user system with a form).

Each editor only publishes in their language scope to avoid confusion (or they coordinate if bilingual). We can use WPML’s translation workflow: an English Editor publishes a page, then marks it “needs translation”, the Arabic Editor gets a notification to translate.

7.2 Content Creation & Update Workflow

For Static Pages (Overview, Stakeholders, etc.):
	1.	Drafting (English): The English Editor or Administrator drafts new content or updates in WordPress. They use the WP editor to format text, add headings, lists, and citations. We’ll instruct them to preserve the citation format; possibly we implement a custom shortcode for citations (like [cite]source info[/cite]) to unify styling.
	2.	Review (English): The site supervisor (Admin) reviews the content for accuracy, clarity, and that citations are included for new data/facts. They ensure tone aligns with objective style.
	3.	Publishing (English): The Editor publishes or updates the page. If it’s a major update, they might leave it unpublished until Arabic is ready, depending on policy (but likely we publish English then follow with Arabic as soon as possible).
	4.	Translation (Arabic): The Arabic Editor gets a notification (WPML can send an email or at least mark the page as needing translation). They open the English content in translation mode, where WPML shows each text segment to translate. They enter the Arabic text accordingly, making sure formatting matches (lists as lists, bold, etc.).
	5.	Review (Arabic): If possible, a second Arabic-proficient person (maybe the site supervisor if bilingual or an external reviewer) can proofread the Arabic content to ensure it conveys the meaning correctly and is free of typos.
	6.	Publishing (Arabic): The Arabic Editor publishes the translated page. Now the site’s both language versions are updated.
	7.	Verification: Check the front-end in both languages to ensure layout hasn’t broken (especially if Arabic text length differs significantly or if any inline numbers need adjusting).

For News/Updates or Reports (if the site has a blog-like section):
	•	The process is similar: an English Contributor drafts a post (e.g. “Quarterly Economic Update – Q1 2026”). Editor reviews and publishes it in English under a category like “Analysis” or “Updates”. Then translator produces Arabic version (perhaps a summary if full translation is too time-critical, to be decided by editorial policy).
	•	Each such post will show author and date to maintain credibility.

We’ll maintain a content calendar (maybe internally) for expected updates:
	•	e.g. “Monthly: update exchange rate and inflation figures on Overview metrics.”
	•	“Quarterly: publish a brief economic update post.”
	•	“Ad-hoc: when major events happen (e.g. a new Saudi deposit or a peace deal affecting economy), write a short analysis or at least update key numbers.”

7.3 Data Update Workflow

Many data series update on set schedules (monthly, yearly). The workflow:
	1.	Data Fetch or Input: On the first week of each month, the Analyst or Developer runs the data update script (or triggers it if automated via cron). This fetches new values (e.g. November inflation, latest exchange rate, etc.) and updates the JSON/DB.
	•	If any source is manual (like IMF released a new report PDF), the Analyst will input the figures into our data storage manually (perhaps via a custom admin form or by editing a config file).
	•	For instance, if the World Bank releases a new GDP estimate for 2024, the Analyst will update the GDP series accordingly.
	2.	Verification: After data update, the team opens the site’s Data Dashboard to ensure the new points are showing correctly and look plausible (no chart breakage or wild data error). Possibly compare with source to double-check (especially if a script might mis-parse something).
	3.	Annotation: If a major change happened (like inflation suddenly halved due to base effect, or GDP revised), the team might add a short note in the Overview narrative to explain it. This is an editorial decision.
	4.	Publishing Data: If it’s via JSON that auto-reflects on charts, just confirming the charts are live is enough. If we store data in posts or pages (less likely), ensure to update those. The advantage of our approach is numeric updates don’t require content editing in many cases.
	5.	Logging: We maintain a data update log (maybe a simple spreadsheet or even a private page on WP visible to admins) noting what was updated and when, and by whom. This helps track if something was missed.

7.4 Workflow for Interactive Content and Development Changes

If changes to interactive tools or site features are needed (say, adding a new indicator or a new chart):
	•	The site supervisor and developer discuss the change. The developer implements on staging (following our git workflow).
	•	Testing on staging by supervisor or editor to see if the new feature works and is understandable.
	•	Upon approval, deploy to production during a low-traffic window.
	•	This workflow ensures that the small dev team can iterate improvements while minimizing risk of breaking the live site.

7.5 Quality Control and Approval

We’ll formalize a quick approval process:
	•	For any new content (especially analysis pieces), at least one person aside from the author must review. E.g., the English editor writes it, the supervisor reviews and approves before publish. In WP, content can be saved as Pending Review, and an Admin can then publish it.
	•	For Arabic, because translation is critical, ideally the translator and the original author communicate if any clarification is needed. Possibly have a bilingual person do a final skim (maybe use a tool to back-translate and see if message aligns).
	•	Citations: Editors will ensure every factual claim or statistic added has a citation. We maintain a consistent style (the 【†】 format). We might use a footnotes plugin for ease: enter sources in a list and reference numbers appear in text. If not, we do it manually as we wrote. The site supervisor can do a check that new numbers match their sources.

7.6 Administrative Routines
	•	Weekly:
	•	Check site for any broken links or errors. Could use a link checker plugin to catch broken external links (because a lot of citations might be external).
	•	Review user feedback or inquiries from the contact form.
	•	If any news events happened that might require a content tweak (like central bank announcement), decide if immediate update is warranted or wait for more data.
	•	Monthly:
	•	Perform data updates as per schedule (first of month).
	•	Review analytics (see which pages are most viewed, etc. – this can guide content priorities).
	•	Check for any plugin or core updates; apply in staging, test, then update production (if using managed hosting, often minor updates auto apply).
	•	Quarterly:
	•	Plan and publish a quarter economic summary if not done in monthly smaller bits.
	•	Possibly host a brief team review meeting to plan upcoming content, discuss any user suggestions.
	•	Biannual/Annual:
	•	Larger review of site structure: do we need to add a new section (like if new type of data emerges)?
	•	Check domain SSL expiry (if Let’s Encrypt, it auto-renews, but ensure it’s working).
	•	Evaluate data source relevance – any new sources we should incorporate? (E.g., if Yemen Central Bank reunifies and publishes official stats, incorporate those).
	•	Update any branding or design if needed (maybe a slight refresh or adding features).

7.7 Editorial Style Guidelines

The workflow also includes adhering to style:
	•	Use neutral, fact-focused language. Avoid political bias. The supervisor ensures all content meets this standard.
	•	Maintain consistency in terminology (e.g., call the two authorities IRG and “de facto authorities in Sana’a” consistently, or use “Houthis” in context – define in Stakeholders and then use shorthand).
	•	Write out acronyms on first use in each page (with translation), then use acronyms. The editor should check this on each page.
	•	For Arabic, ensure formal language (no colloquial dialect), use standard economic terms (like “انكماش” for recession, etc.), consistent naming (e.g., use “الحوثيين” vs “أنصار الله” based on decided style or use both to clarify).
	•	Citation placement: if using numeric footnotes, ensure they appear after punctuation, etc. (We might stick to the bracket style, which is fine inline).
	•	The editor will also ensure that any charts or data mentioned in text correspond to the data on the dashboard (so no conflicting numbers).

7.8 Training and Documentation

We will prepare a simple Admin Manual (maybe a PDF or a Confluence page) with how-tos:
	•	How to add a news post.
	•	How to update a number on the homepage (if not automated).
	•	How to run the data update script (with example commands).
	•	How to add a new indicator (e.g., if we later want to show “exchange rate Euro to YER”, how to add that dataset and chart).
	•	Key contacts for support (if developers are external, an arrangement to contact them for big issues or an SLA if one exists).

Given the small team, cross-training is good: the site supervisor should know how to publish content in a pinch, and an editor might be taught how to run a data update if the analyst is away.

This workflow design ensures that the platform remains current and reliable. Content will be updated in a timely manner, and data will never be too stale, maintaining the platform’s credibility as the go-to economic compass. The separation of duties (editor vs analyst vs admin) also provides checks and balances, which is particularly important when the information is sensitive or impactful (e.g., reporting economic declines or improvements).

(We reference typical newsroom or think-tank workflows where an analyst provides data and an editor writes up insights, with a senior review before publication. The multilingual workflow leverages WPML’s proven translation management. By planning these processes, we mitigate issues such as untranslated content lingering or data not matching text, which often plague bilingual sites if not managed.)

8. Naming, Branding, and Identity Strategy

A strong brand identity will help Yemen Economic Compass stand out as a credible and memorable platform. In this section, we outline how the platform’s naming, visual identity, and messaging align with the Causeway Foundation’s brand values, and how we plan to present this identity across the site and related media.

8.1 Platform Name and Tagline
	•	Name: “Yemen Economic Compass” – this name was chosen to convey guidance and direction in the economic context. We will use the full name prominently on the site (in the header, on the About page). In Arabic, we’ll refer to it as “البوصلة الاقتصادية لليمن”. This literal translation (“Economic Compass for Yemen”) preserves the metaphor and clarity.
	•	We might also use a shorter handle in communications: e.g., “Economic Compass” once context is set, or even an acronym “YEC” internally, but public-facing we’ll spell it out for clarity (acronyms are less recognizable).
	•	Tagline: A concise tagline will accompany the name in logos or headers, such as: “Guiding Yemen’s Economic Future” (English) and “دليلك نحو اقتصاد اليمن” (Arabic). Alternatively, “Data & Insight for Yemen’s Economy” depending on what resonates. The tagline emphasizes the platform’s role as a guide and source of insight.

8.2 Logo Design
	•	The logo will combine imagery and text:
	•	Likely a stylized compass icon integrated with a shape of Yemen or a subtle graph line. For instance, the compass needle could point upward (signifying progress) and the compass circle might encase an outline of Yemen or a bar-chart motif.
	•	Color: use the chosen primary color (deep blue) for most of the logo, with an accent (golden or red) for the compass needle or a specific element to draw attention.
	•	We’ll have two versions: one horizontal (icon left, text right) for website header, and one stacked (icon above, text below) for vertical uses or social avatars.
	•	Include both languages in one logo or separate? Possibly the primary logo will be in English (for international audience recognition) with the Arabic name underneath in smaller font. Or we make a bilingual logo (could be busy). Instead, we might create two localized versions: an English logo and an Arabic logo, and the site will display one based on language. That might be cleaner – e.g. English logo: compass icon + “Yemen Economic Compass” text; Arabic logo: same icon mirrored + “البوصلة الاقتصادية لليمن” text.
	•	The logo should also incorporate subtle reference to Causeway Foundation if desired:
	•	Perhaps a small tagline below: “A Causeway Initiative” or show Causeway’s logo in mini next to it. But to keep the Compass brand front and center, we likely will just mention Causeway in text on site (like footer and About page).
	•	The design will be professional and minimal, aligning with causewayimpact.com style (which uses clean sans fonts and simple icons).
	•	We will create a favicon (the small icon in browser tabs) – typically the compass icon alone can serve as favicon.

8.3 Color and Font Usage (Brand Identity)

As detailed in the UI section:
	•	Primary Brand Color: Deep blue (trust, stability). This will appear in the logo, headers, buttons.
	•	Secondary Color: Golden amber (optimism, energy) – used sparingly for highlights or the compass needle etc.
	•	Neutral Palette: White, light gray backgrounds, dark gray text. Clean and corporate.
	•	These colors should also align with Causeway’s brand if possible. If Causeway has a specific blue or green, we can adopt that exact hex code to tie them together.
	•	Typography for branding: likely same fonts as site content for unity. The logo text could use a slightly different font for distinction (maybe a more geometric sans or a font with a slight flair). But often, using the same font family in bold works (Open Sans Bold for name).
	•	Arabic font in branding: ensure the Arabic name in logo is typeset nicely, possibly adjusting kashida or diacritics if needed for visual harmony.

8.4 Causeway Foundation Alignment

We emphasize the platform as an extension of Causeway’s mission. Causeway Foundation’s values (based on causewayimpact.com content) include bridging finance and impact, transparency, empowerment:
	•	We integrate those values by:
	•	Messaging: In About page and communications, note that this platform is about “merging financial rigor with social impact”, resonating with how causewayimpact says “merging financial rigor with measurable impact” . Our phrasing might be: “Led by financial experts and development practitioners, Yemen Economic Compass merges data-driven analysis with a mission for positive change – reflecting Causeway’s commitment to informed impact.”
	•	Visual cues: Possibly include Causeway’s logo or a mention “Under Causeway Foundation” below the main logo. We’ll use Causeway’s color or stylistic element if available (for example, if Causeway has a signature graphical element, we could incorporate a subtle version in the background or in the footer).
	•	If Causeway’s website is known to our audience, linking the styles will reinforce trust (people see the brand and connect quality).
	•	Brand Pillars: If Causeway has specific thematic pillars (like climate action, equity, etc., as implied on causewayimpact ), this platform clearly falls under an economic development/data transparency pillar. We might label it as such in internal strategy, but externally we focus on the identity of the Compass itself.

8.5 Tone and Voice

The brand voice will be:
	•	Authoritative but Accessible: We speak with the credibility of a research institution, but in plain language where possible. We avoid jargon when addressing general audience, yet provide detailed data for experts. This dual approach is tricky but can be balanced by providing summaries and then deeper dives.
	•	Neutral and Non-partisan: As part of branding, we present as an impartial observer. We don’t take political sides; we objectively report on both IRG and Houthi areas’ data. This is crucial for trust. Our language about stakeholders is factual (as seen in stakeholder profiles).
	•	Empathetic and Purpose-driven: We subtly convey that behind this data is a humanitarian concern – without activism, but with empathy. For example, acknowledging human impact of economic trends (as we do citing poverty, etc.) frames the data in human terms, aligning with causewayfoundation’s humanitarian angle.
	•	Bilingual Professionalism: Both languages will have professional tone. In Arabic, maintain formal tone suitable for policy reports and media consumption. Avoid overly academic phrasing; aim for clarity. The brand should be taken seriously by local economists and officials as well as international experts.

8.6 External Communication and Collateral

We consider how the brand appears outside the website:
	•	Social Media: Possibly create Twitter (X) account like @YemenCompass to share key findings or updates (“Yemen’s rial appreciated 20% after reforms, per Yemen Economic Compass data .”). Use the logo as avatar, consistent color banner.
	•	Reports/PDFs: If the platform ever produces downloadable briefs, we’ll have a Word/PDF template with the logo, colors, and typography to ensure consistency (headers in same font, etc.). Also include Causeway Foundation logo in these as co-brand.
	•	Presentations: If team members present at conferences, have a PowerPoint template with the branding.
	•	Media referencing: When media cite the platform, ensure a standard description is used. For example: “Yemen Economic Compass, a platform by Causeway Foundation, stated that…”. We might prepare a press kit with logo files and an about paragraph.

8.7 Naming Conventions Internally

Within the site’s text:
	•	Use full names on first mention (e.g. Yemen Economic Compass) then can shorten to “the Compass” if context is clear.
	•	In Arabic text, maybe after first mention, refer to it as “المنصة” (the platform) or “البوصلة” (the compass) for variation, but ensure readers always know it’s about the site, not a literal compass.
	•	Emphasize the “compass” metaphor occasionally: e.g., “Our platform serves as a compass to navigate complex economic data.”

8.8 Consistency Across Languages

We ensure the brand identity feels the same in Arabic and English:
	•	The logo’s style and colors identical, just text script differs.
	•	Taglines meaning matched. For instance, “Guiding Yemen’s Economy” vs “دليلك لاقتصاد اليمن” (both convey guidance).
	•	The voice we use in Arabic translations should carry the same tone as English (no over-dramatization or dilution).
	•	We will also consider cultural differences: e.g., in Arabic communications, it might be effective to mention hope for improvement or collective benefit, resonating with cultural narrative of overcoming hardship, which aligns with causeway’s optimistic tone but ensure not to stray into political messaging.

8.9 Intellectual Property and Credits
	•	We should ensure the brand name and logo do not infringe on others. A quick check shows “البوصلة الاقتصادية” might be used by some news sites , but “البوصلة الاقتصادية لليمن” is unique enough. We might consider registering the name if needed, but as a non-profit project, trademark might not be urgent.
	•	We’ll include a footer note: “© 2025 Causeway Foundation – All Rights Reserved.” (or if we want it open data, we might add something about data usage rights, see SEO and terms).
	•	If images are used (like a stock photo in header), ensure licensed properly (maybe use free ones from Unsplash or specific Yemen photos with credit).

In essence, the branding strategy ensures that Yemen Economic Compass is perceived as:
	•	Credible (through professional design and association with Causeway),
	•	Purposeful (through its guiding metaphor and clear mission statement),
	•	Neutral (through balanced language and bipartisan approach),
	•	Aligned with larger goals of transparency and empowerment that Causeway stands for (financial rigor + impact, as on causewayimpact ).

This helps build trust among stakeholders such as international agencies, Yemeni academics, and the public, making them more likely to use and reference the platform. Over time, “Yemen Economic Compass” should become a recognized name in discussions of Yemen’s economy, much like “Yemen Economic Monitor” (WB) or “Sana’a Center reports”, but with its unique niche of live data and bilingual accessibility.

(No direct citations here, as branding content is creative/strategic, but we reflected on causewayimpact’s wording for alignment  to ensure brand consistency.)

9. Security, Analytics, and SEO Specifications

This section details the measures we will implement for web security, analytics tracking, and search engine optimization (SEO). The aim is to protect the platform and its users, gather useful usage data (while respecting privacy), and ensure the site is easily discoverable and ranks well in search results, both English and Arabic.

9.1 Security Specifications

Application Security:
	•	HTTPS Everywhere: The site will enforce HTTPS by default. We will configure an HSTS header so that browsers always default to HTTPS for our domain. No mixed content (all scripts and images also loaded via https). This protects against eavesdropping/tampering .
	•	Content Security Policy (CSP): Implement a Content Security Policy header to restrict allowed sources of content. For example, only allow scripts from our domain and trusted CDNs (and perhaps the specific domains of our data sources if needed). This can mitigate XSS by disallowing inline scripts or foreign scripts. We’ll set Content-Security-Policy: default-src 'self'; img-src 'self' data: https:; script-src 'self' 'unsafe-inline' https://cdn.jsdelivr.net ...; style-src 'self' 'unsafe-inline' ...; (with adjustments as needed during testing).
	•	XSS and Injection: As mentioned, we’ll sanitize all outputs. Using WordPress’s built-in escaping (esc_html, esc_attr etc.) for any dynamic data output. Our custom charts code will get data via safe JSON (which we generate server-side in a controlled manner). No direct eval or dangerous JS. We avoid unsanitized user input processing; the only inputs from users might be search queries or contact form – these will be handled by WP safely (search uses WP query which is sanitized, contact form plugin sanitizes inputs and emails them).
	•	Login Security: Admin login page protected with rename (like example.com/login instead of /wp-login). Use reCAPTCHA or 2FA for login if possible to deter bots. Also, limit attempts as earlier.
	•	User Roles: As in workflows, grant limited roles to content editors. Only one or two Admin accounts. Remove any unused accounts.
	•	Updates: Keep software updated to patch known vulnerabilities. For instance, schedule WordPress core updates (auto-update minor versions, plan major version upgrades with testing). Similarly, update plugins quickly when new versions (the site supervisor or developer monitors security mailing lists or WP notifications).
	•	WAF (Web Application Firewall): Consider using Cloudflare’s free WAF rules or Wordfence’s firewall (blocks common malicious patterns). Cloudflare can block known malicious IPs or countries if needed (though likely we want open access, so we won’t geo-block unless there’s an attack).
	•	Database Security: Use a strong DB password, disallow remote DB access (bind to localhost). Possibly change default WP table prefix to something random to make SQL injection slightly harder to target (security by obscurity minor, but we’ll do it).
	•	File Permissions: Ensure correct file permissions on server: no directory has 777, WP config is not web-writable, etc. Disable file editing from WP admin to prevent any compromised account from injecting code via theme editor.
	•	Backup & Recovery: Because security includes recovery from incidents, we have daily backups as stated. Also, create a plan: if site is defaced or hacked, we have steps to restore backup and close the hole (maybe switch to maintenance mode if that happens, then fix).
	•	Penetration Testing: If resources allow, we could have an expert do a pen test or use an automated scanner (like OWASP ZAP or a service) to find any vulnerabilities (XSS, etc.) and fix them.

Server Security:
	•	Keep OS and server packages updated (set automatic security updates for Ubuntu for instance).
	•	Use SSH keys for server access (disable password auth).
	•	Implement fail2ban on SSH and possibly on WP login (although if we have login attempt limit, that covers WP).
	•	Monitor server logs (install something like Logwatch for daily summaries of any suspicious activity).
	•	Use a swap file or adequate memory to avoid crashes under load (to maintain availability).
	•	Possibly use a cloud monitoring service to alert if CPU or memory usage spikes unexpectedly (which could indicate an attack like brute force or a malfunction).
	•	If using Cloudflare or similar, turn on “I’m Under Attack” mode if a DDoS is suspected (it shows a brief challenge page).

Privacy and Data Security:
	•	The site doesn’t collect much user data, except email addresses if subscribing or if someone contacts. We will ensure any such data is stored securely. If using a mailing list service, rely on their security.
	•	We will publish a privacy policy page explaining what we collect (like cookies for analytics, email if provided) and how we protect it, fulfilling any legal requirements (GDPR, etc., since likely international readers).

9.2 Analytics Specifications

To measure site usage and impact:
	•	Tool: Use Google Analytics 4 (GA4) for comprehensive tracking. GA4 can handle multiple languages and has improved user-centric metrics. Alternatively, if privacy concerns or blocked Google in Yemen (some might have issues), consider also an open-source solution like Matomo (self-hosted analytics) for backup or for more control.
	•	We will integrate GA4 by adding the tracking code snippet to the site (via the header, possibly using a plugin like Site Kit by Google to easily set up GA, Search Console, etc.). GA4 will track pageviews, user demographics (if available), and events.
	•	Key Metrics to Track:
	•	Pageviews of each page (to see which sections popular).
	•	User language/browser language, to infer if they prefer English or Arabic (though we have separate pages for that).
	•	Geographic location of visitors (to know how much local vs international usage).
	•	Engagement time on page (to see if people are reading deeply or bouncing).
	•	Event tracking for interactions: e.g., clicks on Data Download, playing with the timeline slider, switching language, etc. GA4 supports custom events easily. We might configure events like chart_interaction (when user toggles a series or zooms), citation_click (if we want to track external link clicks on sources), subscribe (when someone submits the newsletter form).
	•	File downloads: If we host PDFs or CSVs, GA can track those link clicks.
	•	Analytics Dashboard: The site supervisor and team will have access to GA reports. We might set up a custom dashboard highlighting what we care about (for example, a section showing “Arabic vs English traffic split”, “Most searched terms leading to site”).
	•	Privacy in Analytics: We will anonymize IP addresses in GA (GA allows that to comply with GDPR). We’ll mention in privacy that we use GA and how to opt out (though GA4 doesn’t offer user opt-out easily, we can respect do-not-track via not loading GA if needed).
	•	If wanting to avoid any privacy issues, Matomo could be installed on our server. It gives full control and raw data. We could do this in addition to GA or instead. If we do, ensure performance (Matomo can be heavy but for low volume probably fine).
	•	Uptake and Goals: We can define GA goals like “Newsletter Signup Completed” to measure conversion. Or “Time on site >5min” as an engaged user metric.
	•	Reporting: The site supervisor can get a monthly summary email via GA (we can set that up) showing key stats to share with the team or donors to show site usage growth.

9.3 SEO (Search Engine Optimization) Specifications

We want the platform to rank well for relevant queries (like “Yemen economy data”, “مؤشرات اقتصاد اليمن” in Arabic, etc.). Our SEO strategy:
	•	Technical SEO:
	•	Use proper HTML tags: one H1 per page (the main title), then hierarchical H2, H3 as we have. Search engines use this to understand structure .
	•	Meta Tags: Using an SEO plugin (Yoast or RankMath). We’ll craft unique meta title and description for each page (English and Arabic separately via WPML’s SEO integration):
	•	e.g. Home meta title: “Yemen Economic Compass – Yemen’s Economic Data Platform (EN/AR)”.
	•	Home meta description: “Yemen Economic Compass provides real-time economic indicators, analysis, and interactive dashboards on Yemen’s economy in English and Arabic. Track GDP, inflation, currency exchange rates, and more.” (and Arabic equivalent for Arabic homepage).
	•	Similarly for Overview: “Overview – Yemen Economic Compass: Yemen’s war-torn economy summarized with key data on GDP, inflation, poverty, and economic fragmentation.” etc.
	•	We will add relevant keywords naturally in content (we already have many like “Yemen’s economy”, “currency war”, etc., but ensure key terms like “Yemen GDP”, “Yemen inflation 2025” appear).
	•	URL Structure: Use short, descriptive URLs. Likely base it on page title (in English and a transliterated or Arabic slug for Arabic). E.g. /overview and /ar/overview (could also do /نظرة-عامة if WPML supports Arabic slugs, which it does. Using Arabic slug might help Arabic SEO a bit, but it also can complicate manual link sharing. We might choose to keep slugs in English for simplicity. However, for better Arabic user experience, having Arabic slugs could be beneficial, like /ar/نظرة-عامة. We’ll see what WPML suggests).
	•	Create and submit XML sitemaps (Yoast will generate separate ones for each language). Submit these to Google Search Console and Bing Webmaster for indexing.
	•	Use hreflang tags for multilingual pages. WPML handles adding <link rel="alternate" hreflang="ar" href=".../ar/..."> and vice versa, so Google knows those pages are translations of each other. This prevents them from seeing duplicate content and serves the right language to the right user.
	•	Ensure the site is mobile-friendly, which it will be (Google uses mobile-first indexing).
	•	Schema markup: If possible, add structured data. For example, mark the organization details with Schema.org Organization (Name: Yemen Economic Compass, parentOrganization: Causeway Foundation, URL, logo, etc.). Also, we could mark up the data on certain pages with Schema “Dataset” if we offer downloadable data (providing metadata like dataset name, description, source). Or if we post articles, use Article schema (Yoast does this).
	•	Page Speed (as covered) – ensure fast, because Google factors Core Web Vitals into ranking. We will aim to pass core vitals (good LCP, FID, CLS scores).
	•	Content SEO:
	•	Our content already is rich and long-form (search engines love comprehensive content – e.g. the Overview page will be quite detailed, likely ranking for queries about Yemen economy background).
	•	Keywords: Identify target search queries:
	•	English: “Yemen economic data”, “Yemen GDP 2025”, “Yemen inflation rate”, “Yemen economic news”, etc.
	•	Arabic: “اقتصاد اليمن”, “التضخم في اليمن”, “سعر الريال اليمني مقابل الدولار”, “المؤشرات الاقتصادية اليمنية”.
	•	We naturally cover these in text. We might add an FAQ section on some pages with Q&A that match common queries (for example, an FAQ: “What is the current inflation rate in Yemen?” answer with latest figure and link to dashboard – this can target featured snippet).
	•	We will also ensure to have some cross-linking between pages: e.g. Overview page text mentions “(see the Economic Timeline for details)” linking to it. Stakeholder page linking to timeline where mentioned, etc. This helps SEO and user navigation.
	•	External Backlinks: Over time, encourage or arrange for relevant sites to link to us:
	•	Perhaps get listed on Wikipedia (the Yemen economy article could include a link to our site as an external resource if it meets their criteria).
	•	Outreach to journalists or analysts so they cite our site in articles (like “according to Yemen Economic Compass data… ” – using our provided citations).
	•	Causeway Foundation site should link to us.
	•	Possibly collaborate with Sana’a Center or others, they might mention our site or use our data, giving credit links.
	•	Being bilingual might also get local news sites to reference our Arabic content.
	•	Social Media: While not direct SEO, being active on Twitter with links can indirectly lead to more visibility and maybe links.
	•	Regional search: We will set up Google Search Console for both English and Arabic versions (it will pick up hreflang). This will allow us to monitor how we appear in search queries and click-through rates, and adjust meta titles if needed to improve them.
	•	Arabic SEO specifics:
	•	Use Arabic synonyms occasionally to catch variety (e.g., “الاقتصاد اليمني”, also “اقتصاد اليمن” – which we have both forms). Use both colloquial name “حوثيين” and formal “أنصار الله” somewhere since people search with either regarding northern authority and economy; similarly “حكومة عدن” or “الشرعية”. But maintain clarity.
	•	Because fewer high-quality Arabic economic resources exist, our site could rank well if optimized. We should definitely target Arabic queries as a niche advantage.
	•	Ensure the Arabic content is indexable (no accidental blocking, and is not hidden behind scripts).
	•	The WPML will ensure Arabic pages have appropriate language tags, which helps engines categorize them.
	•	Analytics for SEO: We will check in GA/Search Console which queries bring traffic and tailor content if needed (e.g. if we see many search hits for “Yemen oil production”, but our content on that is sparse, we might expand a section for it).

9.4 Monitoring and Maintenance for SEO & Analytics
	•	Regularly check Search Console for any crawl errors, mobile usability issues, or security issues flagged by Google.
	•	Use Search Console’s Index Coverage report to ensure all key pages are indexed. Also submit any new significant page (like if we add a new analysis).
	•	Also monitor keyword positions for important terms (maybe using a tool or manually searching in incognito).
	•	For analytics, periodically review if our assumptions hold: e.g., are more users using Arabic or English? If Arabic usage is high, maybe allocate more effort to Arabic content promotion.
	•	Watch bounce rates: if the Data page has a high bounce, perhaps needs better intro or speed improvement.
	•	Possibly set up a Google Alert for “Yemen Economic Compass” to catch any mentions in media so we can see our reach (and also to respond if needed, or highlight those mentions).
	•	As for privacy compliance: if targeting EU users, consider a cookie consent banner (since GA sets cookies). Many of our users might be in MENA or US, but to be safe, perhaps implement a simple cookie notice that by using site they accept analytics cookies. This can be a lightweight banner.

By following these specifications, we aim to achieve:
	•	A secure platform resilient to common attacks and preserving user trust.
	•	Insightful analytics to inform content and technical decisions.
	•	High visibility on search engines, driving organic traffic and fulfilling the site’s mission of disseminating information widely.

(Citations: We referenced security best practices e.g. using HTTPS  which Google prioritizes. Also, by aligning with core web vitals and proper tags, we lean on known SEO guidelines, though direct citations for SEO guidelines from Google’s documentation could be added if needed. The context given from focus-economics and others shows the importance of clear meta descriptions  which we incorporate. For analytics, Google’s own guides would back GA usage but those are general knowledge.)

10. Subscription and Software Stack Details

In this section, we enumerate the third-party services, subscriptions, and software components we will use to build and run the platform, including translation support, CMS tools, database, data pipeline libraries, alerting tools, and APIs. This serves as a comprehensive list of the “stack” and ensures the team knows what licenses or accounts to secure.

10.1 Content Management & Translation
	•	CMS Platform: WordPress (as discussed). It’s open-source, no license cost. We will subscribe to:
	•	WordPress Multilingual Plugin (WPML): This is a paid plugin (around ~$79 for the Multilingual CMS version per year). We’ll get a license to use WPML for robust bilingual support. Alternatively, Polylang has a free version, but WPML is more comprehensive and supported. WPML also has translation management features if we had external translators, but our team can handle it directly in the interface.
	•	Possibly Advanced Custom Fields (ACF) Pro plugin ($49 one-time) if we decide to manage data or special fields via WP. For example, to have custom input fields for homepage metrics or to store source attributions in a structured way.
	•	Automatic Translation Tools: We won’t rely on automatic translation for final content (we’ll do human translation for quality). However, for initial content drafting or to assist translators, we might use tools:
	•	Google Translate or Microsoft Translator (free via web UI or small API usage) to generate a draft which human then fixes. But careful: often not accurate for specialized text, so maybe only use to speed up translation of generic sentences.
	•	If many content updates and if we had budget, a service like Weglot (which auto-translates and lets you edit) could be considered – but we already have human translators, and Weglot is subscription-based and not as good for high quality.
	•	Conclusion: rely on human translation with WPML’s interface, maybe with Google Translate as a reference only. No paid subscription needed here beyond WPML.

10.2 Hosting & Infrastructure
	•	Cloud Server: Possibly an AWS Lightsail instance (Linux + WordPress blueprint). Lightsail prices ~ $20-30/month for a suitable instance (with 2vCPU, 4GB RAM). Or a DigitalOcean droplet ($24/mo). We’ll allocate ~$300/year for hosting.
	•	Domain Registration: e.g., through Namecheap or AWS Route53, ~$10-15/year for .org domain.
	•	CDN & DNS: Cloudflare Free Plan – provides CDN caching, SSL, WAF basic rules. No cost for standard usage. We just need to set up an account and update nameservers.
	•	Email (Transactional): The site might send emails (for contact form or WP password resets). We can use a service like SendGrid or Mailgun (they have free tiers e.g. 100 emails/day free) to ensure deliverability, since many cloud servers’ IPs are blocked by email providers. We’ll set that up for reliability (or use an SMTP plugin to route through a Gmail account, but better to use a proper service).
	•	Newsletter Service: If we implement newsletter sign-ups, managing them via a service like Mailchimp or Sendinblue might be easier than sending via WP:
	•	Mailchimp has free tier up to 2000 subscribers, which might suffice initially. It provides signup form and automation (we can embed a form in site).
	•	Sendinblue offers free tier (300 emails per day) but unlimited contacts, could be good if we have many subscribers but low volume of sending.
	•	Alternatively, if list is small, we could just BCC send from a causewayfoundation email, but that’s not scalable or compliant (Mailchimp handles unsubscribes).
	•	We likely go with Mailchimp free for ease and familiarity. No monetary cost until large list.

10.3 Data Pipeline and Visualization Stack
	•	Python for ETL: We’ll use Python scripts to fetch/update data. Python is free. We might use libraries like:
	•	Requests for API calls (to World Bank, etc.).
	•	Pandas to manipulate data if needed (e.g., reading the World Bank microdata CSV, cleaning it).
	•	wbgapi (World Bank API wrapper) as found on GitHub  for convenience to fetch indicators.
	•	If connecting to IMF SDMX, use sdmx Python libraries or just manual requests since not heavy.
	•	Schedule/cron: The server’s Linux cron service to run scripts monthly. Or a WP cron (but those run on page load, less reliable if site low traffic).
	•	We may put these scripts in a /scripts directory and run via crontab.
	•	Data Storage: For timeseries, if not using WP tables:
	•	Possibly store JSON files (we can generate them with Python and place in wp-content/uploads/data/).
	•	Or small SQLite or local CSVs, but JSON is easier for direct use in JS.
	•	No external database needed beyond MySQL (which WP covers). Data volume is low, WP’s MySQL can handle it.
	•	Charting Library: We will include:
	•	Chart.js (free, MIT license) – via either npm build or CDN. Chart.js is ~70KB gzipped, fine to include.
	•	If we choose Highcharts: it requires a license for non-personal websites, but since we’re a non-profit, we might qualify for a free license under their policy (they have a free license for personal, or we could pay ~ $1800 for a standard license, but that might be unnecessary expense since Chart.js can do what we need).
	•	Chart.js can handle line, bar, etc. For timeline maybe we need a different approach (but timeline library like TimelineJS is also free to use under MPL license).
	•	So likely no cost for visualization libraries.
	•	Maps (if any): Use Leaflet.js (open source) and maybe Mapbox tiles if needed (Mapbox has free tier up to lots of map views which we likely won’t exceed, or we can use OpenStreetMap tiles which are free).
	•	If we embed a static map image, maybe use OCHA or others, but interactive would be Leaflet.
	•	We might skip advanced maps at launch, but keep in mind.

10.4 Alerting and Monitoring Tools
	•	Uptime Monitoring: UptimeRobot free plan (monitors every 5 minutes, alerts via email) – we’ll set that for the site homepage. Or a similar free service. They often allow up to 50 monitors for free, which is fine.
	•	Analytics: Google Analytics as discussed (free). We should create a Google account for causewayfoundation to own this property, not personal accounts.
	•	Search Console: Free, just requires verification.
	•	Cloudflare: as a service also can send alerts if site is under attack or if we exceed usage, but free plan likely fine.
	•	Log Monitoring: Could use free tier of Papertrail or similar if we want aggregated logs with alerts (like if certain errors appear repeatedly).
	•	Given a small team, we might just manually check logs periodically unless traffic spikes.

10.5 Collaboration and Development Tools
	•	GitHub or GitLab repository: We might use a private GitHub repo (free for private) for code.
	•	Project Management: Maybe a simple Trello board or GitHub issues to track tasks. Possibly not a formal subscription unless causewayfoundation uses an internal system like Asana.
	•	Communication: The team likely communicates via email or Slack. If needed, create a Slack channel or use Microsoft Teams if causewayfoundation uses it. Slack free plan is fine.

10.6 Hardware/Software Requirements for Users

The platform will be web-based, so end users just need a modern browser. But we ensure:
	•	Compatibility with Chrome, Firefox, Edge (latest versions), Safari. Also that it loads on mobile browsers (Chrome/Android WebView, Mobile Safari). We won’t have any Flash or such dependency, so all good.
	•	Possibly mention if older IE is not supported (IE11 is outdated, we can drop it; if someone in Yemen is on IE on Windows 7 maybe, but usage is minimal now, and we can live without supporting IE specifically).
	•	If any heavy JavaScript, ensure it doesn’t crash on lower-end devices – Chart.js is fairly efficient.

10.7 Future Capacity and Licenses
	•	If the site grows, consider:
	•	Upgrading the hosting instance (maybe to 4vCPU, 8GB) which might cost $40-50/month. Or enabling autoscaling (on AWS, move to a small ECS cluster or AWS Aurora for DB if needed).
	•	Multi-language expansion: WPML license we have covers unlimited languages typically, so if we ever add another language (like maybe French if addressing donor community), we can do that without extra plugin cost.
	•	Additional Highcharts license if needed to use their advanced maps or features, but likely not needed with open alternatives.
	•	Monitor the usage limits of any API: World Bank APIs have generous limits (no explicit limit for reasonable use), mail services free tier vs actual usage (if we have >2000 subscribers, upgrade Mailchimp to paid ~$30/mo, factor that in if needed).
	•	Domain renewal yearly.

10.8 Software and API Summary Table

We can summarize the stack and subscriptions in a quick reference:
	•	WordPress CMS: Open-source (no cost).
	•	WPML Multilingual Plugin: Paid, ~$79/year (needed for bilingual management).
	•	Hosting (Cloud server): ~$25/month (DigitalOcean/AWS Lightsail) – $300/year.
	•	Domain Name: $12/year.
	•	Cloudflare CDN/WAF: Free tier.
	•	Analytics (GA4): Free.
	•	SEO plugin (Yoast): Free (or premium $99/yr but free suffices).
	•	Security plugin (Wordfence): Free version likely enough; premium $99/yr if needed for real-time firewall rules, might skip initially.
	•	Mailchimp: Free until 2000 contacts; potential $0 initially, possibly ~$30/mo if grows.
	•	Email SMTP (SendGrid): Free tier for low volume (or if needed, $15/mo for higher, but likely free).
	•	UptimeRobot: Free for basic monitoring.
	•	Python & libraries: Free.
	•	Chart.js / Leaflet: Free.
	•	GitHub: Free (private).
	•	Slack: Free for small team.
	•	ACF Pro (if used): $49 one-time (or yearly for updates optional).
	•	Backups Storage: We might use Amazon S3 or similar to store backups offsite (a few GB, negligible cost maybe <$1/mo). Or use free Dropbox if small, but better to have at least one external backup.

The site supervisor should ensure budget for things like WPML license and hosting costs year to year. All other components mostly free or covered by causewayfoundation’s existing IT resources.

By listing these, we ensure nothing is forgotten and the developers can set up accounts (e.g., create a Cloudflare account, Google account for analytics, etc.) and have credentials stored safely (maybe in a password manager or a secure doc shared among authorized team).

We have effectively an all-in-one blueprint of tech and subscriptions, so when launching, everything required is ready and accounted for.

*(References in this section are more about known services rather than content to cite. The cost data is common knowledge from providers. The mention of WP share  and world bank data usage  give context to decisions. Security practices align with OWASP, analytics with GA documentation.)

11. Future Expansion Capacity (Technical and Editorial)

While this blueprint establishes a robust platform for current needs, it’s important to ensure the Yemen Economic Compass can expand and evolve. This section outlines how we can scale the platform in the future, both technically (to handle more features or traffic) and editorially (to cover broader topics or other regions), without large redesigns.

11.1 Technical Scalability and Extensibility
	•	Scaling Content & Traffic: The chosen architecture (WordPress + caching + CDN) can handle significantly more content and moderate traffic growth. If traffic surges (say, after a mention on a major news outlet), we can quickly:
	•	Upgrade server resources (vertical scaling). WordPress can handle tens of thousands of visits/day on a decent VM with caching.
	•	If needed, switch to a more scalable setup (e.g., migrate to AWS ECS containers + RDS database, or a high-performance WP host). We built with portability in mind (standard WP, standard plugins).
	•	The CDN will alleviate load by serving static assets to global users from edge nodes, and caching means even high hit pages serve as static HTML.
	•	Multi-Country Expansion: Causeway Foundation might want to replicate this model for other countries. The architecture supports this in a few ways:
	•	Multisite WordPress: We could convert to a WP Multisite network, where Yemen Economic Compass is one site. We could then spin up, say, “Syria Economic Compass” as another site on the network, sharing code but with separate content databases. This would allow centralized maintenance of the theme/plugins but different content teams for each country. WPML also supports multilingual multisite if needed.
	•	Alternatively, simply clone the site setup for a new country and adjust content. The custom code (charts etc.) is fairly generic; we’d just feed it different datasets. We designed interactive components to be data-driven, so using them for another country is feasible (just pointing to a different country’s API data or files).
	•	We would ensure our code is not hardcoded for Yemen specifics beyond defaults. E.g., avoid hardcoding “YER” or country name; instead use a config file that could be changed per deployment. Possibly refactor certain bits to support a “country” parameter.
	•	The branding can adapt: use same “Economic Compass” brand pattern (e.g., “Syria Economic Compass”) with different theme colors maybe, but since Yemen’s is standalone, we can keep this instance focused but keep the concept extensible.
	•	Adding Features:
	•	If we need to incorporate new interactive tools (say a forecast simulator allowing users to tweak assumptions like “if aid increases by X, what’s impact on GDP?” or similar), we can embed such tools (maybe using JavaScript or even integrate small Python apps via Flask served separately). We set up the site such that adding a new page with a custom template is straightforward.
	•	If dynamic data gets heavier, we could implement a small API for our data (e.g., provide JSON endpoints for others to consume our compiled data). For example, api/yemen/gdp returning latest GDP. This could position the platform not just as a site but a data source. Implement as a simple WP REST API endpoint or separate microservice.
	•	We can incorporate user accounts if needed (for instance, if we later add personalized dashboards or comment sections). WP supports user registration. But moderation and security have to be ramped up for that. Perhaps out of scope unless community building becomes a goal.
	•	Possibly integrate more visualization types (maps, tree maps for budget, etc.). The theme could load D3.js for custom complex charts if needed. We have not locked ourselves to anything that prevents including new libraries.
	•	Multilingual beyond AR/EN: If needed to add, say, French content (if donors or researchers want), WPML can handle it. We’d translate or hire translators accordingly.
	•	APIs and Data Integration:
	•	If Yemen’s central bank reunifies and starts publishing data (like unified exchange rates or money supply figures), we can integrate that source. For instance, perhaps they will have a website or at least PDF bulletins. We can parse those or manually input. The data pipeline is designed to be adaptable – adding a new series is as easy as writing a new function to fetch it (if API) or adding to manual update routine.
	•	If a new global API emerges (say a specialized Yemen reconstruction data hub by UN), we can incorporate it similarly.
	•	We have reserved placeholder in our design for “Sectors” and others in future if more data becomes available (like if, post-conflict, there is data on agriculture output, or electricity generation, etc.). We can add those charts and pages.
	•	Also, we might integrate real-time news feed or Twitter feed if that becomes relevant. For example, embed Sana’a Center’s Yemen Review feed or some local currency market feed (some projects use Telegram channels data for black market rates). If that data is crucial, we could incorporate it with API or scraping.
	•	Performance Tuning for Future: If, say, we have far more data points (like daily price data for years, making charts heavy), we might implement pagination or data downsampling. Chart.js can handle a few thousand points fine, but if we try to plot minute-by-minute (which we won’t), we’d need optimization. Possibly use web workers or server-side pre-aggregation for heavy tasks if needed.
	•	Backups and Redundancy: As site importance grows, consider more robust backup (like real-time DB replication or periodic offsite images). Possibly have a standby server if uptime becomes critical (with a load balancer). At current stage, daily backups and quick restore is enough, but we can escalate if required.

11.2 Editorial and Content Expansion
	•	Deeper Analysis: Over time, the team might produce more in-depth analytical reports (e.g., a 20-page quarterly PDF). We can host those on the platform and perhaps create an Analysis section. The blueprint’s content strategy can expand to include:
	•	A library of reports (with filters by year, topic).
	•	Multi-part analysis posts (like series on currency war, etc.).
	•	Even interactive stories (embedding step-by-step charts with narrative, similar to scrollytelling – using libraries or plugins for that).
	•	User Engagement: We could add a comments feature on certain posts for community discussion (moderated to avoid spam/politics fights). If that’s undesirable on site, perhaps a moderated forum or link to a social media discussion page could be an option.
	•	Collaboration: We might partner with local institutions or universities to contribute data or articles. The platform could host guest articles (with proper review). This expands editorial content and also builds community ownership.
	•	Localization and Dialects: If needed to reach a broader Yemeni audience, perhaps consider producing some content in more accessible Arabic (the current is formal MSA, which is fine for educated audience; maybe simplified summaries for general public could be something like infographics or short Arabic explainer videos embedded).
	•	Training & Capacity Building: If the situation in Yemen improves, we could involve local staff to update data (pass the baton), making it sustainable. That means the blueprint is easy to follow by new team members: they should see documentation on how to update each piece.
	•	Other Topics: We focused on economic indicators, but a holistic view might incorporate social indicators (education, health) if data is available. We partially included some (poverty, IDPs). In future, we might have separate sections or dashboards for:
	•	Humanitarian indicators (like number of people needing aid, funding received).
