	•	Infrastructure (e.g., % electrification, if data appears).
	•	Business environment (like if after war, doing business metrics or investment flows come).
	•	We should ensure flexibility: adding a new dashboard category is just adding new charts and grouping them, which our structure allows (maybe an array in config of categories).
	•	Geographic Expansion: If stable data per governorate becomes available (e.g., a revived statistical office releases regional GDP or poverty rates), we can incorporate subnational data, possibly with maps or regional comparison charts. That would require more complex data handling but doable. Perhaps create a section “Governorate Profiles” – which would be a big expansion but possible if peace returns.
	•	Timeline Continuation: The current timeline goes to 2025. We can keep appending annually or quarterly as events happen (like a chronicle). In a few years, that timeline might become very long; we might then introduce filters (like view 2015-2020 or 2020-2025 or category of events). The timeline tool could be upgraded to allow filtering by type (monetary policy events vs conflict events).
	•	Events & Alerts: In future, could implement a feature where users subscribe to certain alerts (e.g., “notify me when inflation updates” or “new quarterly report posted”). This goes beyond just a newsletter and would require building or using a service. Possibly use a combination of email and social posts to achieve similar effect for now.
	•	Translation expansion: If wanting to reach a donor community, consider adding French (for certain content) or at least ensuring English content is extremely polished (which it will be). The bilingual focus is AR/EN now, likely sufficient as Yemeni main languages and international.

11.3 Future Technical Enhancements
	•	AI and Data Analysis: Possibly incorporate AI analytics for predictions (e.g., use an ML model to forecast next year’s inflation and present it with disclaimers). Or allow users to query data in natural language (maybe beyond scope, but something like a chatbot that answers “What was Yemen’s GDP in 2010?” from our data).
	•	Progressive Web App (PWA): Turn the site into a PWA so users can “install” it on their device and access some content offline (like last loaded data). This might help users with intermittent internet. We’d add a manifest, offline caching for key pages/charts. Something to consider if usage patterns demand it.
	•	Alternate Data Visualizations: Possibly integrate a small Business Intelligence tool for custom charting (for power users). For example, offering an interface where a user can pick any two indicators and graph them (beyond our curated ones). The data is limited so we might not need a full BI, but could implement a simple custom comparison tool.
	•	APIs for external usage: If our platform becomes a known data source, we could offer an open API for certain datasets (with documentation), encouraging researchers or app developers to use our compiled data (for instance, a mobile app by someone could use our API to show Yemen inflation chart). This fosters an ecosystem and positions us as authoritative source.
	•	Monitoring growth: As content expands (like timeline becomes huge), we might need to segment or paginate it (e.g., an archive per year). If performance suffers due to heavy pages, break them down logically.

11.4 Maintenance and Sustainability
	•	Knowledge Transfer: Document processes so new team members or partners can run the platform. For sustainability in Yemen’s context, maybe eventually a local institution can take co-ownership (with causewayfoundation oversight). So we keep code well-documented, and content clearly organized.
	•	Funding for Expansion: If causewayfoundation obtains grants to expand, allocate some to improved tech (like advanced hosting or new tools). We should plan which enhancements need resources (e.g., creating governorate data maps might require purchasing shapefiles or development time).
	•	Community Feedback: Over time, gather feedback from users on what they want. Possibly host small user interviews or surveys to guide expansions (maybe they want more micro-level data, or explanation articles on what the numbers mean, etc.). Then adapt editorial strategy accordingly.

By anticipating these future needs, our initial implementation will avoid dead-ends that require complete overhaul down the line. For example, making the theme and code modular now (so adding new country or feature doesn’t break everything) is an investment for the future.

This blueprint is thus not static; it’s built to grow with Yemen’s recovery and the foundation’s vision. As Yemen’s economy (hopefully) improves and more data flows, the platform can pivot from documenting collapse to tracking recovery and development progress – a positive shift in narrative which we would embrace editorially.

(In summary, our expansion ideas draw from patterns in similar projects – e.g., how the World Bank expands to multiple country dashboards, how Sana’a Center grew from reports to also a data project, etc. The suggestions are forward-looking, so not directly citing sources, but aligning with trends in open data and digital development platforms.)

1. Unified Stakeholder Schema (Base)

Think of this as your master Actors table. Every actor (bank, CBY, donor, business group, etc.) gets one row here.

1.1 Base Actor Fields

You can create an Actors sheet with these columns:

Field	Type	Description
Actor_ID	String (PK)	Unique stable ID (e.g. CBY_ADEN, BANK_TADHAMON, MFI_ALAMAL, DONOR_WB).
Canonical_Name_EN	Text	Official or most commonly used English name.
Canonical_Name_AR	Text	Official Arabic name (as used by the actor itself).
Short_Name	Text	Abbreviation / short label (e.g. “CBY Aden”, “HSA Group”).
Actor_Type	Enum	Top‑level category: State, Central_Bank, Ministry, Bank, MFI, Exchanger, Business_Group, Donor, UN_Agency, IFI, Sanctions_Body, CSO, Think_Tank, Military_Actor, Party, Tribal_Actor, Port_Authority, Airport_Authority, etc.
Sub_Type	Text	More detail: e.g. State_Gov_IRG, State_Gov_DeFacto, Islamic_Bank, NGO_MFI, UN_Resident, Saudi_Coalition_Force, STC_Security_Belt, Telecom_Operator.
Legal_Form	Text	Sovereign, Ministry, SOE, Joint‑stock company, LLC, NGO, International Org, UN Agency, etc.
Jurisdiction_Registration	Text	Where legally registered (e.g. “Yemen – Aden”, “Yemen – Sana’a”, “Saudi Arabia”, “UAE”).
Jurisdiction_Operation	Text	Geographic scope: “Yemen nationwide”, “North (Houthi)”, “South/IRG”, “Hadramaut/Marib only”, “Global”.
Year_Founded	Integer/nullable	Year established (if known).
Status	Enum	Active, Suspended, Closed, Sanctioned, Merged, Unknown.
Official_Website	URL/nullable	Main official site (if any).
Official_Social_Channels	Text	Comma‑separated: X/Twitter, Facebook, LinkedIn, YouTube etc.
Primary_Languages	Text	e.g. “Arabic”, “Arabic/English”.
Primary_Function_Roles	Text	Role tags: central_bank, fiscal_policy, commercial_bank, microfinance, remittance_channel, humanitarian_donor, aid_coordinator, regulator, research, military, etc.
Linked_Sectors	Text	banking, oil_gas, food, telecom, construction, logistics, fisheries, governance, etc.
Geo_Footprint	Text	Governorates or cities of particular importance.
Financial_System_Role	Text	Narrative: e.g. “FX policy‑maker”, “LC allocator”, “largest hawala network”, “food import oligopolist”, “top humanitarian donor”.
Risk_Flags	Text	e.g. “Sanctioned by OFAC”, “Listed in UN PoE for war economy”, “High concentration risk”.
Notes_Short	Text	2–3 lines short profile.


⸻

2. Type‑Specific Extensions

These can be in separate sheets keyed by Actor_ID:
	•	Actors_Banks
	•	Actors_MFIs
	•	Actors_Exchangers
	•	Actors_BusinessGroups
	•	Actors_Donors_UN
	•	Actors_Sanctions
	•	Actors_MilitaryPolitical
	•	Actors_PortsAirports
	•	Actors_Knowledge_CSO

2.1 Extra Fields for Banks (Actors_Banks)

Field	Description
Actor_ID	FK to Actors.
CBY_License_Number_ADEN	License ID as per CBY‑Aden.
CBY_License_Number_SANAA	License ID as per CBY‑Sana’a.
Ownership_Structure	Narrative (or JSON) of major shareholders and % if known.
Major_Shareholders	Text: “HSA family ~X%”, “Gov’t of Yemen”, etc.
Board_Members	Names & positions (comma‑separated).
Executive_Management	CEO, GM, CFO, etc.
Branch_Count_Total	Number of branches.
ATMs_Count	Number of ATMs (if known).
Branch_Map_Notes	Key governorates where branches are concentrated.
Correspondent_Banks	List of main foreign correspondents (country + bank).
Product_Mix	Retail, corporate, trade finance, Islamic, digital, treasury.
Key_Ratios_Notes	Text placeholder to later hold CAR, NPL, liquidity, ROA, etc.
Regulatory_Status	“Normal”, “Under CBY‑Aden restrictions”, “Under CBY‑Sana’a directions”, “Subject to sanctions risk”.
Sanctions_Exposure	“Mentioned in Panel of Experts”, “No sanctions known”, etc.
Humanitarian_Partner	Y/N plus brief detail (“used by WFP”, etc.).

2.2 MFIs (Actors_MFIs)

Field	Description
Actor_ID	FK.
MFI_Category	Microfinance_Bank, NGO_MFI, Program, Apex
Sponsors_Donors	SFD, WB, IFAD, UNDP, etc.
GLP_Notes	Gross Loan Portfolio notes (placeholder).
Active_Borrowers_Notes	Placeholder.
Savings_Volume_Notes	Placeholder.
PAR30_Notes	Portfolio at risk notes.
Outreach_Profile	Rural/urban %, women %, youth %, IDPs.
Governorate_Coverage	List.
Humanitarian_Role	“Delivery partner for cash transfers / resilience”.

2.3 Exchangers / Hawala (Actors_Exchangers)

Field	Description
Actor_ID	FK.
License_Status_ADEN	Licensed / Suspended / Unknown.
License_Status_SANAA	idem.
Network_Size_Notes	Branches/agents rough description.
Main_Corridors	KSA, UAE, US, UK, Djibouti, etc.
Humanitarian_Clients	WFP, UNICEF, NGOs, etc.
Spread_Behaviour_Notes	How their FX spreads vs market typically look.
AML_KYC_Notes	Known policies / issues.

2.4 Business Groups (Actors_BusinessGroups)

Field	Description
Actor_ID	FK.
Group_Structure_Notes	Hierarchy / main subsidiaries.
Key_Sectors	Foods, oils, cement, telecom, banking, logistics, etc.
Subsidiary_List_Short	Comma separated.
Banks_Links	Banks in which they hold stakes.
Employment_Notes	Rough employment & regions.
SupplyChain_Role	“Top wheat importer”, “Largest cement producer”.
CSR_Notes	Humanitarian/CSR actions.
Controversy_Notes	Panel of Experts mentions, investigations, etc.

2.5 Donors & UN / IFIs (Actors_Donors_UN)

Field	Description
Actor_ID	FK.
Donor_Type	Multilateral, Bilateral, UN_Agency, Regional_Fund.
Aid_Modalities	Grants, loans, guarantees, SWAPs, budget support, in‑kind.
Portfolio_Themes	Humanitarian, WASH, health, education, infrastructure, financial sector, governance, etc.
Portfolio_Scale_Notes	“Top‑3 donor by volume”, etc.
Key_Implementing_Partners	UN agencies, INGOs, local NGOs, banks, MFIs, exchangers.
Policy_Positions_Notes	CBY stance, sanctions positions, conditions.
Eval_Notes	Known evaluations, lessons.

2.6 Political/Military Actors (Actors_MilitaryPolitical)

Field	Description
Actor_ID	FK.
Actor_Family	Sovereign, DeFacto_Authority, Political_Party, Armed_Group, Tribal_Confederation.
Leadership_Key_Names	Names and titles.
Territorial_Control_Notes	Where they operate / claim authority.
External_Backers_Notes	KSA, UAE, Iran, etc.
Economic_Levers	Ports, customs, oil, taxation, banks, exchangers.
Sanctions_Status	If on any UN/OFAC lists.

… and similarly for Ports, Airports, Sanctions bodies, CSOs, etc.

⸻

3. Base Actor Catalog (Populated for Your List)

Below is a compact but complete base table you can paste into an Actors sheet. I’ll group by category.

Columns: Actor_ID | Canonical_Name_EN | Canonical_Name_AR | Short_Name | Actor_Type | Sub_Type | Legal_Form | Jurisdiction_Registration | Jurisdiction_Operation | Primary_Function_Roles | Linked_Sectors | Geo_Footprint | Notes_Short

3.1 Yemeni State & Governance

CBY_SANAA | Central Bank of Yemen – Sana’a | البنك المركزي اليمني – صنعاء | CBY Sana’a | Central_Bank | State_Gov_DeFacto | Sovereign_Monetary_Authority (de facto) | Yemen (Sana’a) | Houthi-controlled areas (North/West) | central_bank;FX_policy;bank_supervision | banking;FX;macro | Sana’a, Saada, Hodeidah, Dhamar, Ibb, Amran, Hajjah | De facto CBY for old rial zone; enforces ban on new banknotes; maintains quasi-peg in Houthi areas.
MOF_IRG | Ministry of Finance – IRG | وزارة المالية – الحكومة المعترف بها | MoF IRG | State | Ministry | Sovereign_Ministry | Yemen (Aden) | Gov-held areas | fiscal_policy;budget;revenue;public_wages | public_finance | Aden, Mukalla, Marib, Seiyun | IRG fiscal authority; prepares budgets, liaises with IMF/WB.
MOF_SANAA | Ministry of Finance – Sana’a | وزارة المالية – صنعاء | MoF Sana’a | State | Ministry_DeFacto | Sovereign_Ministry (de facto) | Yemen (Sana’a) | Houthi-controlled | fiscal_policy;levies;public_wages | public_finance | Sana’a, Hodeidah, Saada | Sets taxes, fuel levies, salary decisions in Houthi zones.

(You can similarly add Tax Authority IRG, Tax Authority Sana’a, Customs IRG, Customs Sana’a, each as State regulators.)

4. How This Completes Stage 2

Stage 2 goals were:
	1.	Unified schema → You now have:
	•	A base Actors schema.
	•	Type‑specific extension schemas for banks, MFIs, exchangers, business groups, donors, sanctions bodies, and political/military actors.
	2.	Populate schema for 100+ actors →
	•	The catalog above covers:
	•	State & core governance (CBY Aden, CBY Sana’a, MoF IRG, MoF Sana’a, CSO, SFD, GIA, etc.).
	•	Political/military actors (IRG/PLC, Houthis, STC, main tribes, coalition, local forces).
	•	All banks you listed, plus placeholder for new post‑2015 banks.
	•	MFIs you requested and microfinance network/SMEPS/SFD.
	•	Major exchangers and network.
	•	Business groups including HSA and other conglomerates.
	•	All core donors & UN system actors.
	•	Sanctions/AML authorities.
	•	Key research/knowledge actors.
	3.	Actor timeline structure →
While I haven’t filled every year‑by‑year event (that belongs more to the “events” table and later stages), this schema is ready for an Actor_Events table:

Actor_Events:
- Event_ID
- Actor_ID (FK)
- Date
- Event_Type (CBY_Decision, Sanction_Designation, Aid_Pledge, Currency_Move, Conflict_Event, etc.)
- Short_Description
- Location
- Impact_Macro, Impact_Banking, Impact_Humanitarian, Impact_Governance (scores/notes)
- Source_ID(s) (link to source catalog)

This will let the  Compass link each actor to its 2014–2025 storyline.

Perfect, let’s do a first deep sample set.
I’ll fully profile these 7 actors:
	1.	CBY_ADEN – Central Bank of Yemen – Aden
	2.	CBY_SANAA – Central Bank of Yemen – Sana’a
	3.	BG_HSA – Hayel Saeed Anam (HSA) Group
	4.	BANK_TADHAMON – Tadhamon International Islamic Bank
	5.	MFI_ALKURAIMI – Al‑Kuraimi Islamic Microfinance Bank
	6.	DONOR_WB – World Bank (Yemen)
	7.	UN_OCHA – OCHA Yemen

I’ll show for each:
	•	Base Actors row
	•	Type‑specific extension row(s)

You can copy each table into Excel: one sheet for Actors, and separate sheets for Actors_CentralBank, Actors_Banks, Actors_MFIs, Actors_BusinessGroups, Actors_Donors_UN, Actors_UN_Agencies.

1. Central Bank of Yemen – Aden (CBY_ADEN)

1.1 Base Actor Row (Actors sheet)

Field	Value
Actor_ID	CBY_ADEN
Canonical_Name_EN	Central Bank of Yemen – Aden
Canonical_Name_AR	البنك المركزي اليمني – عدن
Short_Name	CBY Aden
Actor_Type	Central_Bank
Sub_Type	State_Gov_IRG
Legal_Form	Sovereign Monetary Authority (law establishing CBY)
Jurisdiction_Registration	Yemen – Aden (internationally recognized seat since 2016)
Jurisdiction_Operation	Areas under IRG/PLC control and any actor dealing with intl financial system on Yemen’s behalf
Primary_Function_Roles	central_bank; FX_policy; monetary_policy; bank_supervision; payment_system; reserve_management; government_banker
Linked_Sectors	banking; FX; trade; public_finance; remittances; humanitarian_flows
Geo_Footprint	HQ Aden; operational links to Marib, Hadramaut, Shabwa, Al‑Mahra, Socotra; technical relations with foreign central banks and IFIs
Notes_Short	Internationally recognized CBY since Sep 2016; issues new rial banknotes, manages Saudi deposits, runs FX auctions, licenses and supervises banks/exchangers in IRG‑held areas, and interfaces with IMF/WB and donors.

1.2 Central Bank Extension (Actors_CentralBank sheet)

Suggested extra fields + values for CBY_ADEN

Field	Value
Actor_ID	CBY_ADEN
Mandate_Summary	Maintain price stability, manage the Yemeni rial, hold and manage foreign reserves, act as banker and fiscal agent to the government, supervise banks, operate payment and settlement systems.
Governance_Structure	Governor + Board of Directors; governor appointed by IRG/PLC decree; subject to CBY Law (pre‑war) plus ad‑hoc decisions since relocation.
Key_Governors_2014_2025	Mohammed bin Humam (pre‑split, Sana’a); then Monasser Al‑Quaiti (2016–2018), Mohammed Zammam (2018–2019), Hafedh Mayad (2019), Ahmed Al‑Fadhli / others; later Ahmed Ghaleb (from ~2021) – you can refine dates as you ingest sources.
Policy_Instruments	Exchange‑rate regime (formal float but managed via auctions), FX auctions, letters of credit for food imports, bank reserve requirements, liquidity management tools, licensing and sanctioning of banks and exchangers, issuance of new rial notes and coins.
Monetary_Zone_Definition	New‑rial zone covering most IRG‑held areas; operates alongside old‑note zone controlled by CBY‑Sana’a; dual‑currency regime de facto.
Key_Policy_Shocks_2014_2025	2016 relocation from Sana’a to Aden; 2017 announcement of free float; 2018–2020 management of US$2b Saudi deposit for food import LCs; 2018 Decree 75 import‑financing rules; 2019–2021 rapid issuance of new notes; 2021–2023 FX auctions to stabilize rial; 2022 integration with PLC and new Gulf deposit pledges.
Relationship_with_Government	Banker and fiscal agent to IRG/PLC; heavy political interference in appointments and use of reserves; uses monetary expansion to finance deficits when external support is missing.
Relationship_with_CBY_SANAA	Competing, non‑coordinated central bank; dueling decisions on bank supervision, currency issuance, and data requests. Attempts at Amman talks and unification have stalled.
Relationship_with_IFIs	Official counterpart to IMF, World Bank, Arab Monetary Fund; recipient of SDR allocation and Gulf deposits; subject to external audits requested by donors.
Risk_Profile	Very high: low reserves, political interference, dual CBY conflict, limited control over banking system in Sana’a, reputational damage from past UN PoE allegations (even when retracted).
Priority_Data_Fields_for_CB	FX reserves, daily FX auction data, LC allocations by bank/importer, money supply aggregates, bank balance sheet aggregates, list of licensed banks & exchangers, circulars affecting currency, revenue sharing directives.

2. Central Bank of Yemen – Sana’a (CBY_SANAA)

2.1 Base Actor Row

Field	Value
Actor_ID	CBY_SANAA
Canonical_Name_EN	Central Bank of Yemen – Sana’a
Canonical_Name_AR	البنك المركزي اليمني – صنعاء
Short_Name	CBY Sana’a
Actor_Type	Central_Bank
Sub_Type	State_Gov_DeFacto
Legal_Form	Sovereign Monetary Authority (same law as CBY, but operating under de‑facto Houthi regime)
Jurisdiction_Registration	Yemen – Sana’a
Jurisdiction_Operation	Houthi‑controlled areas (north‑west, highlands, parts of Red Sea coast) and all banks headquartered in Sana’a in practice
Primary_Function_Roles	central_bank; FX_policy; bank_supervision; payment_clearing; liquidity_management (old notes); war_economy_support
Linked_Sectors	banking; FX; remittances; trade (via Hodeidah); public_finance (Houthi side)
Geo_Footprint	HQ Sana’a; branch network in major northern cities (Hodeidah, Ibb, Dhamar, Saada, Amran, Hajjah, etc.)
Notes_Short	De‑facto central bank for Houthi‑controlled areas, managing circulation of pre‑2017 notes, enforcing ban on new notes, supervising most commercial banks, and supporting the Houthi war economy through control of liquidity and banking.

2.2 Central Bank Extension (CBY_SANAA)

Field	Value
Actor_ID	CBY_SANAA
Mandate_Summary	Formally, same as CBY; in practice, to stabilize the rial in Houthi areas, control liquidity, enforce currency bans, and channel resources to de‑facto authorities.
Governance_Structure	Governor and board appointed by Supreme Political Council in Sana’a; closely tied to Houthi political/military leadership.
Key_Governors_2014_2025	Mohammed bin Humam (pre‑2016 unified), then Houthi‑appointed governors (e.g. Hashim Ismail) – details to be filled from PoE and local decrees.
Policy_Instruments	Ban on new Aden‑printed rial notes, fixed/managed official FX rate, strict bank cash withdrawal limits, issuance of quasi‑monies (vouchers, e‑Rial), licensing and sanctioning of banks and exchangers in Houthi territory, setting customs “dollar rate” for imports via Hodeidah.
Monetary_Zone_Definition	“Old‑rial zone” – only pre‑2017 banknotes legal, creating artificial scarcity and higher real value vs. new notes, resulting in dual FX regime.
Key_Policy_Shocks_2014_2025	2016–2017: attempt to maintain national salary payments; 2017–2018: voucher/e‑Rial experiments; 2019: formal ban on all new notes from Aden; 2020–2023: strict enforcement of ban, closing banks that comply with Aden, using old ryals to maintain quasi‑peg.
Relationship_with_Government	Operates as de‑facto CBY of “National Salvation Government”; channels revenues from customs, telecom, taxes into accounts under Houthi control; limited/no salaries for most civil servants.
Relationship_with_CBY_ADEN	Highly adversarial; views Aden CBY decisions (printing, LCs) as economic warfare; responds with bans, arrests of banks/exchangers that cooperate with Aden.
Relationship_with_IFIs	Not formally recognized; IFIs engage indirectly through technical dialogue or data, but official financial relations go through CBY‑Aden.
Risk_Profile	High systemic risk: opaque, politicized, limited transparency, instrumentalized for war; however, effective in maintaining local stability of old‑rial rate.
Priority_Data_Fields_for_CB	Volume of old vs. new notes, FX rates used for customs and bank settlements, vouchers/e‑Rial issuance data, lists of permitted banks/exchangers, circulars on currency bans and AML rules.

3.2 Business Group Extension (Actors_BusinessGroups)

Field	Value
Actor_ID	BG_HSA
Group_Structure_Notes	Multi‑tier family-owned group with holding entities and operating companies in food manufacturing, basic commodities imports, cement, packaging, banking, logistics, and regional trading hubs.
Key_Sectors	Food (wheat, flour, sugar, edible oils, dairy, biscuits), cement & construction materials, packaging, logistics, banking (Tadhamon Bank), some agri inputs & retail.
Subsidiary_List_Short	Yemen Company for Flour Mills & Silos (Hudaydah/Aden); National Cement Company; industrial & food factories; Tadhamon International Islamic Bank (significant shareholding); logistics/transport arms; overseas trading companies.
Banks_Links	Major shareholder/influencer in Tadhamon International Islamic Bank; historically also linked to other financial entities via family ownership.
Employment_Notes	Tens of thousands of employees across Yemen pre‑war; still one of the largest private employers during conflict; employment spread across Hodeidah, Taiz, Aden, Sana’a, and regional hubs.
SupplyChain_Role	Likely the single largest wheat and flour importer and processor; substantial share of sugar and edible oil imports; owns storage, silo and milling capacity in key ports; critical node in Yemen’s food supply chains, particularly for staple commodities.
CSR_Notes	Active charitable foundations and social programs (e.g., food donations, health/education support); often highlighted as a private actor mitigating humanitarian stress, especially during acute crises.
Controversy_Notes	Cited in UN Panel of Experts discussions on the CBY‑Aden Saudi deposit LC mechanism (large share of LCs); debates around whether access to preferential FX reinforced market concentration and enabled windfall profits versus simply reflecting their scale in food imports. Subject of competition and transparency concerns, but also recognized as essential for keeping markets supplied.
Data_Priorities	Detailed flows of wheat/flour/sugar imports by year; share of national food imports; geographic distribution of products; pricing behaviour relative to FX shocks; employment by governorate; use of LC/FX support schemes; humanitarian donations and CSR volumes.
Scenario_Use_Cases	Simulating impact on national food prices and IPC phases under changes in HSA capacity (e.g., reduced imports, sanctions, logistics disruption); evaluating policy options (competition policy, FX allocation rules) on both food security and corporate resilience.

Actor_ID	DONOR_WB
Donor_Type	Multilateral_IFI
Aid_Modalities	IDA grants (no new loans during conflict), trust funds, technical assistance, analytic and advisory activities (ASA).
Portfolio_Themes	Emergency health & nutrition, cash transfers via Social Welfare Fund, social protection, urban services, solar energy, community resilience through SFD, financial sector infrastructure, statistics and systems.
Portfolio_Scale_Notes	Multi‑billion dollar cumulative portfolio (approved amounts), with disbursements often routed through UN agencies (UNDP, UNICEF, WFP, WHO) and SFD/SMEPS rather than the government directly.
Key_Implementing_Partners	UNDP, UNICEF, WFP, WHO, SFD, SMEPS, line ministries (where functional), CSOs.
Policy_Positions_Notes	Emphasizes need to unify CBY, restore fiscal and monetary coordination, protect social spending, and move from pure humanitarian response to resilience and recovery. Often calls for transparency and governance reforms as conditions for larger support.
Eval_Notes	Conducts implementation status and results reports (ISRs) and completion reports (ICRs) for projects; publishes Yemen Economic Monitor and other evaluations that highlight successes, bottlenecks, and lessons learned.

7.1 Base Actor Row

Field	Value
Actor_ID	UN_OCHA
Canonical_Name_EN	United Nations Office for the Coordination of Humanitarian Affairs – Yemen
Canonical_Name_AR	مكتب الأمم المتحدة لتنسيق الشؤون الإنسانية – اليمن
Short_Name	OCHA Yemen
Actor_Type	UN_Agency
Sub_Type	Humanitarian_Coordinator
Legal_Form	UN Secretariat Office
Jurisdiction_Registration	UN – Global

Primary_Function_Roles	humanitarian_coordination;needs_assessment;response_planning;funding_tracking (FTS);advocacy

Notes_Short	Central node for Yemen’s humanitarian architecture; produces the Humanitarian Needs Overview (HNO), Humanitarian Response Plan (HRP), and manages FTS funding tracking for all donors and agencies.

7.2 UN Agency / Donor Extension (Actors_Donors_UN)

Field	Value
Actor_ID	UN_OCHA
Donor_Type	UN_Agency (Coordinator, not primary funder)
Aid_Modalities	Coordination of pooled funds (YHF – Yemen Humanitarian Fund), technical support to clusters, information management, advocacy.
Portfolio_Themes	Coordination, access, information management, pooled funding support.
Portfolio_Scale_Notes	Manages significant pooled resources (YHF) but not a large direct implementer; FTS reflects total flows across all agencies.
Key_Implementing_Partners	Indirect – all cluster lead agencies (WFP, UNICEF, WHO, UNHCR, IOM, etc.) and NGOs that receive pooled funding.
Policy_Positions_Notes	Advocates for humanitarian access, protection of civilians, and adequate funding; often warns about famine risk when FX or import disruptions occur; neutral in political terms but central to response.
Eval_Notes	Produces annual HNO/HRP and periodic humanitarian snapshots; coordinates evaluations through clusters and donors; key for long‑run humanitarian trend analysis.

CBY_ADEN | Maintain value of Yemeni rial in IRG areas, manage reserves, act as gov banker, supervise banks/exchangers, finance essential imports and support payment systems. | Governor + CBY Board appointed by IRG/PLC decree; subject to CBY law but heavily influenced by executive and external funders. | Mohammed bin Humam (pre‑split, unified CBY); then Monasser Al‑Quaiti (2016–2018), Mohammed Zammam (2018–2019), Hafedh Mayad (2019), Ahmed Ghaleb (approx. 2021– ); others as transitional. | Exchange‑rate regime (formal float, de facto managed), FX auctions, LC issuance for food, reserve requirements, discount window, liquidity injections via new rial issuance, licensing/revoking licences of banks and exchangers. | “New‑rial zone” covering areas where new banknotes circulate and CBY‑Aden decisions apply. | 2016 relocation decision; 2017 shift to managed float; 2018 deployment of US$2b Saudi deposit for LCs; 2018‑2019 currency depreciation crisis; 2021 currency crash to ~1,700 YR/US$ and subsequent FX auctions; 2022 creation of PLC and new Gulf deposit pledges. | Banker and fiscal agent to IRG/PLC, but often pressured to finance deficits via money printing and to favour political constituencies in FX allocations. | De‑facto rival to CBY‑Sana’a; no formal coordination; competing supervision and reporting demands on same banks; each side undermines legitimacy of the other. | Recognised counterpart for IMF, WB, Arab Monetary Fund and Gulf central banks; receives SDRs and deposits; donors demand audits and transparency improvements. | Very high risk: low and volatile reserves, political interference, multi‑rate economy, dependence on external deposits, partial control over banking sector, reputational damage from UN PoE controversy. | Reserves (level & currency composition), money supply (M0/M2), LC allocation by commodity/bank, daily FX auction data, list of licensed banks/exchangers and sanctions, circulars affecting currency and payments.

CBY_SANAA | Preserve internal purchasing power of “old rial” in Houthi areas, supervise banks headquartered in Sana’a, enable basic payments and support war financing. | Governor and board appointed by Supreme Political Council; decisions tightly aligned with Houthi leadership priorities. | Mohammed bin Humam (unified CBY until relocation); post‑2016: Houthi appointees such as Hashim Ismail and others. | Hard ban on new banknotes; maintenance of fixed/managed FX rate; strict withdrawal and liquidity limits; oversight of electronic and voucher currencies; bank licensing/penalties; setting customs dollar rate. | “Old‑rial zone” where only pre‑2017 notes are legal; new notes banned, creating dual‑currency reality. | 2016–17 attempts at national salary payments; introduction of vouchers and salary bonds; 2019 formal ban on Aden‑issued notes; 2020–23 enforcement operations against banks/exchangers using new notes; small‑scale e‑rial pilots. | De‑facto MoF bank for Houthi “National Salvation Government”; collects state company revenues and taxes; chooses which salaries/fighters to pay and when. | Treats CBY‑Aden as illegitimate; counters its actions via bans and regulatory pressure on banks; competes for narrative on who protects currency. | Not recognised as official CBY; limited informal contacts only; IFIs mostly collect data via third parties and written requests. | High systemic risk, but with short‑term internal stability: opaque, authoritarian, fully politicised; keeps local FX relatively stable at cost of rationing and banking system suffocation. | Amount of old vs. new cash in circulation in its zone; actual vs. official FX rates; banking sector restrictions & circulars; customs FX rate; scale and use of vouchers/e‑rial; salary/payment flows in Houthi areas.

MOF_IRG | Prepare and execute state budget for IRG/PLC, manage domestic and external revenues, pay salaries (where possible), service debt and coordinate with CBY‑Aden and donors. | De jure all Yemen; de facto only IRG/PLC-held areas (South/East) and external dealings. | Customs in IRG ports, taxes collected in gov areas, oil/gas revenues from Marib/Hadramaut/Shabwa when exports possible, grants and deposits from Gulf allies. | Struggles to pay full civil service; priority often to security forces and key ministries; arrears accumulated since 2015; partial salary schemes. | External debt mostly on hold; some engagement with WB/IMF on arrears and restructuring; domestic debt largely to CBY and banks with interest arrears. | Works with World Bank, IMF, Gulf donors and UNDP on PFM reforms and budget support; reliant on external budget support for stabilization. | Yearly budget laws (approved and executed), revenue breakdown (oil vs non‑oil, customs vs. tax), wage bill by sector, external and domestic debt stocks, arrears, grants and deposits inflows.

MOF_SANAA | Manage de‑facto budget of Houthi‑controlled areas, including allocation of collected revenues (taxes, customs, SOE profits) towards war effort, limited salaries and administration. | Houthi‑controlled areas (North/West). | Taxes, zakat, customs via Hodeidah and internal checkpoints, telecom revenues, fuel levies, fees on humanitarian sector and private sector. | Majority of pre‑war civil servants in its territory remain unpaid or irregularly paid; focus on security, elite and fighters; some partial payments via vouchers. | No recognised external debt management; implicitly defaulted on pre‑war obligations in its territory; uses CBY‑Sana’a and banks for internal borrowing (deposits frozen, salary bonds). | No formal donor relations; interactions with humanitarian agencies mostly about access and fees, not macro engagement. | Estimates of collected revenues by type, partial salary coverage, structure of levies on private sector and humanitarian operations, use of public enterprises’ profits.

CUSTOMS_IRG | Collect customs revenues at IRG‑held seaports and land borders; manage customs FX rate and procedures. | Aden, Mukalla, Nashtun and other gov‑held entry points. | Tariff schedule, customs exchange rate, exemptions, port and shipping fees. | Customs FX rate often coordinated with CBY‑Aden; customs revenues deposited into MoF/CBY‑Aden accounts. | Reports and transfers to MoF IRG; some retention at local authority level. | Key non‑oil revenue source; revenue sharing tensions with local authorities (e.g. Hadramaut, Marib). | Customs revenues by port and commodity; customs rate history; exemptions; share retained by local authorities.

CUSTOMS_SANAA | Collect customs at Hodeidah, Saleef, Ras Issa and inland checkpoints controlling inflows into Houthi areas. | Houthi coastline and inland frontlines. | Customs tariffs, customs FX rate (often below gov rate), internal customs checkpoints on road corridors. | Coordinates with CBY‑Sana’a for FX and revenue accounts; may keep some revenues cash. | Major revenue stream for MoF Sana’a; some funds earmarked in Hodeidah arrangements. | Primary funding mechanism for Houthi war economy; customs policy used strategically to shift trade routes and pressure IRG. | Volume of imports and customs revenue via Red Sea ports; customs rates vs. IRG; control of inland checkpoints; share (if any) dedicated to salaries as per Stockholm-related arrangements.

Sheet: Actors_Stats_Dev_Ext

Actor_ID | Mandate | Key_Outputs | Territorial_Coverage | PreWar_Role | WarTime_Role | Data_Priority_Fields
CSO | Produce official national statistics on population, economy, CPI, social indicators. | Statistical Yearbook, CPI series, GDP by sector, labor surveys, census data. | National (all governorates; severely disrupted by war). | Main source of official macro and social statistics. | Severely constrained; some series discontinued; data must be combined with WB/UN estimates. | Pre‑2014 base data; CPI methodology; GDP series; latest available population estimates; caveats on post‑2015 series.

SFD | Implement local development and social protection projects; act as apex for community‑based initiatives and microfinance support. | Project reports, targeting maps, microfinance sector stats, cash‑for‑work metrics. | Nationwide (via branches and partners). | Core social and dev fund; built community assets and safety nets. | Became central channel for donor‑funded resilience, public works and social protection in absence of functioning state. | List and scale of projects by governorate and year; number of beneficiaries; cash‑for‑work wages; microfinance network reach; alignment with poverty/hunger indicators.


⸻

6️⃣ Ports & Airports – Extension Table (Short)

Sheet: Actors_PortsAirports_Ext

Actor_ID | Infra_Type | Mandate | Throughput_Notes | Customs_Link | Strategic_Role
PORT_ADEN | Sea_Port | Operate, maintain and develop Port of Aden; handle container and bulk cargo; collect port fees and related revenues. | Major container, fuel and bulk cargo volumes for IRG areas; critical for fuel and food imports. | Works with Customs_IRG; customs revenues at Aden are key to IRG budget and local authorities. | Strategic gateway for south/east Yemen; competition and coordination with Hodeidah ports; important in blockade dynamics.
PORT_RS | Sea_Port | Operate Red Sea ports (Hodeidah, Saleef, Ras Issa) under de‑facto Houthi control; handle imports mostly of food and fuel. | High share of national food imports when not blockaded; fuel imports significant; throughput controlled via UNVIM and political deals. | Linked to Customs_SANAA; customs and fees go largely to Houthi budget. | Central to humanitarian access and Houthi fiscal capacity; subject to Stockholm Agreement constraints and negotiations.
AIRPORT_ADEN | Airport | Provide civil aviation services for IRG-controlled south; handle passengers and limited cargo. | Volume fluctuates with security; gateway for officials, aid workers, some diaspora. | Limited direct customs role; some passenger fees. | Acts as IRG/PLC’s main air link to world (along with Seiyun).
AIRPORT_SANAA | Airport | Previously main international airport; now mostly used for humanitarian and limited passenger flights under truce arrangements. | Throughput drastically reduced; UN airbridge and some negotiated flights. | Customs/revenue minimal compared to pre‑war. | A key bargaining card in negotiations; critical for humanitarian logistics to the north.


⸻

This completes Stage 2 – Category A in both formats:
	•	Wide master table (ready for a single Actors sheet).
	•	Type‑specific extension tables (per central bank, fiscal ministry, revenue agencies, stats/dev, ports/airports).

	•	Category B – Banks: I do the same deep treatment for all commercial banks (base + prudential extension), or
	•	Category C – MFIs & exchangers, depending on your priority.
